Quantities with Addition: The Case of 'Longer'
Explains quantities taken with addition, focusing on 'longer'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how quantities can be considered with additional relations, using the example of 'longer' (atwal) to illustrate multiple layers of addition.

Core Idea
Some dimensional terms involve relational additions beyond pure quantity.

The commentary on page 68 delves into how some quantities are taken not purely but with addition (idafa) to other things. The example of 'longer' (atwal) is analyzed: it involves not just length but a comparison, and possibly multiple comparisons (e.g., longer than something which is itself long relative to something else). This shows the sophistication of kalam in analyzing relational concepts.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish pure quantities from those with addition
The text says some quantities are pure (like line), others are taken with addition (like length assigned to a specific direction).
Addition here means a relation to something else.
2
Analyze the example of 'longer'
Find where 'longer' (atwal) is discussed. Note that it involves at least two additions: longer-ness and length relative to short.
3
Understand the possible third or fourth addition
The commentary considers if 'longer' can have a third addition when compared to another 'longer' object.
This illustrates recursive relationality.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الثالث : الأبعاد الثلاثة للجسم
C
الذي هو الجسم التعليمي ( ومنها ما هي كميات ) مأخوذة ( مع إضافة ) إلى أمر آخر ( كالمفروض ثانياً ) أو أولاً أو ثالثاً فإن كون الامتداد مفروضاً ثانياً إضافة له إلى المفروض أولاً وبالعكس وكونه مفروضاً ثالثاً إضافة إلى مجموع الأولين؛ كما أنَّ لمجموعهما أيضاً إضافة إليه ) وقد يعتبر معه ) أي مع الكم (إضافة ثالثة كالأطول ) فإنه أطول بالقياس إلى ما هو طويل مقيساً إلى قصير فهاهنا إضافتان الأطولية والطول المضايف للقصر لكنه عبر عن الأطولية بالإضافة الثالثة لأنها عارضة الأمر ثالث بعد أمرين تحقق بينهما الطول، أو لأنه نظر إلى أن القصر أيضاً إضافة مقابلة للطول وفيه بعد لأن القصر ليس مأخوذاً مع الأطول، ولو عبر عنها بالإضافة الثانية، لكان أظهر ( أو ) إضافة ( رابعة كالأطول بالنسبة إلى الغير الأطول بالقياس إلى آخر فيكون هناك ثلاث إضافات أطوليتان وطول إضافي . والأطولية الأولى عارضة لأمر رابع فجعلها إضافة رابعة على قياس ما مر، وجعلها ثالثة أولى وفي المباحث المشرقية أن هذه الكميات إذا أخذت مضافة إلى شيء فقد تؤخذ تارة بحيث لا يكون من شرط إضافتها إلى ذلك الشيء إضافتها إلى شيء آخر. وقد تؤخذ تارة أخرى بحيث يكون من شرط إضافتها إلى شيء إضافتها إلى شيء ثالث . مثال الأول أن يقال : هذا الخط طويل عندما يقال :
عليه قوله أي في نفسه بعد وامتداد واحد ، فالظاهر أن يقول : إن كل خط في نفسه طول كما وقع في التجريد الخط طول بلا عرض ولعله أراد بالطويل الطول لأنه متصف بالطول بمعنی دارز شدن وإن كان عينه بمعنى درازي وهذا المتمحل وإن صح إطلاق الطويل عليه لكن لا يصحح قوله وبهذا المعنى الخ لأنه بهذا المعنى قيل : كل خط طول ولا يجري في قوله : كل سطح عريض وكل جسم عميق .
قوله : ( وفي المباحث المشرقية الخ) تأييد لما ذكره سابقاً من أن في الأطول بالقياس إلى الطويل إضافتين وبالقياس إلى غير الأطول ثلاث إضافات .
(
قوله : ( كما أن لمجموعها أيضاً إضافة إليه تحقق الأول والثاني، وإن كان لا يستلزم تحقق الثالث ولا تعقله إلا أن الكلام في الأبعاد الجسمية فلذا كان للأولين إضافة إلى الثالث فإن تعين الطويل والعرض باعتبار العمق .
قوله : ( ولو عبر عنها بالإضافة الثانية لكان أظهر فإن قلت الأطول الذي ذكره المصنف من الطول بمعني أطول الامتدادين، ففيه زيادتان وطول هو إضافة أيضاً، فيصح القول بان الأطول اعتبر فيه إضافة ثالثة بلا تكلف قلت الطول المذكور بمعنى الامتداد مطلقاً وليس إضافة، والأقرب في توجيه كلام المصنف أن يقال : الأطول من زاد طوله على طول غيره بالنسبة إلى قصير ففيه الطولان الإضافيان والزيادة الإضافية فلا غبار في الكلام.
Second observation - Third purpose: The three dimensions of the body. It is said for thickness: it is the filling between surfaces, meaning the mathematical body which is enclosed by one surface or two surfaces or surfaces without additional condition, and in this sense it is said: if a body, then it is in itself deep. And it is said for descending thickness, i.e., thickness qualified by descent, and it is then called ascending thickness, i.e., qualified by ascent, thickness, and in this regard it is said: depth of the well and thickness of the minaret. And it is said: length and width and depth have other meanings besides what was mentioned, such as it is said: length is the extension extending from the center of the world to its circumference, which is the fourth of the meanings of length. And it is said: length also is the extension extending from a human's head to his feet and from the head of quadrupeds to their rear, and this is its meaning. And it is said: width is the extension from a human's right or quadrupeds' right to their left, which is the fourth of the meanings of width. And it is said: depth is the extension from a human's chest to his back and from the back of quadrupeds to the ground, and this is the fourth of the meanings of depth. And know that these mentioned meanings of length, width, and depth are some pure quantities, like length in the sense of the single extension which is the line, width in the sense of the surface, and depth in the sense of thickness. His saying: 'It is the filling between surfaces' thus in a place in the Shifa and in another place it is said depth for the thickness that the surfaces enclose, the intended meaning of 'filling' is that with which it is filled, and the addition of 'filling' to 'what' is lamiyya as is apparent, i.e., filling of an imagined void between surfaces. And it is said that the word 'what' is indefinite and relative, and the addition of 'filling' to it is explicative, thus directing 'filling'. His saying: 'which is enclosed by one surface' like the solid sphere, he indicated by this that mentioning surface as an example, since the intention is not to give a comprehensive and exclusive definition but only a general application to the meanings. His saying: 'and it is said length etc.' he did not combine them with the previous meanings, indicating the abundance of the first applications. His saying: 'from the center of the world to its circumference' like the length of a human, which is the dimension in which the first movement of growth occurs, thus in the Shifa. His saying: 'to the ground' i.e., to its lower part which is on the side of the ground. His saying: 'the single extension which is the line' he states here that the first meaning is the line itself, and it indicates. His saying: 'filling between surfaces meaning the mathematical body etc.' The expression of the text, since it apparently does not include the mathematical body that is in the solid sphere, for it has one surface not multiple surfaces, and includes the natural body, the commentator interpreted it explicitly as intended, although his taking it from the author's phrase is strained. And if he had replaced 'meaning' with 'i.e.', it would be clearer. Then what is in his saying 'filling between surfaces' is either an intensified indefinite or a relative, and the addition of 'filling' to it is explicative. In both cases the intended meaning of 'filling' is not the verbal noun nor the product as thought, but rather the filled thing either terminologically or as a linguistic metaphor. [PAGE_068] Second observation - Third purpose: The three dimensions of the body. C which is the mathematical body. And among them are quantities taken with addition to another matter, like the second assumed or first or third: for the extension being assumed second is an addition to the first assumed and vice versa, and being assumed third is an addition to the sum of the first two; just as the sum also has an addition to it. And sometimes a third addition is considered with it (i.e., with the quantity), like 'longer' (al-atwal): for it is longer in relation to what is long measured against a short one, so here there are two additions: the longer-ness and the length that is relative to shortness. But he expressed the longer-ness as the third addition because it is accidental to a third matter after two matters between which length is realized, or because he considered that shortness is also an addition opposite to length. But there is distance because shortness is not taken with the longer. And if he had expressed it as the second addition, it would be clearer. Or a fourth addition, like 'longer' in relation to another 'longer' in relation to yet another, so there are three additions: two longer-nesses and one relative length. The first longer-ness is accidental to a fourth matter, so he made it a fourth addition by analogy to what preceded, but making it third is preferable. And in the Eastern Investigations, these quantities when taken as added to something, they are sometimes taken such that it is not a condition of their addition to that thing that they be added to something else. And sometimes they are taken such that it is a condition of their addition to something that they be added to a third thing. Example of the first: it is said this line is long when it is said: upon it his saying: i.e., in itself dimension and single extension. So the apparent is that he should say: every line is in itself length, as occurred in the Tajrid: the line is length without width. Perhaps he intended by 'long' (tawil) length (tul), because it is qualified by length in the sense of 'having length' even if it is the same as 'length' in the sense of 'extension'. And this strained interpretation, even if it validates applying 'long' to it, does not validate his saying 'and in this sense etc.' because in this sense it is said: every line is length, and it does not apply in his saying: every surface is wide and every body is deep. His saying: 'and in the Eastern Investigations etc.' supports what he mentioned previously: that in 'longer' in relation to 'long' there are two additions, and in relation to other than 'longer' there are three additions. His saying: 'just as for their sum also there is an addition to it' realizes the first and second, although it does not necessitate realizing the third nor conceiving it, except that the discourse is about bodily dimensions, so for the first two there is an addition to the third, for length and width are determined by depth. His saying: 'and if he had expressed it as the second addition it would be clearer' then if you say: the 'longer' that the author mentioned is from length in the sense of the longer of two extensions, so it has two increases and length is also an addition, so it is correct to say that in 'longer' a third addition is considered without strain. I say: the mentioned length is in the sense of extension absolutely and is not an addition. The closest in directing the author's words is to say: 'longer' is he whose length increased over another's length in relation to a short one, so in it there are two relative lengths and a relative increase, so there is no flaw in the speech.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the three dimensions (length, width, depth) in multiple senses, followed by commentary. The text first defines thickness (thikhn) as the filling between surfaces, i.e., the mathematical body enclosed by one or more surfaces. It notes that in this sense, any body is deep in itself. It then distinguishes between descending thickness (depth of a well) and ascending thickness (thickness of a minaret). Next, it lists four meanings for each dimension: length as (1) the single extension (line), (2) from center of world to circumferences, (3) from head to foot of humans or animals, (4) the extension from right to left? Actually the fourth meaning of length is from center of world to circumference. The text then clarifies that some of these meanings are pure quantities (e.g., length as line, width as surface, depth as thickness) while others involve addition (e.g., specific orientations). The commentary on 'filling between surfaces' explains that 'filling' (hashw) refers to what fills, and the phrase is interpreted as either indefinite or relative, with an explicative addition. It notes that the example of a single surface (solid sphere) is illustrative, not a strict definition. The commentary also discusses the mathematical vs. natural body and the strained interpretation of the author's phrase. On page 68, the commentary continues with how these quantities are taken with addition, e.g., the 'longer' (atwal) involves two or three additions depending on relativity. It cites the Eastern Investigations on conditionality of addition. The discussion ends with clarification that 'longer' means one whose length exceeds another relative to a short one, involving two relative lengths and a relative increase.

Key Terms
كمية (quantity)
إضافة (idafa - addition/relation)
أطول (atwal - longer)
طول (tul - length)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'longer' (atwal) simply means a greater length and does not involve multiple relations.
Correction: The passage shows 'longer' inherently involves at least two additions: the comparative relation (longer-ness) and the length relative to a standard (shortness).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many additions does the term 'longer' (atwal) minimally involve?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Quantities like 'longer' are not simple; they involve multiple relational layers that kalam systematically analyzes.

Quantities with Addition: The Analysis of 'al-Atwal'
Al-Jurjani analyzes the longer as an example of quantities with addition, discussing second, third, and fourth additions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how quantities taken with addition can involve multiple relational layers, as exemplified by the concept of 'the longer' (al-atwal), and why al-Jurjani discusses second, third, and fourth additions.

Core Idea
The longer involves multiple relational additions, not just one.

Al-Jurjani explores the nature of quantities that are defined in relation to other things. Among these, the concept of 'the longer' (al-atwal) presents a puzzle: how many relational additions does it involve? In this passage, he analyzes whether it involves two, three, or four additions, and argues for the most coherent interpretation. This lesson walks through his analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Begin by reading the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the discussion. Note the main claim about quantities taken with addition.
Pay attention to the examples of first, second, third assumptions.
2
Identify the two additions in 'the longer'
Al-Jurjani explains that 'the longer' involves two additions: longer-ness and relational length. Find this in the text.
The longer is longer relative to something that is itself long.
3
Understand the debate over third addition
Some consider the longer as involving a third addition. Why? Al-Jurjani argues it should be considered a second addition. Follow his reasoning.
The third is based on seeing shortness as an opposite addition.
4
Extend to fourth addition
The passage then considers a scenario leading to a fourth addition. See how the pattern extends.
This shows the complexity of relational quantities.
5
Relate to the Eastern Investigations
The passage concludes with a general distinction from the Eastern Investigations about when addition requires a third condition.
This ties the specific example to a broader principle.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الثالث : الأبعاد الثلاثة للجسم
الذي هو الجسم التعليمي ( ومنها ما هي كميات ) مأخوذة ( مع إضافة ) إلى أمر آخر ( كالمفروض ثانياً ) أو أولاً أو ثالثاً فإن كون الامتداد مفروضاً ثانياً إضافة له إلى المفروض أولاً وبالعكس وكونه مفروضاً ثالثاً إضافة إلى مجموع الأولين؛ كما أنَّ لمجموعهما أيضاً إضافة إليه ) وقد يعتبر معه ) أي مع الكم (إضافة ثالثة كالأطول ) فإنه أطول بالقياس إلى ما هو طويل مقيساً إلى قصير فهاهنا إضافتان الأطولية والطول المضايف للقصر لكنه عبر عن الأطولية بالإضافة الثالثة لأنها عارضة الأمر ثالث بعد أمرين تحقق بينهما الطول، أو لأنه نظر إلى أن القصر أيضاً إضافة مقابلة للطول وفيه بعد لأن القصر ليس مأخوذاً مع الأطول، ولو عبر عنها بالإضافة الثانية، لكان أظهر ( أو ) إضافة ( رابعة كالأطول بالنسبة إلى الغير الأطول بالقياس إلى آخر فيكون هناك ثلاث إضافات أطوليتان وطول إضافي . والأطولية الأولى عارضة لأمر رابع فجعلها إضافة رابعة على قياس ما مر، وجعلها ثالثة أولى وفي المباحث المشرقية أن هذه الكميات إذا أخذت مضافة إلى شيء فقد تؤخذ تارة بحيث لا يكون من شرط إضافتها إلى ذلك الشيء إضافتها إلى شيء آخر. وقد تؤخذ تارة أخرى بحيث يكون من شرط إضافتها إلى شيء إضافتها إلى شيء ثالث . مثال الأول أن يقال : هذا الخط طويل عندما يقال : عليه قوله أي في نفسه بعد وامتداد ،واحد ، فالظاهر أن يقول : إن كل خط في نفسه طول كما وقع في التجريد الخط طول بلا عرض ولعله أراد بالطويل الطول لأنه متصف بالطول بمعنی دارز شدن وإن كان عينه بمعنى درازي وهذا المتمحل وإن صح إطلاق الطويل عليه لكن لا يصحح قوله وبهذا المعنى الخ لأنه بهذا المعنى قيل : كل خط طول ولا يجري في قوله : كل سطح عريض وكل جسم عميق .
قوله : ( وفي المباحث المشرقية الخ) تأييد لما ذكره سابقاً من أن في الأطول بالقياس إلى الطويل إضافتين وبالقياس إلى غير الأطول ثلاث إضافات .
( قوله : ( كما أن لمجموعها أيضاً إضافة إليه تحقق الأول والثاني، وإن كان لا يستلزم تحقق الثالث ولا تعقله إلا أن الكلام في الأبعاد الجسمية فلذا كان للأولين إضافة إلى الثالث فإن تعين الطويل والعرض باعتبار العمق .
قوله : ( ولو عبر عنها بالإضافة الثانية لكان أظهر فإن قلت الأطول الذي ذكره المصنف من الطول بمعني أطول الامتدادين، ففيه زيادتان وطول هو إضافة أيضاً، فيصح القول بان الأطول اعتبر فيه إضافة ثالثة بلا تكلف قلت الطول المذكور بمعنى الامتداد مطلقاً وليس إضافة، والأقرب في توجيه كلام المصنف أن يقال : الأطول من زاد طوله على طول غيره بالنسبة إلى قصير ففيه الطولان الإضافيان والزيادة الإضافية فلا غبار في الكلام.
The second observatory - the third purpose: the three dimensions of the body, which is the mathematical body. (And among them are quantities) taken (with addition) to another thing, (like the assumed second) or first or third. For the extension being assumed as second is an addition to what is assumed as first, and vice versa, and it being assumed as third is an addition to the sum of the first two; just as their sum also has an addition to it. And (sometimes is considered with it) i.e., with quantity (a third addition, like the longer [al-atwal]) for it is longer in relation to what is long (which itself is long) measured against a short thing. So here there are two additions: the longer-ness (al-atwaliyya) and the length (al-tul) which is relative to shortness. But he expressed the longer-ness as a third addition because it is an accident of a third thing after two things between which length is realized, or because he considered that shortness is also an addition opposite to length, but this is far-fetched because shortness is not taken with the longer. And if he had expressed it as a second addition, it would be more apparent. (Or) a (fourth) addition, like the longer in relation to another thing which is longer in relation to another, so there are three additions: two longer-nesses and a relational length. The first longer-ness is an accident of a fourth thing, so he made it a fourth addition by analogy to what passed, but making it a third is better. And in the Eastern Investigations (al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya) these quantities when taken as added to something are sometimes taken without it being a condition of their addition to that thing that they be added to something else, and sometimes taken such that it is a condition of their addition to a thing that they be added to a third thing. An example of the first: it is said: this line is long when it is said: upon it his saying: i.e., in itself, after and extension, one. So it is apparent that he should say: every line in itself is length, as occurred in al-Tajrid: a line is length without width. And perhaps he meant by 'long' (al-tawil) length (al-tul) because it is qualified by length in the sense of [؟] becoming long, even if it is the same in the sense of [؟] length. And this far-fetched interpretation, even if applying 'long' to it is correct, does not validate his statement 'and in this sense ...' because in this sense it was said: every line is length, and it does not apply in his statement: every surface is wide and every body is deep. His saying: (And in the Eastern Investigations etc.) is a support for what he mentioned earlier: that in 'the longer' in relation to 'the long' there are two additions, and in relation to what is other than 'the longer' there are three additions. His saying: (Just as their sum also has an addition to it) realizes the first and second, even if it does not necessitate the realization of the third nor its being understood; but the discussion is about bodily dimensions, so the first two have an addition to the third, for the determination of the long and the wide is by virtue of the deep. His saying: (And if he had expressed it as a second addition, it would be more apparent) for if you say: the longer which the author mentioned is from length in the sense of the longer of the two extensions, so in it there are two increases and length is also an addition, so it is correct to say that in the longer a third addition is considered without difficulty. I say: the mentioned length is in the sense of absolute extension and is not an addition. The closest in interpreting the author's words is that it be said: the longer is that whose length exceeds the length of another in relation to a short thing, so in it there are two relational lengths and the relational increase, so there is no flaw in the statement.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of quantities taken with addition, specifically in the context of the three dimensions of a mathematical body (al-jism al-ta'limi). The author first states that among quantities are those taken with addition to another thing, such as 'assumed second' or 'first' or 'third'. He then explains how being 'assumed second' is an addition relative to the 'assumed first', and 'assumed third' is an addition to the sum of the first two. He then introduces the concept of 'the longer' (al-atwal) as an example of a third addition: something is longer relative to something that is itself long when measured against a short thing, so there are two additions (longer-ness and relational length). The author discusses whether this should be called a third or second addition, preferring the latter. He then extends the analysis to a fourth addition, as in 'the longer relative to something else which is longer relative to another', resulting in three additions. He cites the Eastern Investigations (al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya) to support the distinction between quantities that require no further addition and those that require addition to a third thing. The commentary (qawlahu) then clarifies and defends the author's position, addressing an objection about the nature of 'length' in 'the longer', concluding that 'the longer' involves two relational lengths and an additional relation, so the statement is sound.

Correction: Actually, it involves two additions: the longer-ness itself and the relational length that is relative to shortness.

Quantity Per Se and Accidental Quantity with Four Types
Al-Jurjani classifies quantity into per se and accidental, detailing four types of accidental quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between per se quantity and accidental quantity, and the four specific types of accidental quantity as classified by Al-Jurjani.

Core Idea
Accidental quantity has four distinct modes: place, inherence, inherence in place, and relation.

This passage from Al-Jurjani's work on metaphysics refines our understanding of quantity. He distinguishes between quantity in its own right and quantity that is accidental, and then provides a precise classification of accidental quantity into four types. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for grasping how properties like size, number, light, blackness, and even motion relate to quantity.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the basic division between per se and accidental quantity.
The passage begins with a clear bifurcation.
2
Identify the four types
Locate the four types of accidental quantity: place, inhering in quantity, inhering in place of quantity, and related to quantity. Notice how each is defined.
These are enumerated with examples.
3
Understand the example of motion
See how motion combines two aspects (superposition on distance and time) and how it takes on quantitative properties through these relations.
Motion is a composite example showing the utility of the classification.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters for metaphysics: it explains how non-intrinsic quantities can still be described as quantitative.
The passage ends with a summary principle.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الرابع: الكم إما بالذات وإما بالعرض
( الكم إما بالذات وهو ما ذكرناه ) وبينا خواصه وأقسامه ( وإما بالعرض وهو أقسام ) أربعة ( الأول محل الكم كالجسم ) إما بحسب المقدار الحال فيه وهو ظاهر، وإما بحسب العدد إذا كان الجسم متعدداً ( الثاني الحال في الكم كالضوء القائم بالسطح الثالث الحال في محل الكم كالسواد فإنه مع الكم المتصل الذي هو المقدار ( محلهما الجسم وإن اعتبرت تعدد الجسم كان السواد مع الكم المنفصل في محل واحد ( الرابع متعلق الكم كما يقال : هذه القوة متناهية أو غير متناهية باعتبار أثرها ) إما في الشدة أو المدة أو العدة. وقد سبق تحقيق هذه المعاني مستوفاة (فما وصفناه بخواص الكم مما ليس كماً بالذات فلأحد هذه الوجوه الأربعة ( واعلم أنه قد يجتمع في بعض الأمور وجهان من هذه الأربعة كما في الحركة فإنها منطبقة على المسافة ) والانطباق يجري مجرى الحلول فكأن الحركة محل للمسافة التي هي كم بالذات أو بالعكس فيعرضها التفاوت بالقلة والكثرة ) والقصر والطول وتعرضها المساواة والزيادة والنقصان فيقال مثلاً: هذه الحركة مساوية لتلك الحركة كل ذلك بتبعية المسافة ( ومنطبقة على الزمان ) أيضاً فكأنها محل له أو بالعكس ( فيعرضها التفاوت بالسرعة والبطء ) بسبب قلة الزمان كثرته ويعرض لها أيضاً المساواة والمفاوتة بسببه
قوله : (كالسواد النافذ في الجسم.
قوله : ( فلا حد هذه الوجوه الأربعة فهو وصف بحال المتعلق.
قوله : ( والانطباق يجري مجرى (الحلول أي السرياني في اشتراكهما في استلزام الانقسام من الجانبين.
قوله : (فإن مع الكم (محلهما المشهور أنَّ اللون عارض للسطح وليس بنافذ في العمق وقيل : نافذ فيه، وهذا الكلام مبني عليه .
قوله : (كما يقال : هذه القوة متناهية أو غير متناهية فإن القوة تتعلق بالحركات التي تكون محلاً للكم متصلاً أو منفصلاً فتتعلق بالكم في الجملة .
The second observatory - the fourth purpose: quantity is either per se (al-kamm bi al-dhat) or accidental (al-kamm bi al-'arad). (Quantity is either per se, which we have mentioned) and we explained its properties and divisions, (and it is either accidental, and it is divisions) four: (first, the place of quantity [mahall al-kamm], like body) either with respect to the magnitude inhering in it, which is apparent, or with respect to number if the body is multiple; (second, the inhering in quantity [al-hall fi al-kamm], like light subsisting in a surface; third, the inhering in the place of quantity [al-hall fi mahall al-kamm], like blackness, for it is with the continuous quantity which is magnitude (their place is the body, and if the multiplicity of the body is considered, blackness is with the discrete quantity in one place); (fourth, the related to quantity [muta'alliq al-kamm], as it is said: this power is finite or infinite with respect to its effect) either in intensity, duration, or number. And the verification of these meanings has already been completed. (Thus what we described with the properties of quantity, while it is not quantity per se, is due to one of these four aspects.) And know that in some things two of these four aspects may combine, as in motion, for it is superimposed on distance (al-masafa) and superposition operates like inherence, so motion is as if it were the place of distance, which is quantity per se, or vice versa, so it is subject to variation in paucity and multiplicity, shortness and length, and it is subject to equality, increase and decrease; for example, it is said: this motion is equal to that motion, all of that following the distance. And (it is superimposed on time) also, so it is as if it is the place of time or vice versa, so it is subject to variation in speed and slowness due to the paucity or multiplicity of time, and it is also subject to equality and disparity by virtue of it. His saying: (like blackness penetrating the body). His saying: (so there is no [؟] these four aspects; it is a description by the state of the related). His saying: (and superposition operates like inherence, i.e., penetration, in that they both entail division from both sides). His saying: (for with quantity their place is the well-known that color is an accident of the surface and does not penetrate into depth, and it is said: it penetrates it, and this speech is based on it). His saying: (as it is said: this power is finite or infinite, for power is related to motions which are a place for continuous or discrete quantity, so it is related to quantity in general).
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a classification of quantity (al-kamm) into two types: per se quantity (al-kamm bi al-dhat) and accidental quantity (al-kamm bi al-'arad). Per se quantity is what has been previously discussed with its properties and divisions. Accidental quantity is then divided into four types: first, the place of quantity (mahall al-kamm), such as body, which is the substrate for magnitude (continuous quantity) or number (discrete quantity); second, the inhering in quantity (al-hall fi al-kamm), like light that subsists in a surface (which is a continuous quantity); third, the inhering in the place of quantity (al-hall fi mahall al-kamm), like blackness, which coexists with the quantity inhering in the same body; fourth, the related to quantity (muta'alliq al-kamm), like a power being described as finite or infinite due to its effect in intensity, duration, or number. The author then notes that anything described with the properties of quantity that is not per se quantity must be accidental via one of these four aspects. He gives an example of motion, which combines two aspects: motion is superimposed on distance (a continuous quantity) and on time, so it can be described as equal or unequal in length, fast or slow, etc., by following the quantity of distance or time. The commentary (qawlahu) provides additional clarifications: blackness may or may not penetrate the body; superposition is like inherence in requiring division from both sides; power relates to motions that are places for quantity.

Correction: Al-Jurjani shows there are four distinct types depending on whether the thing is the place of quantity, inhering in quantity, inhering in the place of quantity, or related to quantity.

Intrinsic Quantity and Accidental Quantity
Al-Iji defines intrinsic and accidental quantity with examples of relative terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between intrinsic and accidental quantity and how relative terms like 'longer' illustrate accidental quantity.

Core Idea
Quantity is either intrinsic (essential) or accidental (by relation).

Al-Iji begins by distinguishing two kinds of quantity: that which is intrinsic to a thing (like its own magnitude) and that which is attributed to it by comparison or relation. The examples of 'longer' and 'wider' show how accidental quantity involves relativity. This lesson establishes the foundation for the detailed classification that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow from examples to the classification.
The passage starts with relative terms like 'longer' and 'wider' before defining the categories.
2
Understand the examples
Al-Iji gives examples of relative comparisons: 'longer' is long relative to another long thing, etc. These illustrate how accidental quantity is not intrinsic but relational.
The same logic applies to discrete quantity like numbers.
3
Grasp the core distinction
Intrinsic quantity is what a thing has in itself (e.g., its own length). Accidental quantity is attributed because of a relation to another thing. This prepares for the four types.
Memorize: intrinsic = essential, accidental = relational.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الرابع: الكم إما بالذات وإما بالعرض للخط الآخر أنه ليس بطويل أو يقال : هذا السطح عريض عندما يقال لسطح آخر أنه ليس بعريض أو هذا الجسم كبير ثخين عندما يقال لجسم آخر أنه ليس كذلك ونظيره في الكم المنفصل أن يقال : هذا العدد كثير بالقياس إلى آخر هو قليل مقيساً إليه، ومثال الثاني الأطول والأعرض والأعمق والأكبر فإن الأطول أطول بالقياس إلى طويل وذلك الشيء طويل بالقياس إلى قصير وكذا القول في سائر الأقسام. [ المقصد الرابع : الكم إما بالذات وإما بالعرض ] ( الكم إما بالذات وهو ما ذكرناه ) وبينا خواصه وأقسامه ( وإما بالعرض وهو أقسام ) أربعة ( الأول محل الكم كالجسم ) إما بحسب المقدار الحال فيه وهو ظاهر، وإما بحسب العدد إذا كان الجسم متعدداً ( الثاني الحال في الكم كالضوء القائم بالسطح الثالث الحال في محل الكم كالسواد فإنه مع الكم المتصل الذي هو المقدار ( محلهما الجسم وإن اعتبرت تعدد الجسم كان السواد مع الكم المنفصل في محل واحد ( الرابع متعلق الكم كما يقال : هذه القوة متناهية أو غير متناهية باعتبار أثرها ) إما في الشدة أو المدة أو العدة. وقد سبق تحقيق هذه المعاني مستوفاة (فما وصفناه بخواص الكم مما ليس كماً بالذات فلأحد هذه الوجوه الأربعة ( واعلم أنه قد يجتمع في بعض الأمور وجهان من هذه الأربعة كما في الحركة فإنها منطبقة على المسافة ) والانطباق يجري مجرى الحلول فكأن الحركة محل للمسافة التي هي كم بالذات أو بالعكس فيعرضها التفاوت بالقلة والكثرة ) والقصر والطول وتعرضها المساواة والزيادة والنقصان فيقال مثلاً: هذه الحركة مساوية لتلك الحركة كل ذلك بتبعية المسافة ( ومنطبقة على الزمان ) أيضاً فكأنها محل له أو بالعكس ( فيعرضها التفاوت بالسرعة والبطء ) بسبب قلة الزمان كثرته ويعرض لها أيضاً المساواة والمفاوتة بسببه قوله : (كالسواد النافذ في الجسم. قوله : ( فلا حد هذه الوجوه الأربعة فهو وصف بحال المتعلق. قوله : ( والانطباق يجري مجرى (الحلول أي السرياني في اشتراكهما في استلزام الانقسام من الجانبين. قوله : (فإن مع الكم (محلهما المشهور أنَّ اللون عارض للسطح وليس بنافذ في العمق وقيل : نافذ فيه، وهذا الكلام مبني عليه . قوله : (كما يقال : هذه القوة متناهية أو غير متناهية فإن القوة تتعلق بالحركات التي تكون محلاً للكم متصلاً أو منفصلاً فتتعلق بالكم في الجملة .
The second observatory - the fourth objective: Quantity is either intrinsic or accidental. For the other line, it is not long, or it is said: This surface is wide when it is said of another surface that it is not wide, or this body is large and thick when it is said of another body that it is not so, and its analogue in discrete quantity is that it is said: This number is many in relation to another that is few measured against it, and the example of the second is the longer, the wider, the deeper, and the greater; for the longer is longer in relation to a long thing, and that thing is long in relation to a short thing, and so on for the rest. [The fourth objective: Quantity is either intrinsic or accidental.] (Quantity is either intrinsic, which we have mentioned) and we have explained its properties and divisions, (or accidental, and it has four divisions: (the first is the locus of quantity like body) either according to the magnitude inhering in it, which is apparent, or according to number if the body is multiple; (the second is the state in quantity like light subsisting on a surface; (the third is the state in the locus of quantity like blackness, for it is with continuous quantity which is magnitude (their locus is the body, and if the multiplicity of the body is considered, blackness is with discrete quantity in one locus); (the fourth is the related object of quantity as it is said: This power is finite or infinite with respect to its effect) either in intensity, duration, or number. And the verification of these meanings has already been thoroughly treated. (Thus what we have described with the properties of quantity that is not intrinsic quantity is due to one of these four aspects.) (And know that two of these four aspects may combine in some things, as in motion, for it is superimposed on distance) and superimposition takes the place of inherence, so motion is as if a locus for distance which is intrinsic quantity, or vice versa, and thus it admits variation in fewness and multiplicity, and shortness and length, and it admits equality, increase, and decrease, so it is said, for example: This motion is equal to that motion, all that by following distance; (and it is superimposed on time) as well, so it is as if a locus for it or vice versa, (so it admits variation in speed and slowness) due to the fewness or multiplicity of time, and it also admits equality and difference because of it. He said: (like blackness penetrating the body). He said: (So there is no limit to these four aspects, so it is a description by the state of the related object. He said: (And superimposition takes the place of inherence, i.e., pervasion, in that they both entail division from both sides. He said: (For with quantity (their locus is the body) the well-known is that color is accidental to the surface and does not penetrate into depth, and it is said: It penetrates into it, and this statement is based on that. He said: (As it is said: This power is finite or infinite, for power is related to movements that are a locus for continuous or discrete quantity, so it is related to quantity in general.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of quantity (al-kamm) into two types: intrinsic (bil-dhat) and accidental (bil-'arad). The author first gives examples of relative terms (longer, wider) to illustrate comparison. Then he defines intrinsic quantity as that which has been previously described (i.e., continuous and discrete quantity). Accidental quantity is divided into four categories: (1) the locus of quantity, like a body having magnitude; (2) the state inhering in quantity, like light on a surface; (3) the state inhering in the locus of quantity, like blackness in a body that has magnitude; and (4) the thing related to quantity, like a power being called finite or infinite by virtue of its effect. The passage then notes that anything described with properties of quantity without being intrinsic quantity falls under one of these four aspects. The commentary (قوله) explains specific points: blackness penetrating the body, the meaning of 'limit' in the four aspects, superimposition (انطباق) being like inherence, and the well-known view that color is accidental to the surface. This lesson establishes the foundational taxonomy for accidental quantity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That accidental quantity is a separate kind of quantity like length or number.
Correction: Accidental quantity is not a separate kind; it is a description of things that are not quantities but are spoken of in quantitative terms due to relation.

Four Types of Accidental Quantity
Al-Iji lists the four types of accidental quantity with examples, plus a discussion of motion and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of accidental quantity: locus, state in quantity, state in locus, and related object, along with their examples and the special case of motion.

Core Idea
Things acquire quantitative descriptions through four accidental relations: being a locus, a state, a state of a locus, or a related object.

After distinguishing intrinsic and accidental quantity, Al-Iji enumerates four ways something can be described as a quantity accidentally. Each type is illustrated with clear examples: body as locus, light on a surface, blackness in a body, and power as related to effect. The passage also discusses how motion combines two aspects, and the commentary clarifies technical details. This lesson provides the complete taxonomy of accidental quantity.

Guided Path
1
List the four types
Read the passage and identify the four divisions: (1) locus of quantity, (2) state in quantity, (3) state in the locus of quantity, (4) related object of quantity.
Each type is introduced with the word 'الأول', 'الثاني', etc.
2
Examine each example
For each type, note the example: body as locus, light as state in quantity, blackness as state in locus, and power as related object. Understand why each is accidental.
The commentary adds nuance, e.g., blackness penetrating the body.
3
Analyze the motion example
Al-Iji explains that motion combines two aspects (locus and state) due to superimposition on distance and time. This illustrates how the four types can overlap.
Motion inherits quantitative properties from the distance covered.
4
Read the commentary
The commentary (قوله) explains key terms: 'superimposition takes the place of inherence', 'color is accidental to the surface', and 'power is related to movements'. This deepens understanding.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الرابع: الكم إما بالذات وإما بالعرض للخط الآخر أنه ليس بطويل أو يقال : هذا السطح عريض عندما يقال لسطح آخر أنه ليس بعريض أو هذا الجسم كبير ثخين عندما يقال لجسم آخر أنه ليس كذلك ونظيره في الكم المنفصل أن يقال : هذا العدد كثير بالقياس إلى آخر هو قليل مقيساً إليه، ومثال الثاني الأطول والأعرض والأعمق والأكبر فإن الأطول أطول بالقياس إلى طويل وذلك الشيء طويل بالقياس إلى قصير وكذا القول في سائر الأقسام. The second observatory - the fourth objective: Quantity is either intrinsic or accidental. For the other line, it is not long, or it is said: This surface is wide when it is said of another surface that it is not wide, or this body is large and thick when it is said of another body that it is not so, and its analogue in discrete quantity is that it is said: This number is many in relation to another that is few measured against it, and the example of the second is the longer, the wider, the deeper, and the greater; for the longer is longer in relation to a long thing, and that thing is long in relation to a short thing, and so on for the rest. [The fourth objective: Quantity is either intrinsic or accidental.] (Quantity is either intrinsic, which we have mentioned) and we have explained its properties and divisions, (or accidental, and it has four divisions: (the first is the locus of quantity like body) either according to the magnitude inhering in it, which is apparent, or according to number if the body is multiple; (the second is the state in quantity like light subsisting on a surface; (the third is the state in the locus of quantity like blackness, for it is with continuous quantity which is magnitude (their locus is the body, and if the multiplicity of the body is considered, blackness is with discrete quantity in one locus); (the fourth is the related object of quantity as it is said: This power is finite or infinite with respect to its effect) either in intensity, duration, or number. And the verification of these meanings has already been thoroughly treated. (Thus what we have described with the properties of quantity that is not intrinsic quantity is due to one of these four aspects.) (And know that two of these four aspects may combine in some things, as in motion, for it is superimposed on distance) and superimposition takes the place of inherence, so motion is as if a locus for distance which is intrinsic quantity, or vice versa, and thus it admits variation in fewness and multiplicity, and shortness and length, and it admits equality, increase, and decrease, so it is said, for example: This motion is equal to that motion, all that by following distance; (and it is superimposed on time) as well, so it is as if a locus for it or vice versa, (so it admits variation in speed and slowness) due to the fewness or multiplicity of time, and it also admits equality and difference because of it. He said: (like blackness penetrating the body). He said: (So there is no limit to these four aspects, so it is a description by the state of the related object. He said: (And superimposition takes the place of inherence, i.e., pervasion, in that they both entail division from both sides. He said: (For with quantity (their locus is the body) the well-known is that color is accidental to the surface and does not penetrate into depth, and it is said: It penetrates into it, and this statement is based on that. He said: (As it is said: This power is finite or infinite, for power is related to movements that are a locus for continuous or discrete quantity, so it is related to quantity in general.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of quantity (al-kamm) into two types: intrinsic (bil-dhat) and accidental (bil-'arad). The author first gives examples of relative terms (longer, wider) to illustrate comparison. Then he defines intrinsic quantity as that which has been previously described (i.e., continuous and discrete quantity). Accidental quantity is divided into four categories: (1) the locus of quantity, like a body having magnitude; (2) the state inhering in quantity, like light on a surface; (3) the state inhering in the locus of quantity, like blackness in a body that has magnitude; and (4) the thing related to quantity, like a power being called finite or infinite by virtue of its effect. The passage then notes that anything described with properties of quantity without being intrinsic quantity falls under one of these four aspects. The commentary (قوله) explains specific points: blackness penetrating the body, the meaning of 'limit' in the four aspects, superimposition (انطباق) being like inherence, and the well-known view that color is accidental to the surface. This lesson establishes the foundational taxonomy for accidental quantity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'accidental quantity' refers to a separate type of quantity like length.
Correction: Accidental quantity is not a quantity; it's a way of describing non-quantitative things using quantitative terms due to relation.

Checkpoint

A) Light on a surface
B) Blackness in a body
C) A body's magnitude

Key Takeaway
The four types of accidental quantity—locus, state in quantity, state in locus, and related object—explain how non-quantitative things can be described in quantitative terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Motion and Time as Instances of Accidental Quantity
Al-Iji explains motion and time as accidental quantity with multiple aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how motion and time are classified as accidental quantity in the tradition of Islamic philosophy and kalam, including the multiple layers of accidental quantity they exhibit.

Core Idea
Accidental quantity manifests in multiple ways, as shown by motion and time.

Having established the general categories of quantity, we now examine how they apply to concrete phenomena like motion and time. Motion exhibits accidental quantity in two distinct ways, while time uniquely combines intrinsic and accidental continuity. This passage from al-Iji's Mawaqif demonstrates that ontological categories can overlap in complex and illuminating ways.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two ways motion is accidental quantity
Read the first part of the passage to find the two aspects: one is the inherence of intrinsic quantity in motion, the other is motion's inherence together with intrinsic quantity in the same subject.
Motion carries magnitude because it occurs in a moving body.
2
Understand discrete quantity accidentally applying to continuous
The passage states that we can divide continuous quantities like time into discrete units, such as hours or cubits. This is an accidental attribution of discrete quantity to continuous quantity.
This shows that categories can cross-apply; there is no harm in that.
3
Analyze time's dual continuity and accidental discreteness
Time is intrinsically continuous because its parts meet at the 'now', and accidentally continuous because it superimposes on motion, which superimposes on distance. It also has accidental discreteness when measured in units.
Thus time combines intrinsic continuity, accidental continuity, and accidental discreteness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا وجه من الوجوه الأربعة وجد في الحركة ( وتقوم ) الحركة ( بالجسم المتحرك ) الذي هو محل المقدار ( فتتجزأ بتجزئه ) فهذا وجه آخر من تلك الوجوه وجد في الحركة أيضاً فهي كم بالعرض من وجهين أحدهما حلول الكم بالذات فيها أو عكسه، والثاني حلولها مع الكم بالذات في محل واحد ) والكم المنفصل قد يعرض للمتصل ) القار وغير القار ( كما إذا قسمنا الأزمان ) بالساعات أو إلا شك بالأذرع) فيتعدد أجزاء الكم المتصل ولا بأس بعروض نوع من مقولة لنوع آخر منها كما الإضافات ( وقد يكون الشيء كماً متصلاً بالذات و) كماً متصلاً (بالعرض كالزمان فإنه كم متصل (بالذات ( لما مر من أن أجزاءه تتلاقى على حد مشترك هو ) الآن ( و منطبق على الحركة المنطبقة على المسافة فيكون منطبقاً بواسطة الحركة على المسافة التي هي كم بالذات فيكون كماً متصلاً بالعرض فقد اجتمع في الزمان الاتصال بالذات والاتصال بالذات والاتصال بالعرض والانفصال بالعرض.
So this is one of the four aspects found in motion; and motion subsists in the moving body which is the locus of magnitude, so it is divided by its division. So this is another of those aspects also found in motion. So it is accidental quantity in two ways: one is the inherence of intrinsic quantity in it or its opposite, and the second is its inherence together with intrinsic quantity in one locus. And discrete quantity may accidentally pertain to continuous quantity, both stationary and non-stationary, as when we divide times by hours or cubits, so the parts of continuous quantity become multiple. And there is no harm in one type of category accidentally pertaining to another type, as in relations. And sometimes a thing may be continuous quantity intrinsically and continuous quantity accidentally, like time. For it is continuous quantity intrinsically because its parts meet at a common boundary, which is the 'now', and it is superimposed on motion which is superimposed on distance, so by means of motion it is superimposed on distance which is intrinsic quantity, thus it becomes continuous quantity accidentally. So in time, intrinsic continuity and accidental continuity and accidental discreteness are combined.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is an application of the earlier classification of accidental quantity to motion and time. It shows that motion exhibits two of the four aspects: first, as a locus for intrinsic quantity (distance), and second, as inhering together with intrinsic quantity (magnitude) in the same subject (the moving body). Thus motion is accidental quantity in two ways. The passage then discusses that discrete quantity (number) can accidentally pertain to continuous quantity, as when we measure time in units. It notes that categories can accidentally apply to other categories, like relations. Finally, time is analyzed: it is intrinsically continuous (its parts share a boundary, the 'now'), but also accidentally continuous because it is superimposed on motion which is superimposed on distance. Therefore, time combines intrinsic continuity, accidental continuity, and accidental discreteness. This lesson demonstrates the complexity of applying the taxonomy of quantity to concrete examples.

Correction: The passage shows that time has accidental discreteness when measured in units (e.g., hours), and also has accidental continuity via its relation to motion and distance.

Key Takeaway
Motion and time are not simply quantities but exhibit accidental quantity in multiple layers, demonstrating the richness of ontological categories.

The Mutakallimun's Denial of Number
Al-Iji presents the Mutakallimun's argument that number is non-existent because unity is non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mutakallimun's argument that number is not a real external entity because it is composed of non-existent units.

Core Idea
If unity doesn't exist, then number (composed of units) cannot exist either.

The Mutakallimun (theologians) famously denied that numbers have extra-mental reality. They argued that number is nothing but a collection of units, and if each unit (unity) is not a real thing, then the collection also cannot be real. In this passage, al-Iji presents their first argument: an infinite regress that shows unity cannot exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the two key premises: (1) number is composed of units, and (2) unity is non-existent. Note that the non-existence of the part implies non-existence of the whole.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Understand the proof by regress
The proof that unity does not exist is given: if unity existed, it would itself have unity (since every existent is one), leading to an infinite regress. This shows unity cannot be existent.
The regress is in mental considerations if unity is not existent.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that number, composed of non-existent units, is also non-existent in the external world. Number exists only as a mental concept.
This distinguishes the Mutakallimun's view from the philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الخامس : المتكلمون أنكروا العدد ] ( إنَّ المتكلمين أنكروا العدد الذي هو الكم المنفصل (خلافاً للحكماء لمسلكين أحدهما أنه أي العدد الذي هو الكثرة ) مركب من الوحدات والوحدة ليست وجودية وعدم الجزء يستلزم عدم الكل ضرورة) فالعدد المركب من الوحدات العدمية يكون عدمياً قطعاً ( بيان أنَّ الوحدة لا توجد ) في الخارج ( أمران : الأول لو وجدت الوحدة فلها (وحدة لأن كل موجود موصوف بأنه واحد ( ولزم حالاً قوله : (لو وجدت أي في الخارج لأن الكلام فيه . قوله: (لو وجدت الوحدة فلها (وحدة موجودة لأن ما من شأن الوجود في الخارج يكون الاتصاف به فرع وجوده إنما قيدنا بذلك القيد ليترتب قوله ولزم التسلسل إلخ . قوله : ( لو وجدت الوحدة فلها (وحدة وأما إذا لم يوجد فلا يلزم التسلسل في الموجودات، بل في الاعتبارات وهل يمتنع التسلسل في الاعتباري النفس الأمري قد سبق الكلام فيه فلا نعيده.
[The fifth objective: The mutakallimun denied number] (Indeed, the mutakallimun denied number, which is discrete quantity, contrary to the philosophers, for two paths: one is that number – i.e., plurality – is composed of units, and unity is non-existent, and the non-existence of the part necessarily entails the non-existence of the whole, so number composed of non-existent units is definitely non-existent. The proof that unity does not exist in the external world is two things: First, if unity existed, it would have unity because every existent is described as one, and an infinite regress would immediately follow. He said: (If it existed means in the external world because the discussion is about that. He said: (If unity existed, it would have an existing unity, because whatever has the property of existence in the external world, its being described [with that property] is subsidiary to its existence; we only restricted it by that restriction so that his statement 'and an infinite regress would follow' would be entailed, etc. He said: (If unity existed, it would have unity. But if it does not exist, then no infinite regress would follow in existents, but rather in considerations, and whether infinite regress is impossible in mental considerations has been previously discussed, so we will not repeat it.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objective of the second observatory: the Mutakallimun (theologians) denied number (al-‘adad), which is discrete quantity, in opposition to the philosophers (al-hukama’). The argument has two parts (مسلكين). The first part states that number is composed of units, and unity (al-wahda) is non-existent (non-entity). Since the part is non-existent, the whole (number) must also be non-existent. Then a proof is given that unity does not exist in the external world: (1) If unity existed, it would itself have unity (since every existent is described as one), leading to an infinite regress. The commentary clarifies that 'if existed' refers to external existence, and the unity attributed to it would be an existing unity, leading to regress. If unity does not exist, there is no regress in existents but only in mental considerations, and the possibility of infinite regress in mental considerations has already been discussed. This lesson is a classic kalam argument against the reality of number as a discrete entity.

Misconception: The Mutakallimun deny the existence of any number at all, including in the mind.
Correction: They deny number as a real external entity, but accept it as a mental concept (i'tibari).

Checkpoint

A) Number is composed of units
B) Unity is existent
C) The whole can exist without its parts

Key Takeaway
The Mutakallimun argue that number is not a real external entity because it is composed of units, and unity cannot exist without infinite regress; hence number exists only as a mental concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Critique of the Divisibility Argument: Only Negates Universal Affirmation
Commentator distinguishes between two types of negation in response to the divisibility argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divisibility argument fails to prove that no one is indivisible, and what kind of negation it actually establishes.

Core Idea
The divisibility argument only disproves that everything is indivisible, not that anything is indivisible.

The commentator carefully distinguishes between two types of logical negation. While the divisibility argument shows that not every entity is indivisible, it does not prove that no entity is indivisible. This distinction is crucial for defending the possibility of indivisible entities such as the indivisible atom and the Necessary Existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Note the key terms for negation types.
The passage is short but dense with logical terminology.
2
Identify the two types of negation
Distinguish between 'negation of universal affirmation' (رفع الإيجاب الكلي) and 'universal negation' (السلب الكلي). The first means 'not every X is Y', the second means 'no X is Y'.
This difference is central to the critique.
3
Understand the argument's failure
The divisibility argument only achieves the first negation, but the intended claim (المدعى) requires the second. The argument does not disprove unity for indivisible entities like the atom or God.
The claim of no difference between divisible and indivisible is dismissed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الثاني أنَّ الواحد إلخ) فيه أنَّ هذا إنما يدل على رفع الإيجاب الكلي لا على السلب الكلي الذي هو المدعى، إذ لا يدل على عدمية وحدة الواحد الذي لا ينقسم كالجوهر الفرد والواجب تعالى وادعاء عدم الفرق مما لا يسمع.
His saying: 'Secondly, that the one etc.' There is in it that this only proves the negation of universal affirmation, not the universal negation which is the claim, because it does not prove the non-existence of the unity of the one that is not divisible, like the indivisible atom and the Necessary Exalted, and the claim of no difference is not to be listened to.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This is a critical remark by the commentator on the second argument (الثاني). He notes that the argument only shows that not every one is indivisible (rafʿ al-ījāb al-kullī), meaning it disproves the claim that every one is indivisible. But the intended claim (al-muddaʿā) is the universal negation (al-salb al-kullī), i.e., that no one is indivisible or that unity is never existent. The argument does not prove that for entities which are not divisible, such as the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) or God (al-wājib taʿālā), their unity is non-existent. The suggestion that there is no difference between divisible and indivisible entities in this respect is baseless and not accepted.

Key Terms
رفع الإيجاب الكلي
السلب الكلي
الجوهر الفرد
الواجب تعالى
الوحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the divisibility argument disproves the reality of unity for all things, including indivisible entities.
Correction: The argument only shows that not everything is indivisible; it does not prove that nothing is indivisible. Indivisible entities like the atom and God can still have unity.

Checkpoint
What is the commentator's main criticism of the second argument?
A) It is circular.
B) It only negates universal affirmation, not universal negation.
C) It relies on a false premise.
D) It is too long.

Key Takeaway
The divisibility argument only disproves that every entity is indivisible; it does not prove that no entity is indivisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
When you think about the 'indivisible atom' and God, do you consider how our limited categories might fail to fully describe the Creator? How does this lesson humble your intellectual pride?

Proof that Unity is Non-Existential from Divisibility
Sharh al-Mawaqif presents the argument that unity cannot be an existent attribute because it would be divisible in a divisible subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that unity cannot be an existent attribute because it would have to divide with a divisible subject, and how theologians respond to objections about non-sarayani indwelling.

Core Idea
If unity were an existent attribute of a divisible body, it would have to be divisible—which is absurd.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a classic kalam proof that unity is a mental consideration (amr iʿtibārī) rather than an actual existent attribute. The argument systematically examines how an existent unity would have to inhere in a divisible subject, showing each possibility fails. It then addresses a key objection involving the concept of non-sarayani indwelling, strengthening the overall case. Let’s walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once. Try to follow the flow of the argument before analyzing it in detail.
The passage starts with the main argument and then introduces an objection and its answer.
2
Identify the three options for inherence
The argument considers three possibilities for where an existent unity inheres in a divisible subject: in one part, in no part, or in every part. Notice how each leads to a contradiction.
The third option splits into 'completely' and 'not completely'.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection claims that inherence could be non-sarayani — in the aggregate as a whole without division. The answer rebuffs this by arguing that sarayani inherence is actually required for an attribute in a divisible subject.
The response uses the idea that every part of the subject must have a part of the attribute.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
After establishing that an existent unity would have to be divisible, the argument concludes that unity is not an existent attribute but a mental consideration.
This conclusion is crucial for the broader discussion on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني إِنَّ الواحد قد يقبل القسمة كالجسم الواحد ( وانقسام المحل يوجب انقسام ما حل فيه لأنه إن كان الحال الذي هو الوحدة مثلاً ( في جزء منه كان ( ذلك الجزء من المحل ( هو الواحد ) لأن الوحدة قائمة به ( دون الكل ) والمقدر خلافه ( وإن لم يكن الحال ( في شيء من أجزائه لم يكن بالضرورة صفة (له أتي للمحل الذي فرضناه موصوفاً به وهذا أيضاً باطل ( وإن كان ) ( الحال ( في كل جزء من المحل ( فإما بالتمام فيقوم الواحد ) الشخصي (بالكثير) وقد عرفت بطلانه بديهة ) أو لا بالتمام فيكون جزء منه قائما بجزء وجزء بآخر وهو المراد بالانقسام يعني انقسام الحال بحسب انقسام المحل وقد اعترض على هذا الاستدلال بأنه يجوز أن يقوم الحال بمجموع المحل المنقسم من حيث هو مجموع، ويكون صفة له وإن لم ينقسم بانقسامه. ومثل ذلك يسمى حلولاً غير سرياني فأشار إليه وإلى جوابه بقوله : ( وقول من قال : (هذا) الذي ذكرتموه ( إنما يصح فيما يكون الحلول) في المحل المنقسم ( حلول السريان فيه إذ بدونه لا يلزم انقسام الحال بانقسام محله ) ولا طائل (له ( أي لا فائدة فيه ( لأنا برهنا على أن كل جزء من المحل ) المنقسم الذي حل فيه صفة ) متصف بجزء منها ولا معنى للسريان إلا ذلك ) وفيه بحث، لأن حاصل ذلك الاعتراض أنا لا نسلم أنه إذا لم يكن الحال ولا شيء منه في جزء من أجزاء المحل لم يكن صفة له ودعوى الضرورة غير مسموعة لجواز أن يكون حالا في المجموع من حيث هو ولا يكون حالاً في شيء من أجزائه كالنقطة الخط، والإضافة في محلها عند القائل بوجودهما هذا وإذا ثبت أن الحال في المحل المنقسم يجب أن يكون منقسما بحسبه ( فإذا كانت الوحدة وجودية لزم انقسامها ) بانقسام الجسم الذي حلت فيه ( وإنه ) أعني انقسام الوحدة (ضروري البطلان ) فوجب أن تكون الوحدة أمراً اعتبارياً
Secondly: the one may accept division, like the one body. And the division of the subject necessitates the division of what inheres in it, because if the inherence – for example unity – is in a part of it, then that part of the subject is the one, because unity subsists in it, not in the whole, and the assumption is contrary. And if the inherence is not in any of its parts, then necessarily it is not an attribute for it, so the subject which we supposed as described by it would not be described by it, and this is also false. And if the inherence is in every part of the subject, then either completely, so the individual one subsists by many, and you know its falsity intuitively, or not completely, so a part of it subsists by a part and a part by another, and that is what is meant by division – i.e., division of the inherence according to the division of the subject. And this argument was objected to by saying that it is permissible for the inherence to subsist by the aggregate of the divided subject insofar as it is an aggregate, and be an attribute for it without being divided by its division. Such is called non-sarayānī indwelling. So he pointed to it and to its answer by saying: 'And the statement of one who said: this which you have mentioned is only valid when the indwelling in the divided subject is sarayānī indwelling, because otherwise the division of the inherence by the division of its subject does not follow. And there is no benefit in it, because we have proven that every part of the divided subject in which an attribute inheres is characterized by a part of it, and the meaning of sarayān is nothing other than that.' And there is discussion in it, because the gist of that objection is that we do not accept that if the inherence nor anything of it is in any part of the subject, then it is not an attribute for it; and the claim of necessity is not listened to, because it is permissible for it to be an inherence in the aggregate insofar as it is an aggregate without being in anything of its parts, like the point in the line, and the relation in its subject according to those who affirm their existence. This being so, and if it is established that the inherence in the divided subject must be divided according to it, then if unity is existent, its division is necessary by the division of the body in which it inheres, and it – i.e., the division of unity – is necessarily false. Therefore unity must be a mental consideration.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (istidlāl) that unity (al-waḥda) is not an existent attribute but a mental consideration. The argument runs: take a divisible body described as one. If unity were an existent attribute inhering in that body, then because the body is divisible, the unity must inhere either in one part, in no part, or in every part. If in one part, only that part would be one, not the whole. If in no part, the body would not be described by unity. If in every part, either the whole unity is in each part – impossible, as one thing cannot subsist by many – or part of unity is in each part, meaning unity divides. All options are false, so unity cannot be an existent attribute. An objection is raised: the inherence could be in the aggregate as a whole, called non-sarayānī indwelling, which would not require division. The answer: we have proven that for any inherence in a divisible subject, each part is characterized by a part of the attribute; that is exactly sarayān. The commentator adds a discussion (وفيه بحث), noting that the objector denies the necessity that if no part contains the inherence then the subject lacks the attribute, giving examples like a point on a line. The argument then concludes: since inherence in a divisible subject requires division of the inherence, if unity were existent it would have to be divisible, which is impossible; therefore unity is merely a mental consideration (amr iʿtibārī). The proof is built step by step, with objections answered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is sometimes thought to deny the oneness of God.
Correction: Actually, the argument shows that unity is a mental concept applied to existents, not an existent attribute itself. It does not deny that things can be described as one; it only denies that unity is a concrete, independent entity.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the argument presented in this lesson?
A) Unity is an existent attribute that divides with the body.
B) Unity must be a mental consideration because if it were existent, it would be divisible, which is impossible.
C) Unity is not a real property of anything.
D) The argument fails for indivisible entities.

Key Takeaway
Unity is a mental consideration because an existent unity in a divisible subject would have to be divisible, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call something 'one'—like your life, your body, or your faith—do you assume that oneness is a real thing added to it? Consider: if your life is divided into moments and parts, does its unity exist outside your mind? How does that challenge your attachment to the idea of a unified self?
?
The argument shows that unity cannot be an existent quality without absurdity. In what area of your belief or practice might you be treating a conceptual unity (like 'my identity,' 'my religion,' or 'my community') as if it were a concrete thing that can be divided and possessed? What would change if you saw it as a mental consideration?
?
God is one, but not with a unity that is an attribute. How does this distinction protect your faith from imagining God as composed or divisible? Do you ever think of God's oneness in a way that limits Him? Let this lesson correct that assumption.

Answer to the Objection that Mental Unity Would Also Be Divisible
Objection and answer regarding the divisibility of mental unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mind's consideration of totality prevents the division of mental unity, resolving a key objection to the reality of unity.

Core Idea
Mental unity does not divide because the mind considers the whole, not the parts.

In this passage, the author addresses a sophisticated objection: if unity is mental, it would still be divisible in the mind. The answer reveals a subtle insight about how the mind abstracts from division by considering the aggregate as a single whole. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the dilemma: unity must be either existent (wujūdī) or mental (iʿtibārī). If existent, it divides externally; if mental, it divides in estimation (tawahhum). Both lead to impossibility.
The objector uses a dichotomy to force a contradiction.
2
Read the answer
See how the answer distinguishes between considering the subject in its parts versus considering it as a whole (ijmāl). The mind conceives non-division by looking at the aggregate, not the details.
The key term is 'al-majmūʿ min ḥaythu al-ijmāl' – the aggregate from the perspective of totality.
3
Reflect on 'mental aspects'
The answer adds that mental considerations (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyya) cannot be applied to external things. This reinforces that unity is a mental concept of wholeness, not a real divisible attribute.
4
Consider the broader significance
This answer preserves the possibility of unity as a valid attribute, defending the kalam position against a powerful objection.
It shows the importance of perspective in metaphysical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: الوحدة التي هي صفة للجسم بحسب نفس الأمر، إن كانت وجودية وجب انقسامها بحسب الخارج، وإن كانت اعتبارية وجب انقسامها بحسب التوهم. وكلاهما محال قلت : إنَّ العقل يعتبر المجموع من حيث الإجمال فيعتبر له عدم الانقسام أعني الوحدة فلا يلزم انقسامها أصلاً لأن محلها ملحوظ من حيثية لا مجال فيها للانقسام ولا يمكن اعتبار الحيثيات العقلية في الأمور الخارجية
For you might say: Unity which is an attribute of the body according to the reality of the matter, if it were existent, its division would be necessary according to the external, and if it were mental, its division would be necessary according to estimation. Both are impossible. I say: The mind considers the aggregate from the perspective of totality, so it considers for it non-division, meaning unity; thus its division does not follow at all, because its substrate is considered from a perspective in which there is no room for division. And the consideration of mental aspects cannot be applied to external things.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This is an objection and answer. The objector argues: unity, as an attribute of a body, must be either existent or mental. If existent, it would divide externally (as established). If mental, it would still divide in the mind (tawahhum), because mental attributes follow the division of their subject in conception. Both are impossible, so unity cannot be attributed at all. The answer: the mind does not consider the subject in its parts but as a whole (from the perspective of totality, ijmāl). It considers the aggregate as undivided, i.e., unity. Thus no division of unity occurs even mentally, because the substrate is taken from a perspective where division has no place. Moreover, mental considerations (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyya) are not applied to external things in the same way; the unity is a mental concept of the whole, not a real attribute that divides.

اعتبارية
وجودية
التوهم
الإجمال
الحيثيات العقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If unity is mental, it must still be divisible in the mind just like the subject.
Correction: The mind does not necessarily consider the subject with its parts; it can abstract from division by considering the aggregate as a whole, thus unity conceived as non-division remains coherent.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The mind's ability to consider an aggregate as a whole allows for a concept of unity that is not subject to division, even in estimation.
