The Priority of Yesterday over Today: Temporal Priority Leads to Infinite Regress
Argument that temporal priority implies an infinite regress of times, which is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun argue that the priority of yesterday over today must be temporal, and how this leads to an infinite regress of times.

Core Idea
Temporal priority between two events requires a third time, leading to an infinite chain.

The mutakallimun begin by examining the relationship between yesterday and today. They rule out all other forms of priority—causal, essential, rank, and nobility—leaving only temporal priority. This forces them to posit a time that contains both, but then that time itself has a yesterday and today, requiring yet another time, ad infinitum.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial setup
Read the first part of the passage where the mutakallimun consider the types of priority and conclude that the priority of yesterday over today is temporal.
Notice they use the phrase 'عندكم أيها الحكماء' addressing the philosophers.
2
Identify the definition of temporal priority
Temporal priority means the prior is in an earlier time and the posterior in a later time. This implies that yesterday is in a time and today is in a time.
3
Understand the regress argument
The time that contains yesterday and today itself has parts (its own yesterday and today) which also have temporal priority, leading to an infinite series of overlapping times.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Note the conclusion of impossibility
The mutakallimun conclude that such an infinite regress of times existing simultaneously is impossible in itself.
This is the first conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد السابع : إنكار المتكلمين للزمن
يتصور بينهما تقدم بالعلية ولا بالذات وهي في أنفسها متساوية في الشرف فلا تقدم بحسبه ولا بحسب الرتبة، لأن التقدم الرتبي يتبدل بالاعتبار، وتقدم الأمس على اليوم لازم لا يتبدل ( فهو بالزمان لانحصاره عندكم أيها الحكماء في خمسة فإذا انتفى أربعة منها تعين الخامس ) فيكون للزمان (زمان) لأن معنى التقدم الزماني أن المتقدم في زمان سابق والمتأخر في زمان لاحق فيكون الأمس في زمان متقدم واليوم في زمان متأخر عنه ( والكلام في ذلك الزمان وتقدم بعض أجزائه على بعض ويلزم التسلسل في الأزمنة الموجودة معاً، أي يلزم أن يكون هناك أزمنة غير متناهية منطبق بعضها على بعض ( وأنه محال) في نفسه بالضرورة ومع ذلك أي ومع كونه محالاً يستلزم وهو أن يقال (فمجموع) تلك (الأزمنة) التي لا تتناهى وينطبق بعضها على بعض، (يكون أمسها مقدماً على يومها) تقدماً ( بالزمان ) لامتناع اجتماع. فيكون أمس المجموع واقعاً في زمان ويومه واقعاً في زمان آخر ( فزمان المجموع ظرف له لوقوعه فيه ( فيكون ) ذلك الزمان ( داخلا في المجموع) لأنه زمان من الأزمنة المتطابقة ( وإلا ) وإن لم يكن داخلاً فيه ( لم يكن المجموع) الذي فرضناه (مجموعاً) لخروج بعض الآحاد عنه حينئذ، (و) يكون (خارجاً) أيضاً (عن المجموع لأن ظرف الشيء لا يكون جزءه وأنّه ) أي كونه داخلاً وخارجاً معاً بالقياس إلى المجموع ( محال
The Second Observatory - Seventh Objective: The Denial of Time by the Mutakallimun. A priority by causality and not by essence is conceivable between them, and they are in themselves equal in nobility, so there is no priority according to it nor according to rank, because rank priority changes with consideration, and the priority of yesterday over today is necessary and does not change. (So it is by time, because it is confined according to you, O philosophers, to five [types]; when four of them are negated, the fifth is determined.) So time would have a time, because the meaning of temporal priority is that the prior is in a previous time and the posterior is in a later time. Thus yesterday is in a previous time and today is in a time posterior to it. (And the discussion is about that time and the priority of some of its parts over others, and an infinite regress follows in times existing simultaneously, i.e., it follows that there are infinite times overlapping each other. And that is impossible in itself necessarily. And with that, i.e., with it being impossible, it entails [another impossibility], namely that one says: The sum of those infinite times overlapping each other — its yesterday is prior to its day by time, because it is impossible for them to coexist. So the yesterday of the sum occurs in a time and its day occurs in another time. So the time of the sum is a container for it because of its occurrence in it. Thus that time would be included in the sum, because it is one of the overlapping times. Otherwise, if it were not included in it, the sum that we assumed would not be a sum because some individuals would be excluded from it. And it would also be outside the sum, because the container of a thing is not its part. And that — i.e., its being both inside and outside with respect to the sum — is impossible.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's proof for the non-existence of time as a real entity. It begins by considering priority (taraqqub) between two things: priority can be by causality or by essence, but here the priority of yesterday over today is not by causality or essence, nor by rank (since rank changes with perspective), nor by nobility (since they are equal). So the only remaining type is temporal priority. This priority of yesterday over today implies that yesterday is in a time and today is in a time, so there is a time that contains both. But then that time itself has parts (its own yesterday and today) which also have temporal priority, requiring another time, and so on ad infinitum. This leads to an infinite series of overlapping times, which is absurd. Moreover, even if one tries to conceive of the sum of all these times, a contradiction arises: the time that contains the sum would have to be both inside the sum (as one of its members) and outside the sum (as its container), which is impossible. Thus the original assumption that time exists leads to impossibility.

Key Terms
تقدم بالزمان
تسلسل
انطباق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that time can be defined without reference to another time.
Correction: The argument shows that any temporal relation, like 'yesterday before today,' presupposes a time that contains both, leading to a regress.

Checkpoint
Why do the mutakallimun conclude that the priority of yesterday over today is temporal?
A) Because it is causal
B) Because it is not by causality, essence, nobility, or rank
C) Because it is by essence
D) Because it is by rank

Key Takeaway
The priority of yesterday over today must be temporal, which implies an infinite regress of times, making time's existence impossible.

?

How does this affect your understanding of the Day of Judgment and the Hereafter as being 'after' this life? Are you prepared to think of the afterlife not as a future point in a real timeline but as something that is already present in a different order? Let the argument unsettle your assumption that time is a container.
?
This lesson argues that the priority of yesterday over today leads to an infinite regress of times, making time's existence impossible. Do you live as though time is a real thing that can be owned and spent, or does this argument shake your reliance on time as a constant? How would your life change if you truly believed that there is no independent entity called 'time' — only a succession of moments created by Allah? Ask yourself: Are you using time as an excuse to delay repentance, thinking there will be a tomorrow? Write a concrete change you will make today based on this reality.
?
The mutakallimun used this argument to deny the reality of time. If time is not a real thing, then your past sins are not actually 'in the past' as a separate time — they are present in the same created order. Does that thought make you want to turn to Allah for forgiveness immediately? What will you do within the next hour to seek forgiveness?

The Contradiction of the Sum of Times: Inside and Outside
Argument that the sum of times leads to a contradiction of being both inside and outside the sum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's argument that even if one attempts to conceive of the sum of all overlapping times, a contradiction arises because the time containing the sum must be both inside and outside the sum.

Core Idea
The time containing the sum of all times must be part of the sum and not part of it—a contradiction.

Even if we accept the infinite regress of times, the mutakallimun show a further impossibility. They consider the sum of all those overlapping times. The sum's yesterday is prior to its day, so it must occur in a different time. But that time—the container—must be one of the times in the sum (otherwise the sum is incomplete) yet also cannot be part of it (since a container is not its part). This contradiction proves the impossibility of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the argument
Read the passage starting from 'فمجموع تلك الأزمنة' to the end.
This builds on the previous conclusion of infinite regress.
2
Understand the concept of the sum
The mutakallimun imagine taking all the overlapping times together as a single sum. They then consider the priority of the sum's yesterday over its day.
3
Identify the container time
The priority of the sum's yesterday over its day implies that the sum occurs in a time, i.e., it has a container time. That container time must be one of the times in the sum (otherwise the sum is incomplete).
4
Recognize the contradiction
But a container cannot be part of what it contains, so the container time must also be outside the sum. Thus it is both inside and outside—impossible.
This contradiction shows the initial assumption of time's existence leads to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد السابع : إنكار المتكلمين للزمن
يتصور بينهما تقدم بالعلية ولا بالذات وهي في أنفسها متساوية في الشرف فلا تقدم بحسبه ولا بحسب الرتبة، لأن التقدم الرتبي يتبدل بالاعتبار، وتقدم الأمس على اليوم لازم لا يتبدل ( فهو بالزمان لانحصاره عندكم أيها الحكماء في خمسة فإذا انتفى أربعة منها تعين الخامس ) فيكون للزمان (زمان) لأن معنى التقدم الزماني أن المتقدم في زمان سابق والمتأخر في زمان لاحق فيكون الأمس في زمان متقدم واليوم في زمان متأخر عنه ( والكلام في ذلك الزمان وتقدم بعض أجزائه على بعض ويلزم التسلسل في الأزمنة الموجودة معاً، أي يلزم أن يكون هناك أزمنة غير متناهية منطبق بعضها على بعض ( وأنه محال) في نفسه بالضرورة ومع ذلك أي ومع كونه محالاً يستلزم وهو أن يقال (فمجموع) تلك (الأزمنة) التي لا تتناهى وينطبق بعضها على بعض، (يكون أمسها مقدماً على يومها) تقدماً ( بالزمان ) لامتناع اجتماع. فيكون أمس المجموع واقعاً في زمان ويومه واقعاً في زمان آخر ( فزمان المجموع ظرف له لوقوعه فيه ( فيكون ) ذلك الزمان ( داخلا في المجموع) لأنه زمان من الأزمنة المتطابقة ( وإلا ) وإن لم يكن داخلاً فيه ( لم يكن المجموع) الذي فرضناه (مجموعاً) لخروج بعض الآحاد عنه حينئذ، (و) يكون (خارجاً) أيضاً (عن المجموع لأن ظرف الشيء لا يكون جزءه وأنّه ) أي كونه داخلاً وخارجاً معاً بالقياس إلى المجموع ( محال
The Second Observatory - Seventh Objective: The Denial of Time by the Mutakallimun. A priority by causality and not by essence is conceivable between them, and they are in themselves equal in nobility, so there is no priority according to it nor according to rank, because rank priority changes with consideration, and the priority of yesterday over today is necessary and does not change. (So it is by time, because it is confined according to you, O philosophers, to five [types]; when four of them are negated, the fifth is determined.) So time would have a time, because the meaning of temporal priority is that the prior is in a previous time and the posterior is in a later time. Thus yesterday is in a previous time and today is in a time posterior to it. (And the discussion is about that time and the priority of some of its parts over others, and an infinite regress follows in times existing simultaneously, i.e., it follows that there are infinite times overlapping each other. And that is impossible in itself necessarily. And with that, i.e., with it being impossible, it entails [another impossibility], namely that one says: The sum of those infinite times overlapping each other — its yesterday is prior to its day by time, because it is impossible for them to coexist. So the yesterday of the sum occurs in a time and its day occurs in another time. So the time of the sum is a container for it because of its occurrence in it. Thus that time would be included in the sum, because it is one of the overlapping times. Otherwise, if it were not included in it, the sum that we assumed would not be a sum because some individuals would be excluded from it. And it would also be outside the sum, because the container of a thing is not its part. And that — i.e., its being both inside and outside with respect to the sum — is impossible.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's proof for the non-existence of time as a real entity. It begins by considering priority (taraqqub) between two things: priority can be by causality or by essence, but here the priority of yesterday over today is not by causality or essence, nor by rank (since rank changes with perspective), nor by nobility (since they are equal). So the only remaining type is temporal priority. This priority of yesterday over today implies that yesterday is in a time and today is in a time, so there is a time that contains both. But then that time itself has parts (its own yesterday and today) which also have temporal priority, requiring another time, and so on ad infinitum. This leads to an infinite series of overlapping times, which is absurd. Moreover, even if one tries to conceive of the sum of all these times, a contradiction arises: the time that contains the sum would have to be both inside the sum (as one of its members) and outside the sum (as its container), which is impossible. Thus the original assumption that time exists leads to impossibility.

Correction: The argument shows that any aggregate with temporal parts requires a container time, leading to a contradiction.

Key Takeaway
The sum of all times leads to a contradiction of being both inside and outside its container, proving time's impossibility.

The Answer: Temporal Priority is Intrinsic to Time
Response that temporal priority between parts of time is intrinsic, avoiding regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that temporal priority between parts of time is intrinsic (bi al-dhat) to time itself, thereby avoiding an infinite regress of times.

Core Idea
Priority between parts of time is not by another time; it is intrinsic to time itself.

The previous argument raised the problem of infinite regress if every temporal priority required a distinct time. In this passage, the author provides the answer: when we speak of priority between parts of time, that priority is not mediated by a separate time but is an inherent property of time. This lesson unpacks this key distinction and shows how it stops the regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the phrase 'temporal priority does not require that each be in a different time.'
2
Distinguish intrinsic vs. extrinsic priority
Notice the distinction between priority that is 'an accident of the parts of time essentially' and priority of other things through them. Understand that for parts of time, no extra time is needed.
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
The author argues that if every priority required another priority, there would be an infinite chain, which is absurd. The example of father and son illustrates this.
4
Grasp the solution: essential priority
The regress stops at what is prior essentially, namely time itself. Time is defined as continuous passing away and renewal, which gives intrinsic priority to its parts.

And it was answered concerning this aspect: (that the priority of the parts of time) some over others, even if it is priority by time, is not priority by another time. For temporal priority does not require that each of the prior and posterior be in a time different from it, but rather requires that the prior be before the posterior in a priority in which the before does not coexist with the after. This priority does not exist without time. If the prior and posterior in this priority are not parts of time, they must occur in two times, one prior to the other. But if they are parts of time, then priority there is not by a time additional to the prior, but by a time that is the prior itself, because the mentioned priority is an accident (arid) of the parts of time essentially (bi al-dhat) and of others through their intermediacy. To this he alludes by his saying: 'So priority is an accident of them' — i.e., of the parts of time — 'essentially, and of others through their intermediacy, since not every priority that is an accident of a thing [requires] another priority that is an accident of another thing, otherwise there would be an infinite regress, and with the priority of father over son, for example, there would be infinite priorities accidental to infinite prior things, and that is absolutely false. So one must end at what is prior essentially, and that is what you call time.' For its essence, as you will come to know it, is the continuity of passing away and renewal, i.e., non-stability. So if parts are assumed in it, they exhibit [priority]... His saying: 'For temporal priority, etc.' And if you refuse to apply the term 'temporal priority' except to what is by time, then let this be a sixth type and name it whatever you wish of priority by essence and others. His saying: 'Continuity' of passing away 'and renewal' does not intend its apparent meaning, for continuity and renewal are impossible, and because continuity is not quantity and time is quantity. Rather, by continuity he means the continuous, for they express that which is continuous in itself as continuity, because it is an intrinsic necessary attribute of it, as if it were continuity itself. And its addition to passing away and renewal... its existence is impossible, as is apparent from the text, or by its entailing the impossible infinite regress here, and by its entailing an impossibility without demonstrating the impossibility of the entailment of the regress for the mentioned impossibility until what is mentioned is pondered.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the answer to the previous argument. It begins by stating that when two parts of time are said to be prior and posterior, the priority is not by a separate time but by the very nature of time itself. Temporal priority means a 'beforeness' that does not coexist with 'afterness'; this beforeness exists only within time. If the compared items are not parts of time, they need two different times. But if they are parts of time, no extra time is needed: the priority is intrinsic (bi al-dhat) to the parts of time, and for other things it is derived. The commentator then explains that if every priority required another priority, there would be an infinite regress, as in the example of father and son. So there must be a fundamental priority that is essential, and that is what we call time. The essence of time is continuous passing away and renewal (non-stability). The commentary then clarifies that the phrase 'continuity of passing away and renewal' should not be taken literally, because time is a continuous quantity, and renewal is not a quantity. Rather, 'continuity' refers to the continuous nature of time itself. The passage concludes with a note that this explanation resolves the regress.

Temporal Precedence: Objection and Answer
Objection and answer on the nature of temporal precedence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians defend the inherent precedence among parts of time against an objection that would lead to contradiction.

Core Idea
Parts of time have inherent precedence, not requiring a separate time.

This passage presents a sophisticated dialectical exchange in kalam. An objection argues that if parts of time are parts of a whole, their container time leads to a contradiction. The answer reveals that temporal precedence among time-parts is not extrinsic but intrinsic, avoiding infinite regress. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection starts from a whole composed of yesterday and today. Consider that the time containing the whole must be either inside or outside the whole, leading to a contradiction. Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument.
The objection is presented up to '...is impossible'.
2
Identify the claimed contradiction
Notice why the time of the whole cannot be inside (it would be part of the whole) or outside (the container is not part of the contained). This creates a paradox for the assumption that parts of time form a whole.
3
Read the answer and the key distinction
The answer denies that temporal precedence requires a separate time. For parts of time themselves, precedence is by the prior part itself, not by another time. This avoids the container problem.
The answer begins with 'And it was answered...'
4
Understand why infinite regress is avoided
If precedence always required an external time, there would be an infinite chain of times. The answer shows that for time-parts, precedence is accidental to them by essence, so no external time is needed.
The author gives the example of father and son to illustrate the absurdity.
5
Reflect on the significance for kalam
This argument preserves the coherence of time without postulating an infinite regress. It shows how careful analysis of temporal concepts is central to theological reasoning.

How Time's Parts Acquire Priority and Posteriority
Commentary on how time's parts acquire priority and the role of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how priority and posteriority are attributed to parts of time and motion, and why time is considered a discrete quantity in the mind but continuous in itself.

Core Idea
Time's parts acquire priority in the mind, not externally; motion borrows it from distance.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a subtle distinction: time's parts are not inherently prior or posterior in external reality; they are continuous and non-stable. Priority and posteriority only arise in the mind when we suppose parts. Motion, on the other hand, gets its priority and posteriority from the distance it traverses. This clarifies the nature of time as a number and magnitude derived from motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the reference to Avicenna's Shifā' in the latter part.
2
Understand the core distinction
Identify that time is not characterized by priority externally; it is continuous and non-stable. Parts acquire priority only in the mind.
3
Contrast with motion
Motion's priority comes from its application to a continuous distance. Motion itself is not necessarily continuous; its parts gain priority from the distance.
The passage cites Avicenna for detail on how motion acquires number and magnitude.
4
Grasp the conclusion about time
Time is the number and magnitude arising from motion applied to distance. This refutes the idea that time's parts are inherently prior.
The final answer refutes a specific objection by showing mere non-coexistence suffices.

قوله : ( فإذا فرض إلخ) يعني أنَّه ليس موصوفاً بالتقدم والتأخر في الخارج حتى يلزم كونه كماً منفصلاً وكونه مجتمع الأجزاء، بناء على أن التقدم والتأخر لكونهما إضافتين توجدان معاً فيكون معووضاهما موجودين معاً بل هو أمر متصل في ذاته غير مستقر إذا فرض له أجزاء عرض لها في الذهن التقدم والتأخر لذاتها لكونها أجزاء لأمر غير مستقر . قوله : ( ولا يحتاج في عروضهما إلخ) وإن كان يحتاج في ثبوتهما إلى الحركة فهي واسطة في الثبوت لا في العروض. قوله : ( بخلاف ما عداها حتى الحركة فإن حقيقتها كمال ما بالقوة، وليس يلزمها اتصال حتى لو فرضنا ثلاثة أجزاء لا تتجزأ، وكان المتحرك حين يتحرك في الأوسط عند حركته إلى الثالث كمال ما بالقوة لم يكن على متصل، فنفس كونه كمالة ما بالقوة لا أن تكون منقسمة فضلاً عن أن تكون أجزاؤها متقدمة ،ومتأخرة وإنما يعرض الانقسام والتقدم والتأخر بسبب انطباقها على المسافة الموصوفة بالاتصال والتقدم والتأخر، وتفصيله ما ذكره الشيخ في الشفاء أنَّ الحركة يلحقها أن ينقسم إلى متقدم ومتأخر، وإنما يوجد فيها المتقدم ما يكون منها في التقدم من المسافة والمتأخر ما يوجد منها في المتأخر من المسافة لكن يتبع ذلك أن المتقدم للحركة لا يوجد مع المتأخر منها، كما يوجد المتقدم والمتأخر في المسافة معاً فيكون للتقدم وللتأخر في الحركة خاصية يلحقها من جهة ما هما ليست من جهة ما هما للمسافة ويكونان معدودين بالحركة فإن الحركة بأجزائها بعدم المتقدم والمتأخر فتكون الحركة لها عدد من حيث لها في المسافة تقدم وتأخر ، ولها مقدار أيضاً بإزاء مقدار المسافة والزمان هو هذا العدد والمقدار . • يوجب يعرضها عدم الاستقرار ولا شك أن الحركة أيضاً كذلك فهذا التقرير لا يفيد كون عروض التقدم لأجزاء الزمان بحسب ذاتها ولجميع ما عداها بواسطتها وأما حديث انقطاع السؤال فقد عرفت ما فيه قوله : ( وقد أجيب عنه أيضاً إلخ) قد أشرنا إلى أن مجرد عدم اجتماع المقدم والمؤخر الظاهر في أجزاء الزمان يكفي في أصل الاستدلال فهذا الجواب إنما يفيد مجرد نفي القول بعدم
His saying: (Then if parts are supposed, etc.) means that it is not characterized by priority and posteriority in the external such that it would necessitate its being a discrete quantity and its having its parts coexistent, on the basis that priority and posteriority, being relations, coexist and thus their subjects would also coexist; rather it is a continuous thing in itself, non-stable; if parts are supposed for it, then priority and posteriority occur to them in the mind by themselves because they are parts of a non-stable thing. His saying: (And they do not need in their occurrence to them, etc.): even if they need in their existence motion, it is a means in existence, not in occurrence. His saying: (Unlike other things, even motion, for its reality is perfection of potentiality, and it is not necessarily continuous; even if we suppose three indivisible parts, and the moving object while moving in the middle at its motion to the third is a perfection of potentiality, it is not continuous; so its being a perfection of potentiality does not require it to be divisible, let alone its parts being prior and posterior; rather division and priority and posteriority occur to it due to its application to the distance described by continuity and priority and posteriority. The detail is what the Master mentioned in the Shifā': that motion is susceptible to being divided into prior and posterior; what is in the prior part of the distance is the prior of motion, and what is in the posterior part of the distance is the posterior of motion; but it follows that the prior of motion does not coexist with its posterior, while the prior and posterior in distance coexist; thus priority and posteriority in motion have a particularity that attaches to them from one aspect, not from the aspect they have for distance; and they are enumerated by motion, for motion with its parts lacks the prior and posterior, so motion has a number from the fact that it has priority and posteriority in the distance, and it also has a magnitude corresponding to the magnitude of the distance; and time is this number and magnitude. • It necessitates that non-stability occur to it, and there is no doubt that motion is also so; thus this statement does not establish that the occurrence of priority to parts of time is by themselves and to all others by their mediation. As for the point about the cessation of the question, you have known what there is in it. His saying: (And it was also answered, etc.): we have indicated that mere non-coexistence of prior and posterior apparent in parts of time suffices for the basis of the argument; this answer only serves to refute the saying of non-...

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the definition of time, focusing on how its parts acquire priority and posteriority and contrasting with motion. The commentator explains that time's parts are not characterized by priority in the external world; rather, they are a continuous non-stable reality, and priority/posteriority occur to them only in the mind. Motion, unlike time, is not inherently continuous; its priority and posteriority come from its application to a continuous distance. The commentator refers to Avicenna's Shifā' to explain that motion has number and magnitude derived from distance, and time is this number and magnitude. The passage ends by noting that the answer given earlier only refutes the denial of a particular claim, not the entire argument.

Ibn Sina's Response and Its Critique: The Mismatch of Arguments
Ibn Sina's response and commentator's critique on argument mismatch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Sina's objection to the argument for time's existence does not apply when the argument is framed as an ilzām (necessary implication) rather than a simple disjunctive syllogism.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's response refutes a straw man, not the actual argument.

Ibn Sina offers a logical objection to the claim that time must exist in the present, past, or future. The commentator, however, points out that this objection only works against a weaker formulation of the argument. Ibn Sina's answer, while logically sound on its own terms, does not engage with the author's more sophisticated argument by necessary implication. This lesson unpacks the critical distinction between two argument structures and why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's objection
Read the Arabic passage of Ibn Sina's response carefully. Identify his claim: the three temporal categories are 'more specific' (أخص) than existence in general, so failing to be in them doesn't negate existence.
Note that he uses the terms الأخص and الأعم.
2
Understand the commentator's critique
The commentator argues that Ibn Sina's objection is misdirected because it assumes a different formulation of the argument (the simple disjunctive 'ظرفية'). The actual argument by al-Ījī is an ilzām (necessary implication), which is not vulnerable to this objection.
The key term is الظرفية (containment).
3
See the mismatch
The passage concludes that the author's statement (that Ibn Sina answered) is problematic because it conflates two different interpretations. Recognize that this is a meta-critique of how the response was framed.
Think about why precision in argument formulation matters in kalam.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this distinction affects the overall debate on time's existence. Does Ibn Sina's response survive the commentator's critique? Why or why not?
This illustrates a common pattern in kalam: critiquing not just the argument but the opponent's understanding of it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجاب عنه ابن سينا ) بأن قال: (لم قلتم : أنه لو وجد ) الزمان ) فإما في الآن ( أي الحاضر ) أو في الماضي أو في المستقبل فإنَّ كلاً منها أخص من الموجود المطلق، ولا يلزم من كذب الأخص ) وانتفائه ( كذب الأعم) وانتفاؤه (وهو مشكل لأنَّ وجود الشيء ( مع أنه لا يوجد في الحال ولا في الماضي ولا في المستقبل ) 
قوله : ( وهو مشكل إلخ) لا يخفى عليك أن هذا الإشكال غير وارد على ما قررنا الجواب مطابقاً لتقرير المصنف للاستدلال، وإنما يرد لو قرر الجواب على ما قرره القوم جواباً عن الاستدلال بطريق الظرفية حيث قالوا : إِنَّ الزمان لو كان موجوداً فإما أن يوجد في الحال أو في الماضي أو في المستقبل، لكن الجواب حينئذ لا يكون جواباً عن تقرير المصنف فلا يصح قوله : أجاب عنه والحاصل أنه لو قرر الجواب بطريق الظرفية كما في عبارة القوم، كان الإشكال وارداً عليه لكن لا يكون مطابقاً لتقرير المصنف، وإن قرر على وجه يطابق تقرير المصنف لا يتجه الإشكال المذكور، فكلام المصنف لا يخلو عن اختلال
Ibn Sina answered it by saying: 'Why do you say that if time existed, it would be either in the now (i.e., the present) or in the past or in the future? For each of them is more specific than absolute existence, and the falsity (and non-existence) of the more specific does not entail the falsity (and non-existence) of the more general.' And it is problematic because the existence of a thing (while it does not exist in the present, nor in the past, nor in the future). His saying: 'And it is problematic' – it is not hidden from you that this criticism does not apply to what we have set forth as the answer matching the author's presentation of the argument; rather, it would only apply if the answer were set forth as the people (the philosophers) set it as an answer to the argument by way of containment (al-ẓarfiyya), where they said: 'If time existed, it would exist either in the present, or in the past, or in the future.' But then the answer would not be an answer to the author's presentation, so his saying 'he answered it' would not be correct. The upshot is that if the answer were set forth by way of containment as in the people's expression, then the above-mentioned problem would arise, but it would not match the author's presentation; and if it is set forth in a way that matches the author's presentation, the mentioned problem does not apply. So the author's statement is not free from disorder.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's response to the argument that time must be in the present, past, or future. Ibn Sina objects that these categories are more specific than 'existence' in general; the non-existence of a specific mode does not entail the non-existence of the thing itself. For example, eternal things exist without being in any of the three temporal categories. Thus time could exist without being present, past, or future. The commentary then critiques this response: the criticism (problem) does not apply if the answer is framed according to the author's (al-Ījī's) original argument, which was an argument by necessary implication (ilzām) rather than a simple disjunctive syllogism. If the argument is taken as a simple disjunctive (as the philosophers understood it), then Ibn Sina's objection stands, but it does not address the author's actual argument. The commentator concludes that the author's statement (that Ibn Sina answered it) is problematic because it conflates two different interpretations.

الماضي
المستقبل
الأخص
الأعم
الظرفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's objection refutes the original argument for time's existence.
Correction: Ibn Sina's objection only refutes the argument if it is formulated as a simple disjunctive; it does not apply to the author's ilzām formulation.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does Ibn Sina's objection not apply to the author's argument?
A) Because time cannot be in the present.
B) Because the author's argument uses ilzām not a disjunctive.
C) Because Ibn Sina's argument is invalid.
D) Because the three categories are exhaustive.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's response only works against a different formulation of the argument; when the argument is properly understood as an ilzām, his objection does not apply.

?

Commit to one concrete step to improve your intellectual honesty this week.
?
The commentator points out that the author's own wording was disordered. When you explain complex ideas, do you take time to ensure your formulation is precise, or do you rely on your audience to 'get it' regardless? What is at stake if you are sloppy with your words, especially when dealing with matters of faith? Write down one resolution to clarify your speech before you speak next time.

The Denial of Future Time and Ibn Sina's Contradiction
Presents the mutakallimun's denial of future time and Ibn Sina's apparent self-contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's denial of future time, the apparent contradiction in Ibn Sina's response to the burhān al-taṭbīq, and the objection based on the distinction between time and temporal things, as well as its resolution.

Core Idea
Future time is impossible, and Ibn Sina's own logic contradicts this denial.

This lesson delves into a sophisticated argument from the mutakallimun regarding the nature of time. The claim that future time is inconceivable leads to a charge of contradiction against Ibn Sina, who seems to contradict his own principle that what does not exist in any of the three times is non-existent. As you read the passage, pay close attention to how the objection and subsequent clarification rely on a subtle distinction between time itself and temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument's flow. Notice the structure: a claim, a quotation, a charge of contradiction, an objection, and a resolution.
The passage uses technical terms like ظرفية (container-ness) and برهان التطبيق.
2
Identify the contradiction
Locate where Ibn Sina is quoted and where the author says he contradicted himself. Understand why the author sees a contradiction: Ibn Sina said that what does not exist in any of the three times is non-existent, yet he denies future time.
Future time is one of the three times, so denying it seems to violate his own rule.
3
Examine the objection
Read the objection that attempts to resolve the contradiction by distinguishing between time (which exists in itself) and temporal things (which must exist in a time). The objection claims that time can exist without being in past, present, or future.
The objection argues that time is not a temporal entity.
4
Understand the resolution
The passage then clarifies that on the interpretation of 'container-ness' (ẓarfiyya), the objection is repelled. That is, a changing thing like motion exists in all time but not in any particular part, so it does not need to be contained in a specific time.
This resolves the contradiction by showing that the original claim about future time is not contradicted.
5
Reflect on the concept of time
Consider the implications: the mutakallimun's denial of future time is based on the view that time is not an independent entity but merely a measure of motion. This lesson shows the complexity of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد السابع : إنكار المتكلمين للزمن المستقبل (متعذر بل هو غير متصور ( وقد ناقض ابن سينا ( نفسه حيث قال ) في جواب استدلالنا ببرهان التطبيق على امتناع وجود الحوادث المتعاقبة إلى غير النهاية : ( جميع الحركات الماضية التي لا تتناهى (لا توجد) أصلاً حتى يتصور فيها التطبيق وتتصف بالزيادة والنقصان ، ( وإلا ففي الماضي أو الحال أو المستقبل والكل باطل). فقد حكم هناك بأن ما لا يوجد في شيء من الأزمنة الثلاثة لم يكن موجوداً ومنعه هاهنا أنه تناقض صريح فإن قلت : لا مناقضة فإن ما ليس بزمان كالحركة زمانياً إذا لم يوجد في شيء من الأزمنة لم يكن موجوداً بخلاف الزمان كالماضي مثلاً فإنه عندنا موجود في حد نفسه وإن لم يكن موجوداً في الحال ولا في الاستقبال وهو ظاهر ولا في الماضي لاستحالة كون الشيء ظرفاً لنفسه وتوضيحه أن قطعا مثلا، ويسمى تقرير الظرفية وتقرير الفردية أو المقول بأن معنى قوله : أجاب عنه أجاب عن الوجه الثاني بناء على تقرير الظرفية ولذا قدر الشارح قدس سره قوله : ولما كان حاصل الوجه الثاني، وقرره بطريق الظرفية مما لا يفوه به عاقل فضلاً عن فاضل ثم اعلم أنه على تقرير الظرفية هذا الإشكال مندفع أيضاً لأن وجود الشيء مع أنه لا يوجد في الحال ولا في الماضي ولا في المستقبل ليس متعذراً بل إذا كان ذلك الشيء من المتغيرات ولا يكون منطبقا على كل الزمان كالحركة، فإنه موجود في كل الزمان وليس موجوداً في شيء من الأزمنة. مطلقاً
The Second Observatory - Seventh Section: The denial of future time by the mutakallimun (is impossible, rather it is inconceivable). And Ibn Sina contradicted himself when he said in response to our proof by the burhān al-taṭbīq (argument of application) on the impossibility of the existence of successive events ad infinitum: 'All past motions that are infinite do not exist at all such that application can be conceived in them and they are described by increase and decrease, (otherwise they would be in the past, present, or future, and all is false).' So he ruled there that whatever does not exist in any of the three times does not exist, and he forbade it here, which is a clear contradiction. If you say: There is no contradiction, for that which is not time, like motion, is temporal; if it does not exist in any of the times, it does not exist, unlike time, like the past for example, for it is existent in itself according to us even if it does not exist in the present or future, and it is obvious that it does not exist in the past because of the impossibility of a thing being a container for itself. And its explanation: ... [a section unclear] ... Then know that according to the presentation of container-ness (taqrīr al-ẓarfiyya), this objection is also repelled, because the existence of a thing while it does not exist in the present, past, or future is not impossible. Rather, if that thing is among changing things and does not apply to all time, like motion, then it exists in all time and does not exist in any of the times absolutely.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's denial of future time and Ibn Sina's apparent self-contradiction. It begins with the claim that future time is impossible, then quotes Ibn Sina's response to the burhān al-taṭbīq (argument of application) in which he said that infinite past motions do not exist as a collected whole. The author notes that Ibn Sina ruled that what does not exist in any of the three times (past, present, future) is non-existent, but here he denies future time, leading to contradiction. An objection is raised: there is no contradiction because what is not time (e.g., motion) is temporal; if it does not exist in any time, it is non-existent, whereas time itself (e.g., the past) exists in itself even if not in the present or future. The objection explains that time cannot exist in the past because a thing cannot contain itself. The commentary then clarifies that on the interpretation of 'container-ness' (ẓarfiyya), the objection is repelled: a changing thing like motion exists in all time without existing in any specific temporal part.

Key Terms
زمن
متكلمون
برهان التطبيق
ظرفية
متعذر
غير متصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that for something to be real, it must exist in the past, present, or future.
Correction: The passage clarifies that time itself (e.g., the past) exists in its own right and not necessarily within those divisions. Also, changing things like motion can exist in all time without being concentrated in a specific temporal instance.

Checkpoint
What is the apparent contradiction in Ibn Sina's position according to this passage?
A) He denied future time but affirmed the existence of past motion.
B) He ruled that what does not exist in any of the three times is non-existent, yet he allowed future time to be non-existent.
C) He used the burhān al-taṭbīq to prove the existence of motion.
D) He claimed time is self-subsistent but motion is not.

Key Takeaway
The denial of future time by the mutakallimun leads to a charge of contradiction against Ibn Sina, which is addressed by distinguishing the existence of time itself from the existence of temporal events, and then refined by the concept of container-ness.

Will you search your current beliefs for such hidden contradictions this week?

The mutakallimun claim future time is 'inconceivable.' But the lesson proceeds to analyze it rationally. Resolving Ibn Sina's Contradiction as a Verbal Dispute
Resolves Ibn Sina's contradiction by clarifying collective existence and time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the alleged contradiction in Ibn Sina's statements about past motions is resolved by distinguishing collective existence from individual existence, and why it is ultimately a verbal dispute.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's 'contradiction' vanishes when we clarify what 'existence in time' means.

This lesson addresses a common charge of inconsistency against Ibn Sina concerning the existence of past motions. The commentator argues that the apparent contradiction arises from a misunderstanding of Ibn Sina's intended meaning. By carefully distinguishing between collective and individual existence, and by clarifying the relationship between temporal things and time itself, the contradiction is shown to be merely verbal. The passage walks through the key points of this resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own argument unfold before you interpret.
Notice the dense style typical of kalam commentaries.
2
Identify the initial claim of no contradiction
The commentator first asserts there is no contradiction because 'all past motions do not exist' means they do not exist collectively, so burhan al-tatbiq does not apply. This is the key move.
The burhan al-tatbiq requires coexistence, which is absent here.
3
Examine the objection and response about time
An objection is raised: every temporal thing has a 'when' (past, present, future), unlike time itself. The response denies this, arguing something can exist in all time without existing in any part, analogous to time itself.
This shows that temporal things can exist 'in' time differently than expected.
4
Understand the meaning of 'impossible rather inconceivable'
The commentator clarifies that 'impossible' here refers to actual reality, not conceptual possibility. So the ascending negation (from possible to impossible) is justified when considered in reality.
5
Conclude that the dispute is verbal
The final point: the whole issue arises because Ibn Sina interpreted the people's expression as relying on container-ness. Once clarified, the contradiction disappears—it is a verbal dispute not to be heeded.
This underscores that careful definition resolves apparent conflicts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وقد ناقض إلخ) لا مناقضة في كلامه لأن مراده من قوله جميع الحركات الماضية لا يوجد أن الحركات الماضية مجتمعة لا توجد، فلا يجري فيها برهان التطبيق لاشتراط الاجتماع فيه ولا شك أنَّ الأمور المتغيرة إذا كانت مجتمعة الوجود لا بد أن تكون موجودة إما في الماضي أو في المستقبل أو الحال . قوله: (فإن قلت خلاصته أنَّ كل ما هو زماني فله متى إما الحاضر أو الماضي أو المستقبل بخلاف الزمان ، كما أنَّ كل ما هو مكاني له مكان بخلاف المكان . قوله : (إذا لم يوجد في شيء من الأزمنة إلخ هذا ممنوع إذ يجوز أن يكون موجوداً في كل الزمان، ولا يكون موجوداً في شيء منها بأن يكون متصلاً واحداً منطبقاً عليه منقسماً بانقسامه، فكما أن الزمان واحد موجود في نفسه منقسم بعد التجزئة إلى الأقسام الثلاثة، كذلك الحركة منطبقة عليه يحصل لها الأقسام الثلاثة وليست موجودة في شيء منها . قوله : ( متعذر بل هو غير متصور أراد بالتعذر بحسب التحقيق، وإن كان ممكناً بحسب المفهوم فظهر وجه الترقي بنفي ذلك الإمكان وإن حمل التعذر على التعسر مجازاً فالأمر أظهر . قوله : ( فإن قلت لا مناقضة) حاصل السؤال إنَّ عبارة القوم كانت على وجه حمله ابن سينا على الظرفية فرد عليه وإن كان عبارة المصنف في تقرير الاستدلال صريحاً في المقصود الآني وحاصل الجواب أن مقصودهم أيضاً ما أشار إليه المصنف والمنازعة اللفظية مما لا يلتفت إليها
His statement: '(He contradicted, etc.)' – There is no contradiction in his speech, because his meaning in his statement 'all past motions do not exist' is that past motions collectively do not exist, so the burhān al-taṭbīq does not apply to them because it requires coexistence; and there is no doubt that changing things, if they exist collectively, must exist either in the past, future, or present. His statement: '(If you say, its summary is that every temporal thing has a when, either present, past, or future, unlike time, just as every spatial thing has a place, unlike space).' His statement: '(If it does not exist in any of the times, etc.)' – This is denied, for it is possible that it exists in all time, and not exist in any of them, by being a continuous single thing applied to it, divided by its division. Just as time is one, existing in itself, divided after division into three parts, likewise motion is applied to it, acquiring three parts, and it does not exist in any of them. His statement: '(Impossible, rather it is inconceivable)' – He meant impossibility according to investigation, even if it is possible according to conception; thus the aspect of ascending by negating that possibility appears, and if impossibility is interpreted metaphorically as difficulty, the matter is more evident. His statement: '(If you say, no contradiction)' – The gist of the question is that the expression of the people was such that Ibn Sina interpreted it as container-ness, so he responded accordingly, although the wording of the author in presenting the argument was explicit about the intended meaning. And the gist of the answer is that their intention too is what the author indicated, and verbal disputes are of no consequence.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage resolves the apparent contradiction in Ibn Sina's statements by clarifying his intended meaning. It first states that there is no contradiction because when Ibn Sina said 'all past motions do not exist', he meant they do not exist collectively, so the burhān al-taṭbīq (which requires coexistence) does not apply. Changing things existing collectively must be in one of the three times. Then it addresses an objection that every temporal thing has a 'when' (past, present, or future), unlike time itself. The response denies this, arguing that something can exist in all time without existing in any specific part, just as time itself is one and divided only conceptfully into three parts, and motion applied to time also acquires those parts without being in any one part. The passage then explains the phrase 'impossible, rather inconceivable': the impossibility is in actual reality, though it is possible in concept, so the ascending negation of possibility is justified. Finally, it concludes that the whole dispute is verbal: Ibn Sina interpreted the argument as relying on container-ness, and once this is clarified, the contradiction vanishes—it is merely a verbal dispute not to be heeded.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ibn Sina literally contradicted himself by denying the existence of past motions.
Correction: Ibn Sina denied the collective existence of past motions, not each individual motion. This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is there no contradiction in Ibn Sina's statements?
A) He never denied future time.
B) He meant that past motions do not exist collectively, not that each individual motion is non-existent.
C) He admitted that time is self-subsistent and cannot be denied.
D) The burhān al-taṭbīq is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The contradiction attributed to Ibn Sina is resolved by clarifying that 'existence in time' can mean existing throughout time without being in any part, making the dispute merely verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely misinterpreted someone's words before, like Ibn Sina was misinterpreted. When was the last time you accused someone of contradicting themselves without seeking their intended meaning? How can you commit to asking clarifying questions before drawing conclusions?
?
The passage distinguishes between 'existence in all time' and 'existence in a particular part of time'. In your spiritual life, do you view your connection to Allah as a continuous effort spanning your entire life, or as isolated moments of devotion? Which perspective leads to greater consistency, and what will you change today to embody continuity?
?
Verbal disputes, the passage says, are of no consequence. Think of a relationship strained by a misunderstanding over words. What specific step can you take tonight to resolve that verbal dispute and restore harmony, even if the words themselves were ambiguous?

Refuting al-Razi's Objection from Motion
Al-Jurjani responds to al-Razi's objection that motion disproves the denial of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defends the denial of time against al-Razi's objection by analyzing the concept of motion and showing that the objection fails on two counts.

Core Idea
Motion does not refute the denial of time because its existence is either mental or non-coextensive.

Al-Razi argued that if the tripartite division of time into past, present, and future proves time's non-existence, the same logic would apply to motion, which is clearly existent. In this passage, al-Jurjani responds by distinguishing two meanings of 'motion': one as a continuous mental construct (cutting) and another as a non-stationary state (being in the middle). Neither meaning undermines the denial of time.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi objects that the same argument used to deny time (based on the tripartite division) would also deny motion, which is obviously existent. This is presented as a refutation of the proof against time.
The objection is that motion, like time, is divided into past, present, and future, yet motion is real.
2
Examine al-Jurjani's first answer
Al-Jurjani introduces two senses of motion. First, motion as 'cutting' (qaṭ‘) — a continuous thing from start to finish. This is not externally existent; it is only in the imagination.
This sense of motion is similar to time in being non-existent externally.
3
Examine al-Jurjani's second answer
Second, motion as 'being in the middle' (ḥuṣūl fī al-wasaṭ) — a non-stationary state that exists at each point of the distance. However, it is not co-extensive with the distance, so its indivisibility does not imply indivisibility of the distance.
Thus, motion in this sense exists but does not cause the same problem as time.
4
Reflect on the implications
Neither sense of motion refutes the denial of time. The objection fails because it equivocates on the meaning of motion.
This shows the importance of precise definitions in theological arguments.

قوله : (هذه منازعة لفظية) أي منازعة منشؤها اللفظ أعني كلمة في، ولو حذفت من البين اندفع الجواب المذكور ، وليس المراد أنها نزاع في اللفظ دون المعنى كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( إذ المقصود إلخ ) قد عرفت اندفاعه بما حررنا لك من أن هذه الأقسام اعتبارية حاصلة بعد التجزئة فهو موجود في نفسه من غير أن يكون شيئاً منها . قوله : (قلنا : إذا انحصر الأعم إلخ ) هذا إذا كانت تلك العدة أفراداً حقيقية له أما إذا كانت اعتبارية فلا قوله : ( لأن الحركة كالزمان إلخ قد عرفت أن الحركة منطبقة على الزمان موجودة في تمامها إنما ينقسم إلى الحاضر والماضي والمستقبل بعد التجزئة فهي أقسام لها في العقل بعد وجودها في الخارج فلا يلزم من انتفاء أقسامها انتفاؤها. قوله : ( وهو باطل بالدليل الدال لم يقل وأنتم لا تقولون به إذ النقض لا يكون إلزامياً . قوله : ( في عدة أمور التقييد بقوله في عدة أمور بالنظر إلى محل الكلام، وإلا فمطلق الانحصار كاف في الغرض. قوله : ( والإمام الرازي نقضه إلخ) أي في المباحث المشرقية فيه بحث إذ قد مر أنَّ الدليل المذكور إلزامي فلا يتجه النقض ؛ وقد يقال : ليس في المباحث المشرقية حديث الإلزام فالظاهر الحكماء لا يقولون بوجود الزمان فالنقض بالنسبة إليهم قبل النقض بالنسبة إلى قولهم أنَّ بعض وتبطله بدليله وقد أشرنا إلى أنه أيضاً إلزامي .

( والجواب ) عن هذا النقض ( أنَّ الحركة ) كما سيأتي ( تطلق ) بالاشتراك اللفظي تارة ( بمعنى القطع ) وهو الأمر المتصل الذي يعقل للمتحرك فيما بين المبدأ والمنتهى، ولا وجود لها بهذا المعنى لأن المتحرك ما لم يصل إلى المنتهى لم يكن ذلك الأمر المتصل الممتد من المبدأ إلى المنتهى موجوداً . وإذا وصل إليه فقد بطل ذلك المتصل المعقول، فلا يتصور له وجود في الأعيان بل الحركة بمعنى القطع إنما ترتسم في الخيال كما ستطلع عليه (و) تطلق أخرى (بمعنى الحصول في الوسط) وهو حالة منافية للاستقرار يكون بها الجسم أبداً متوسطاً بین المبدأ والمنتهى ولا يكون في حيز واحد ،آنين والحركة بهذا المعنى ( مستمرة من أول المسافة إلى آخرها، وليست منطبقة عليها بل هي موجودة في كل حد من الحدود المفروضة على المسافة، لكنها باستمرارها وعدم استقرار نسبتها إلى حدود المسافة تقتضي ارتسام ذلك الأمر المنطبق عليها في الخيال، فظهر أن لا نقض بالحركة بالمعنى الأول إذ لا وجود لها في الأعيان كالزمان ، ولا بالمعنى الثاني لأنها وإن كانت موجودة إلا أنها غير منطبقة على المسافة، فلا يلزم من عدم انقسامها عدم انقسام المسافة ولا أن يكون

قوله : ( وبمعنى الحصول في الوسط إلخ) في الحركة بمعنى التوسط شبهة وهي أنها تحدث في آن، ففي ذلك الآن لا بد أن يكون الجسم في مكان ما فذلك المكان إما المكان الأول وأنه محال، لأن المكان الأول محل سكون، وإما المكان الثاني وأنه محال أيضاً؛ لأن المكان الثاني لا يحصل الجسم فيه إلا بعد قطع لا يحصل إلا في زمان فيكون مسبوقاً بتوسط فتأمل. قوله : ( تقتضي ارتسام ذلك الأمر (المنطبق) أورد عليه أن الحركة بمعنى القطع لم تكن موجودة فكيف تنطبق على المسافة الموجودة فإن معنى الانطباق التلازم في الانقسام وكيفيته وذلك بعد الوجود وأجيب : بمنع اقتضاء الانطباق وجود أجزاء المنطبقين .
قوله: (إلا أنها غير منطبقة على المسافة قيل عليه : إنها وإن لم تنطبق على المسافة بأسرها، إلا أنها تنطبق دائماً على جزء من أجزائها على التبادل، فيلزم المحذور، فإن أجيب : بأن
Place exists in itself even if it does not exist in any of the places, unlike the place-like (al-makānī), for if it does not exist in a place it is not existent. I said: This is a verbal dispute because the intent is that if time existed, that time would be either the past itself, or the present, or the future, and all are false as you have known. (His words: It does not follow from the falsity of the more specific the falsity of the more general. We said: If the more general is confined to several things each of which is more specific than it, and none of those things exist, then the more general certainly does not exist, for the general has no existence in the external world except within the specific, necessarily.) And Imam al-Razi, after refuting Ibn Sina's answer, objected to it—i.e., he objected to the second proof indicating the non-existence of time—by motion itself, for the argument stands regarding it, because motion, like time, is confined to three divisions: past, present, and future. The past of them is what was present, and the future is what will be present. So if present motion had no existence, motion would not exist. There is no doubt that the present part of it is indivisible because it is non-stationary (ghayr qārra), so motion would be composed of indivisible parts, and the distance would be composed of them, which is false by the proof that negates the part. Therefore it must not exist. But its existence is necessary, attested by the senses, so your proof is refuted by motion. His words: (This is a verbal dispute) i.e., a dispute originating from the word, namely the word 'in'; if it were removed the aforementioned answer would be repelled. It is not meant that it is a dispute over the word without the meaning, as is not hidden. His words: (For the intent, etc.) You have known that it is repelled by what we have clarified for you: that these divisions are mental, produced after division; so it exists in itself without being any of them. His words: (We said: If the general is confined, etc.) This is if those things are real individuals of it; but if they are mental, then no. His words: (Because motion, like time, etc.) You have known that motion is co-extensive with time, existing in its entirety; it is only divided into present, past, and future after division; so they are divisions for it in the mind after its existence in the external world, so it does not follow from the non-existence of its divisions its non-existence. His words: (And it is false by the proof that indicates—) He did not say: and you do not accept it, because the refutation (naqd) is not binding (ilzāmī). His words: (In several things) The restriction 'in several things' is with respect to the place of discussion; otherwise absolute confinement is sufficient for the purpose. His words: (And Imam al-Razi objected to it, etc.) i.e., in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya, there is discussion in it, for it has passed that the mentioned proof is binding (ilzāmī), so the objection is not valid. And it may be said: In al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya there is no mention of binding; evidently the philosophers do not affirm the existence of time, so the objection is against them before the objection against their statement that some... and he refutes it with his proof, and we have indicated that it is also binding. (And the answer) to this objection (is that motion), as will come, (is used) by homonymy, sometimes (in the sense of cutting (al-qaṭ‘))—which is the continuous thing conceived for the moving body between the starting point and the endpoint, and it has no existence in this sense, because as long as the moving body has not reached the endpoint, that continuous thing extended from the starting point to the endpoint does not exist; and when it reaches it, that conceived continuous thing has ceased, so it cannot be conceived as having existence in external entities; rather motion in the sense of cutting is only depicted in the imagination, as you will learn—and it is used another time (in the sense of being in the middle (al-ḥuṣūl fī al-wasaṭ))—which is a state contrary to stability by which the body is always intermediate between the starting point and the endpoint, and it is not in a single location at two moments; and motion in this sense (is continuous from the beginning of the distance to its end, and it is not co-extensive with it; rather it exists in every limit of the limits supposed on the distance; but by its continuity and the instability of its relation to the limits of the distance, it necessitates the depiction of that thing co-extensive with it in the imagination). So it became clear that there is no objection by motion in the first sense, for it has no existence in external entities like time; nor in the second sense, because although it exists, it is not co-extensive with the distance, so it does not follow from its non-division the non-division of the distance, nor that... His words: (So that has ceased, etc.) If he means that it was not existent at the moment of reaching the endpoint, it is conceded; but that does not entail that it was not existent in the time between the starting point and the endpoint. If he means that it was not existent at the moment of reaching nor in the preceding time, it is denied. Moreover, it is refuted by temporal sounds and letters, for it would entail that they do not exist while they are heard; and the secret is that the existence of a non-stationary thing (al-amr ghayr al-qārr) is co-extensive with all time, not existent at its limits. His words: (In the sense of cutting) it is called that because it is a result of the moving body cutting the distance. His words: (It necessitates the depiction, etc.) as in the falling droplet and the moving flame. His words: (And in the sense of being in the middle, etc.) Regarding motion in the sense of intermediacy there is a doubt: that it occurs in an instant, so in that instant the body must be in some place; that place is either the first place—impossible because the first place is a place of rest—or the second place—also impossible because the body does not arrive at the second place except after a cutting that does not occur except in time, so it would be preceded by intermediacy—so reflect. His words: (It necessitates the depiction of that thing (co-extensive).) It was objected that motion in the sense of cutting was not existent, so how can it be co-extensive with the existent distance? For the meaning of co-extension (inṭibāq) is correspondence in division and its modality, and that is after existence. And it was answered: by denying that co-extension necessitates the existence of the parts of the two co-extensive things. His words: (Except that it is not co-extensive with the distance.) It was said against it: Although it is not co-extensive with the whole distance, it is always co-extensive with a part of its parts successively, so the undesirable consequence follows. And if it is answered: by...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The author first presents an argument that time is not existent because if it existed it would have to be past, present, or future—all of which are invalid. Then an objection is raised: the same reasoning would apply to motion, which is clearly existent, thus refuting the proof. The author responds by distinguishing two senses of 'motion': (1) motion as 'cutting' (al-qaṭ‘), which is a continuous thing from start to finish—this does not exist in external reality but only in imagination, like time; (2) motion as 'being in the middle' (al-ḥuṣūl fī al-wasaṭ), which is a non-stationary state existing at each point of the distance but not co-extensive with it, so its indivisibility does not imply indivisibility of the distance. Thus neither sense refutes the denial of time. The commentary then discusses nuances: the divisions are mental, the general exists only in specifics, and objections based on indivisibility are addressed. The answer is that the objection fails because motion in the first sense is unreal, and in the second it does not imply the same problem.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since motion is clearly existent and has the same tripartite division as time, the argument against time must be invalid.
Correction: The answer shows that motion has two meanings, and neither undermines the denial of time. The first sense (cutting) is not externally existent; the second sense (being in the middle) is existent but not co-extensive, so the indivisibility argument does not apply.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to al-Razi's objection, why does motion not refute the denial of time?
A) Motion does not exist at all.
B) Motion in the sense of 'cutting' does not exist externally, and motion in the sense of 'being in the middle' is not co-extensive with the distance.
C) Motion is indivisible just like time, so it supports the denial.
D) The objection is invalid because it uses a different definition of time.

Key Takeaway
Motion does not undermine the denial of time because the first sense of motion (cutting) has no external existence, and the second sense (being in the middle) is not co-extensive with the distance, thus avoiding the problem of indivisibility.

Time and the Instant: The Philosophers' Refutation of Indivisible Parts
Refutation of time as composite of instants; present as boundary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that time is not composed of indivisible instants and that the present is merely a boundary between past and future.

Core Idea
Time is not an aggregate of instants; the present is just a dividing line.

The mutakallimun argued that if time is continuous, it must be made of indivisible instants—much like atoms in space. The philosophers refute this by drawing an analogy with continuous motion and by redefining the present as a common boundary, not a part of time. In this lesson, we follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the analogy with motion
Read the opening argument: just as divisible motion does not require its parts to coincide, so indivisible motion (the instant) does not require parts of divisible time to gather. Ask yourself why this analogy works.
2
Redefine the present as a boundary
Notice the claim that the present is not a part of time but a common boundary between past and future, like a point on a line. Consider why this undermines composition.
This is a key move: the present is not a part, so time can't be made of instants.
3
See how the second aspect is repelled
The commentary explains that the argument against composition relies on the present being a part. Since it's only a boundary, the second aspect (that time is composed of instants) is refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الآن لا تتجزأ فكما أنه ليس يلزم من استمرار الحركة السيالة التي تنقسم أن تجتمع الأجزاء المفروضة في الحركة الممتدة بعضها مع بعض كذلك لا يلزم من استمرار الذي لا ينقسم أعني مقدار الحركة الغير المنقسمة أن تجتمع الأجزاء المفروضة في الزمان المنقسم الذي هو مقدار الحركة المنقسمة فمن أين يلزم أن يوجد زمان الطوفان في الآن . ولو وجب ذلك لوجب أن توجد الحركة في أول المسافة مع الحركة في آخرها ثم إِنَّ هاهنا بحثاً آخر وهو أن الزمان عند الحكماء إما ماض وإما مستقبل فليس عندهم زمان هو حاضر، بل الحاضر هو الموهوم الذي هو حد مشترك بينهما بمنزلة النقطة المفروضة على الخط وليس جزءاً من الزمان أصلاً لما أنَّ الحدود المشتركة بين أجزاء الكم المتصل مخالفة لها في الحقيقة فلا يصح حينئذ أنَّ الزمان الماضي ما كان حاضراً والمستقبل ما سيحضر، فكما أنه لا يمكن أن يفرض في خط واحد نقطتان متلاقيتان بحيث لا تنطبق إحداهما على الأخرى فكذلك لا يمكن أن يفرض في الزمان آنان متلاقيان، كذلك فلا يكون الزمان مركباً من آنات متتالية ولا الحركة مركبة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ فيندفع حينئذ الوجه الثاني. عرفت من قوله : ( كذلك لا يلزم إلخ فيه أنَّ مقصود المصنف أنه يلزم أن يكون زمان الطوفان عين الزمان الحاضر كما أنَّ الحركة الشخصية من أول المسافة إلى آخرها واحدة، والبديهة تكذبه وليس مقصوده أنه يلزم اجتماع زمان الطوفان مع الآن . قوله : ( لوجب أن توجد الحركة إلخ) فيه أن اللازم أن تكون الحركة الموجودة . المسافة موجودة في آخرها وهو حق فإن الحركة الشخصية باقية في جميع الحدود ما لم يطراً عليها السكون . قوله : (إما ماض وإما مستقبل ) أي بعد التجزئة . أول قوله : ( فيندفع حينئذ الوجه الثاني) لأن مبناه كون الحاضر جزءاً من الزمان وذلك إنما يصح على مذهب أصحاب الجزء . من الزمان عند الحكماء، هو الآن السيال مخالف لما نقل في الكتب من مذهبهم من أنَّ الزمان الماضي الموجود عندهم كم متصل غير قار الذات . قوله : ( فلا يصح حينئذ أنَّ الزمان الماضي ما كان حاضراً إلخ) فإن قلت : هذا لا يشفي لأن فيه ثبوت أصل مدعى المستدل أعني عدمية الزمان لأن الماضي معدوم قطعاً، وكذا فالمستقبل، فلو لم يكن الحاضر زماناً موجوداً لم يوجد الزمان أصلاً قلت لما ثبت أنَّ الموجود عند هو الآن السيال فالمستدل أن نفي وجوده فلا يتم دليله وأن نفي وجود الأمر الممتد فلا خلاف فيه حينئذ
The instant is not divisible. Just as it does not necessarily follow from the continuity of flowing motion that is divisible that the supposed parts in the extended motion come together with each other, likewise it does not necessarily follow from the continuity of what is not divisible—i.e., the measure of non-divisible motion—that the supposed parts in the divisible time, which is the measure of divisible motion, come together. So from where does it follow that the time of the flood exists in the instant? And if that were necessary, then it would be necessary that motion at the beginning of the distance exist with motion at its end. Then here there is another discussion: that time, according to the philosophers, is either past or future, so there is no present time according to them; rather, the present is the imagined thing that is a common boundary between them, like a point supposed on a line, and it is not a part of time at all, because the common boundaries between parts of continuous quantity are different from them in reality. So then it is not correct that the past time was present and the future will be present. Just as it is not possible to suppose two points meeting in one line such that one does not coincide with the other, likewise it is not possible to suppose two instants meeting in time. Thus time is not composed of successive instants, nor motion composed of indivisible parts, so then the second aspect is repelled. You have known from his statement, 'Likewise it does not follow, etc.,' that the author's intention is that it would follow that the time of the flood is the very present time, just as the individual motion from the beginning of the distance to its end is one, and intuition denies it, and his intention is not that it would follow that the time of the flood coincides with the instant. His statement, 'If that were necessary, then motion would have to exist, etc.,' contains that the necessary thing is that the existing motion at the distance exists at its end, and that is true, for the individual motion remains in all boundaries as long as rest does not occur to it. His statement, 'Either past or future,' i.e., after division. The beginning of his statement, 'So then the second aspect is repelled,' because its basis is that the present is a part of time, and that is only valid according to the school of the partisans of the part. Time according to the philosophers is the flowing instant, contrary to what is transmitted in the books from their school that the past time existing according to them is a continuous quantity with non-stable essence. His statement, 'So then it is not correct that the past time was present, etc.,' For if you say: This does not suffice, because it establishes the very claim of the arguer, namely the non-existence of time, because the past is certainly non-existent, and likewise the future, so if the present were not existing time, time would not exist at all. I say: Since it is established that the existent according to them is the flowing instant, the arguer intends the negation of its existence, so his proof is not complete, and the negation of the existence of the extended thing is not disputed then.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the mutakallimun's view that time is composed of indivisible instants. The argument begins by likening motion and time: just as a continuous motion does not require its parts to coincide, the indivisible measure of motion (instant) does not imply that parts of divisible time gather. The author then raises another discussion: philosophers hold that time is only past or future; the present is not a part but a common boundary, like a point on a line. Therefore, time cannot be composed of successive instants, and motion cannot be composed of indivisible parts. The commentary then clarifies the author's intent: the flood’s time would have to be the same as the present, which intuition rejects. It also explains that the necessity of motion at both ends is true because motion persists. The argument that repels the second aspect is based on the present being a part, which only holds for atomists. The commentator defends the philosophers' view that time is a flowing instant, and addresses an objection about the non-existence of time: the arguer’s proof fails because the existence of the flowing instant is established.

Key Terms
آن
زمان
حاضر
ماض
مستقبل
حد مشترك
كم متصل
حركة سيالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that the present moment is a part of time, like a slice of a timeline.
Correction: The philosophers argue that the present is merely a common boundary (ḥadd mushtarak) between past and future, analogous to a point on a line, and thus not a part of time.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers as presented in this passage, what is the present time?
A) A part of time that is currently existing
B) A common boundary between past and future, like a point on a line
C) An indivisible instant that is a component of time
D) A flowing motion that measures duration

Key Takeaway
Time is not composed of indivisible instants; the present is a boundary, not a part, so the second argument for composition fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the present is only a boundary, not a part, then your entire life is either past or future. What part of your past are you still clinging to as if it were present? And what future moment are you so certain of that you forget it may never arrive? Will you start living as if only the boundary matters between what you have done and what you will do?
?
The philosophers argue that the present is not a stretch of time but a fleeting dividing line. When you pray or read Quran, do you experience the moment as something that slips away, or do you imagine you can hold onto it? How would your worship change if you truly accepted that the present is not a container you can fill but a boundary you constantly cross?
?
You probably believe that time is made of moments that you can count. This lesson tells you that the instant is indivisible and not a part. If you cannot possess even a single instant, what do you actually own of your life? What would you do today if you knew that every 'now' is gone before you can grasp it?
