The Argument That Extension Is Imaginary
The commentator gives the argument that extension is imaginary because it applies to non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun argue that quantitative extension (al-imtidad) is imaginary because it can be predicated of non-existent intervals.

Core Idea
Extension is imaginary because it is attributed to pure non-existents.

The commentator presents a classical argument: if something can be predicated of non-existent subjects, it cannot be externally real. He applies this to the extension between historical events, which is a quantity that admits increase and decrease.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument passage
Carefully read the Arabic and translation of the argument. Notice how the commentator uses an example comparing intervals between historical events.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument has a premise: possibilities that admit increase/decrease are hypothesized in pure non-existents. Find the example and the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that extension cannot be externally existent. Ask yourself: why does predication on non-existents imply unreality?
This is a key move in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد السابع : إنكار المتكلمين للزمن عرفته من الله الحركة الوهمية ( وهمية ) بلا شبهة لاستحالة قيام الموجود بالموهوم (ولأنها ) أعني هذه الإمكانات القابلة للزيادة والنقصان تنفرض فى (الأعدام الصرفة ( فإن ما بين يوم الطوفان ومحمد له أكثر مما بين بعثة موسى وبعثة محمد عليهما السلام) ولا شك أنَّ ما يمكن عروضه لأمور معدومة، لا يكون موجوداً خارجياً، ثم التحقيق ما قد أن الحركة بمعنى القطع والزمان الذي هو مقدارها لا وجود لهما في الخارج، بل هما إنما يرتسمان في الخيال لكن ليس ارتسامهما فيه من أمر معدوم بالضرورة، بل من أمرين موجودين في الخارج، لأنا نعلم أنَّ ذلك الامتداد المرتسم الخيال بحيث لو فرض وجوده في الخارج وفرض فيه أجزاء لامتنع اجتماعها معاً، بل كان بعضها متقدما على بعض، ولا يكون الامتداد العقلي كذلك، إلا إذا كان في الخارج شيء مستمر غير مستقر يحصل في العقل بحسب استمراره وعدم استقراره ذلك الامتداد، ولما كان هذان الامتدادان الخياليان ظاهرين في بادئ الرأي ودالين على قوله : (تنفرض في الأعدام أي يعرض للأعدام كما يدل عليه قول الشارح قدس سره، ولا في شك أنّ ما يمكن عروضه إلخ إلا أنَّ عروضه لها لما كان فرضياً قال : تنفرض في الأعدام . قوله : ( فإن ما بين إلخ ) أي الإمكانات التي بين الطوفان ومحمد الله . أكثر من الإمكانات التي بين البعثتين ولا شك أنَّ معروضاتها معدومات صرفة إذ لا وجود لها في الخارج ولا في الذهن لعدم استحضارها مفصلة، حتى يحكم بينهما بالقلة والكثرة وفيه أنها ليست معدومات صرفة لكونها موجودات في أوقاتها . قوله : ( إن ما يمكن (عروضه هذا إنما يفيد لو كان عروضه للأعدام بالذات أما إذا كان يتبع الحركات فلا كما لا يخفى . قوله : (بل) من أمرين موجودين كون ارتسام امتداد الزمان من أمر موجود سوى الحركة بمعنى التوسط مما لا دليل عليه كما مر . قوله : ( ولأنها أعني هذه الإمكانات إلخ هذه الواو من الشرح لا من المتن كما يدل عليه النظر في نسخ المتن، فكان غرض الشارح الإشارة إلى ما هو حق العبارة، لأن الفاء التفريعي قوله : فهذه الإمكانات وهمية دالة على أن التعليل مستفاد من السابق فينبغي أن يجعل قوله : لأنها تنفرض إلخ معطوفاً على التعليل المقدر المستفاد من السابق، وهو الذي ذكره الشارح بقوله : لاستحالة قيام الموجود بالموهوم، وإن وجد الواو في بعض نسخ المتن فالأمر أظهر. قوله : ( ولا شك أنَّ ما يمكن عروضه (إلخ لفظة ما عبارة عن الإمكان المذكور أعني الممتد والأمور المعدومة عبارة عما بين الطوفان ومحمد عليه السلام وما بين بعثة موسى ومحمد عليهما السلام ونحوهما، والعروض عبارة عن الحمل فإنَّ الأكثر المحمول على المابين في الأول والأقل المحمول عليه في الثاني عبارة عن الامتداد فافهم.
The second observation - seventh section: The denial of time by the mutakallimun. You have known from God that the imaginary movement (is imaginary) without doubt because of the impossibility of the existent subsisting in the imaginary. (And because these possibilities—I mean these possible things that admit increase and decrease—are hypothesized in pure non-existents. For what is between the day of the Flood and Muhammad is more than what is between the mission of Moses and the mission of Muhammad (peace be upon them). And there is no doubt that what can be predicated of non-existent things cannot be externally existent. Then the investigation is that movement in the sense of cutting and time which is its measure have no external existence; rather they are only depicted in the imagination, but their depiction therein is not from a non-existent thing necessarily, but from two existing things in the external world. For we know that that depicted extension in the imagination is such that if its existence were supposed externally and parts were supposed in it, they could not coexist together; rather some would be prior to others. And the mental extension is not like that unless there is in the external world something continuous and non-stationary, by whose continuity and non-stationarity that extension obtains in the mind. And since these two imaginary extensions are apparent at first glance and indicate His saying: (they are hypothesized in non-existents), i.e., they occur to non-existents as indicated by the commentator's saying. And there is no doubt that what can be predicated etc., except that its occurrence to them is hypothetical, so he said: 'they are hypothesized in non-existents.' His saying: (for what is between, etc.), i.e., the possibilities between the Flood and Muhammad are more than the possibilities between the two missions, and there is no doubt that their subjects are pure non-existents, since they have no existence in the external world nor in the mind because they are not recalled in detail, such that one judges between them by few and many. And in it they are not pure non-existents because they are existents in their times. His saying: (what can be predicated) this is only useful if its predication of non-existents is essential; but if it follows movements, then no, as is not hidden. His saying: (rather) from two existing things—that the depiction of the extension of time is from an existent thing other than movement in the sense of traversing is not something for which there is evidence, as has passed. His saying: (and because these possibilities, etc.) This waw is from the commentary, not from the text, as examination of copies of the text indicates. The commentator's purpose was to indicate what is the correct expression, because the fa of 'so these possibilities are imaginary' indicates that the explanation is derived from what precedes, so his saying 'because they are hypothesized' should be taken as conjoined to the estimated explanation derived from what precedes, which the commentator mentioned in his saying: 'because of the impossibility of the existent subsisting in the imaginary.' And if the waw appears in some copies of the text, the matter is more apparent. His saying: (and there is no doubt that what can be predicated, etc.) The word 'ma' refers to the mentioned possibility, i.e., the extended thing, and 'the non-existent things' refers to what is between the Flood and Muhammad (peace be upon him) and what is between the mission of Moses and Muhammad (peace be upon them) and the like; and 'predication' means attribution, for the greater attributed to the between in the first and the lesser attributed to it in the second is the extension, so understand.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents and refines the argument that the quantitative extension (al-imtidād) is imaginary. The argument states: possibilities that admit increase and decrease are hypothesized in pure non-existents, such as the interval between the Flood and Muhammad being greater than between two prophetic missions. Since these subjects are non-existent, the extension predicated of them cannot be externally existent. The commentator then clarifies the real position: movement-as-cut and time are only depicted in the imagination, but this depiction stems from two existing things (the moving body and the distance). He further explains that the extension in the mind has the property that if it were external, its parts could not coexist, indicating it is mental. The commentary then dissects the phrasing, noting that the word 'liannahā' (because they) belongs to the commentary, not the original text, and explains the correct reading. It also addresses an objection that the subjects are not pure non-existents because they are existent at their times, and notes that the predication is hypothetical.

Key Terms
الامتداد
الأعدام الصرفة
العروض
الزيادة والنقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that because we can imagine a longer interval between two past events, the extension must be real.
Correction: The argument shows that the interval is not an existing thing; it is a mental comparison. Extension predicated of non-existents cannot be externally real.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator conclude that extension (al-imtidad) is not externally existent?
A) Because it is a continuous quantity
B) Because it is predicated of pure non-existents
C) Because it is identical with speed
D) Because it is a category of substance

Key Takeaway
Extension is imaginary because it is predicated of pure non-existents; what applies to non-existents cannot be externally real.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the extension between events in history is imaginary, how does that affect your perception of time and your place in Allah's plan? What action should you take now to ground yourself in what is real?
?
You often measure your spiritual progress by the distance between where you are and where you want to be. If that distance is imaginary, what becomes the true measure of your faith?
?
The passage shows that what applies to non-existents cannot be externally real. What are you attaching importance to that might be merely imaginary? How can you redirect that attachment to what is truly existent?

Time and Movement: Mental but Rooted in Existence
The commentator clarifies that time and movement are only mental but their depiction originates from existing things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's position that time and movement-as-cut are imaginary yet their mental depiction arises from two existing things in the external world.

Core Idea
Time and movement are mental constructs grounded in external reality.

After establishing that extension is imaginary, the commentator clarifies that time and movement do not exist externally, but their mental images come from real things: the moving body and the distance traversed. This nuanced view prevents misunderstandings.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarification passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the commentator's clarification. He asserts two main points: no external existence, but depiction from two existing things.
2
Understand the 'two existing things'
What are these two things? The commentator refers to 'two existing things in the external world' that give rise to the mental depiction. Typically, these are the moving body and the path.
3
Contrast mental and external extension
The commentator describes a property of the mental extension: if it were external, its parts would not coexist; they would be sequential. This shows it is a mental construct.
This reinforces its imaginary nature.

Source Text

معدومات صرفة إذ لا وجود لها في الخارج ولا في الذهن لعدم استحضارها مفصلة، حتى يحكم بينهما بالقلة والكثرة وفيه أنها ليست معدومات صرفة لكونها موجودات في أوقاتها . قوله : ( إن ما يمكن (عروضه هذا إنما يفيد لو كان عروضه للأعدام بالذات أما إذا كان يتبع الحركات فلا كما لا يخفى . قوله : (بل) من أمرين موجودين كون ارتسام امتداد الزمان من أمر موجود سوى الحركة بمعنى التوسط مما لا دليل عليه كما مر . قوله : ( ولأنها أعني هذه الإمكانات إلخ هذه الواو من الشرح لا من المتن كما يدل عليه النظر في نسخ المتن، فكان غرض الشارح الإشارة إلى ما هو حق العبارة، لأن الفاء التفريعي قوله : فهذه الإمكانات وهمية دالة على أن التعليل مستفاد من السابق فينبغي أن يجعل قوله : لأنها تنفرض إلخ معطوفاً على التعليل المقدر المستفاد من السابق، وهو الذي ذكره الشارح بقوله : لاستحالة قيام الموجود بالموهوم، وإن وجد الواو في بعض نسخ المتن فالأمر أظهر. قوله : ( ولا شك أنَّ ما يمكن عروضه (إلخ لفظة ما عبارة عن الإمكان المذكور أعني الممتد والأمور المعدومة عبارة عما بين الطوفان ومحمد عليه السلام وما بين بعثة موسى ومحمد عليهما السلام ونحوهما، والعروض عبارة عن الحمل فإنَّ الأكثر المحمول على المابين في الأول والأقل المحمول عليه في الثاني عبارة عن الامتداد فافهم.
The second observation - seventh section: The denial of time by the mutakallimun. You have known from God that the imaginary movement (is imaginary) without doubt because of the impossibility of the existent subsisting in the imaginary. (And because these possibilities—I mean these possible things that admit increase and decrease—are hypothesized in pure non-existents. For what is between the day of the Flood and Muhammad is more than what is between the mission of Moses and the mission of Muhammad (peace be upon them). And there is no doubt that what can be predicated of non-existent things cannot be externally existent. Then the investigation is that movement in the sense of cutting and time which is its measure have no external existence; rather they are only depicted in the imagination, but their depiction therein is not from a non-existent thing necessarily, but from two existing things in the external world. For we know that that depicted extension in the imagination is such that if its existence were supposed externally and parts were supposed in it, they could not coexist together; rather some would be prior to others. And the mental extension is not like that unless there is in the external world something continuous and non-stationary, by whose continuity and non-stationarity that extension obtains in the mind. And since these two imaginary extensions are apparent at first glance and indicate His saying: (they are hypothesized in non-existents), i.e., they occur to non-existents as indicated by the commentator's saying. And there is no doubt that what can be predicated etc., except that its occurrence to them is hypothetical, so he said: 'they are hypothesized in non-existents.' His saying: (for what is between, etc.), i.e., the possibilities between the Flood and Muhammad are more than the possibilities between the two missions, and there is no doubt that their subjects are pure non-existents, since they have no existence in the external world nor in the mind because they are not recalled in detail, such that one judges between them by few and many. And in it they are not pure non-existents because they are existents in their times. His saying: (what can be predicated) this is only useful if its predication of non-existents is essential; but if it follows movements, then no, as is not hidden. His saying: (rather) from two existing things—that the depiction of the extension of time is from an existent thing other than movement in the sense of traversing is not something for which there is evidence, as has passed. His saying: (and because these possibilities, etc.) This waw is from the commentary, not from the text, as examination of copies of the text indicates. The commentator's purpose was to indicate what is the correct expression, because the fa of 'so these possibilities are imaginary' indicates that the explanation is derived from what precedes, so his saying 'because they are hypothesized' should be taken as conjoined to the estimated explanation derived from what precedes, which the commentator mentioned in his saying: 'because of the impossibility of the existent subsisting in the imaginary.' And if the waw appears in some copies of the text, the matter is more apparent. His saying: (and there is no doubt that what can be predicated, etc.) The word 'ma' refers to the mentioned possibility, i.e., the extended thing, and 'the non-existent things' refers to what is between the Flood and Muhammad (peace be upon him) and what is between the mission of Moses and Muhammad (peace be upon them) and the like; and 'predication' means attribution, for the greater attributed to the between in the first and the lesser attributed to it in the second is the extension, so understand.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents and refines the argument that the quantitative extension (al-imtidād) is imaginary. The argument states: possibilities that admit increase and decrease are hypothesized in pure non-existents, such as the interval between the Flood and Muhammad being greater than between two prophetic missions. Since these subjects are non-existent, the extension predicated of them cannot be externally existent. The commentator then clarifies the real position: movement-as-cut and time are only depicted in the imagination, but this depiction stems from two existing things (the moving body and the distance). He further explains that the extension in the mind has the property that if it were external, its parts could not coexist, indicating it is mental. The commentary then dissects the phrasing, noting that the word 'liannahā' (because they) belongs to the commentary, not the original text, and explains the correct reading. It also addresses an objection that the subjects are not pure non-existents because they are existent at their times, and notes that the predication is hypothetical.

Key Terms
الحركة بمعنى القطع
الزمان
الخيال
الامتداد العقلي
أمرين موجودين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if time is imaginary, it has no basis in reality at all.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that while time and movement are not externally existent, their mental depiction arises from two real things: the moving body and the distance. So they are grounded in reality.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, from what do the mental depictions of time and movement originate?
A) Pure non-existents
B) Two existing things in the external world
C) The imagination alone
D) The soul's own constructions

Key Takeaway
Time and movement are imaginary yet their mental depictions stem from real existing things, grounding them in external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is a mental construct rooted in real change, then every moment of your life is a real movement towards or away from your Creator. What 'existing things' (your actions, intentions, environment) are shaping your mental image of time? Are you using your time as if it is a real measure of your journey to Allah?
?
The passage teaches that mental constructs can have a firm basis in reality. Your own understanding of time may feel abstract, but your deeds are concrete. Examine your daily schedule: do you prioritize actions that leave a lasting impact in the external world (good deeds) over those that merely pass time? Will you commit today to treat every second as a real opportunity for eternal gain?

The Circularity Objection to the Argument from Motion and Its Refutation
Imam al-Razi's circularity objection and the commentator's two-part refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's circularity objection against proving time from motion and how the commentator refutes it by appealing to immediate sensory experience and logical distinction.

Core Idea
What is known by the senses does not need prior proof of time.

Imam al-Razi raises a powerful objection: if the argument for time relies on two motions beginning together, doesn't that togetherness itself require time, creating a vicious circle? In this passage, the commentator responds by pointing out that such simultaneity is directly observable and logically distinguishable from the definition of time. Let's see how he dismantles the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the circularity claim: that proving time requires assuming temporal simultaneity, which presupposes time.
Notice the phrase 'المعية الزمانية' (temporal togetherness).
2
Understand the first response
The commentator calls the denial 'obstinacy' because the simultaneity is empirically known even to children. Consider why this is a powerful counter.
3
Analyze the logical distinction
The commentator distinguishes between proving the existence of time and the concept of time itself. Evaluate why this avoids circularity.
This distinction is key in kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد اعترض الإمام الرازي على هذا الوجه بأنه مبني على إمكان وجود حركتين تبتدئان معاً، وتنتهيان معاً وليست هذه المعية إلا المعية الزمانية التي لا يمكن إثباتها إلا بعد إثبات الزمان فيلزم الدور. قوله : ( بأنه مبني إلخ لا شك في كون هذا المنع مكابرة فإنَّ ابتداء الحركتين وانتهاء هما معاً مما هو واقع يعلمه الصبيان، وإن لم تعلم المعية الزمانية . قوله : (إلا بعد إثبات (الزمان إن أراد بعد إثبات وجود الزمان فممنوع، وإن أراد بعد نفس الزمان فلا نسلم لزوم الدور.
Imam al-Razi objected to this argument, saying it is based on the possibility of two motions beginning together and ending together, and this togetherness is nothing but temporal togetherness, which cannot be proven except after establishing time, leading to circularity. He said: (that it is based etc.) There is no doubt that this denial is obstinacy, for the beginning of two motions and their ending together is something actual known by children, even if temporal togetherness is not known. He said: (except after establishing time) If he means after establishing the existence of time, it is denied; if he means after time itself, we do not concede the circularity.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's objection and the commentator's response. The objection is that proving time by the argument from motion requires assuming two motions that start and end together, but that togetherness is temporal, thus presupposing time and causing circularity. The commentator responds that this denial is obstinate because such simultaneity is known by sense experience, even to children, without needing to know the definition of time. He further clarifies that if the objector demands prior proof of time's existence, that is denied; if he demands time itself, circularity does not follow.

Key Terms
المعية الزمانية
الدور
المكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any argument that assumes something similar to what it aims to prove is circular and invalid.
Correction: If the assumed premise is directly known by the senses (like simultaneity), it does not require prior proof, and no vicious circle occurs.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator describe al-Razi's objection as 'obstinacy'?
A) Because al-Razi ignored the definition of time
B) Because the simultaneity of two motions is empirically known even to children
C) Because al-Razi assumed motions of different speeds
D) Because time is undefinable

Key Takeaway
Direct sensory knowledge does not require prior proof of time, so the circularity objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a clear truth because you told yourself it needed a proof you hadn't worked out, while your own experience already confirmed it? Where in your life are you doing that right now? Commit to accepting what is evident even before you fully reason it.
?
The commentator calls al-Razi's objection 'obstinacy'. Is there a point in your faith where you have hidden behind intellectual objections to avoid submitting to something that is already plain? Will you today lay down that resistance and embrace the truth your heart already knows?
?
If children can know two motions begin together without needing a theory of time, what does that teach you about the simplicity of knowing God? Are you complicating what is meant to be simple? What step will you take to return to childlike certainty?

Refuting Circularity from Speed and Slowness
Objection and response regarding speed and slowness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that proving time requires two motions of different speeds, but speed itself presupposes time, and how an Ash‘ari commentator resolves this by appealing to sensory perception.

Core Idea
Speed and slowness are known by sense, not by time, breaking the circularity.

Imam al-Razi raised a second objection: if we need two motions of different speeds to prove time, we first need time to define speed. The commentator meets this challenge directly by showing that speed and slowness are immediately perceptible through the senses, not dependent on a prior concept of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Notice how the objection is presented as a conditional: if speed and slowness cannot be proven without time, then circularity follows.
The passage includes the objection and the beginning of the response.
2
Identify the objection's structure
The objection states that speed and slowness are defined by either time (if distance same) or distance (if time same). Hence, they require prior knowledge of time, creating circularity with the argument from motion.
3
Understand the response
The commentator counters that speed and slowness are perceived by the intellect through sense perception. This sensory grounding does not require a definition of time, so the circularity does not arise.
The response is short but effective: it denies the premise that speed and slowness require time to be conceptualized.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this response is sufficient for the Ash‘ari position. It shows that empirical concepts can be used without full theoretical definition, preserving the argument from motion.
This connects to broader themes in kalam about the role of sensory evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً هو مبني على صحة وجود حركتين إحداهما أسرع والأخرى أبطأ ولا يمكن إثبات السرعة والبطء ولا تعقلهما إلا بعد إثبات الزمان و تعقله فيلزم دور آخر. قوله : ( ولا) يمكن إثبات السرعة والبطء إلخ) فإنهما يجتمعان إما باختلاف الزمان عند اتحاد المسافة أو باختلاف المسافة عند اتحاد الزمان . قوله: (فیلزم دور آخر لا يخفى أنَّ السرعة والبطء مما يناله العقل بواسطة الحس، وهو كاف لنا في ذلك التصوير.
Also, it is based on the validity of the existence of two motions, one faster and the other slower, and speed and slowness cannot be proven nor conceived except after establishing time and conceiving it, leading to another circularity. He said: (Nor can speed and slowness be proven etc.) For they are combined either by difference in time when the distance is equal, or by difference in distance when time is equal. He said: (Thus another circularity follows) It is not hidden that speed and slowness are attained by the intellect through the senses, and that is sufficient for us in that conceptualization.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its response. The objection states that the argument requires two motions of different speeds, but speed and slowness presuppose time, causing circularity. The commentator responds that speed and slowness are known by the intellect via the senses, not requiring prior conceptualization of time. He explains that speed and slowness are defined by the relationship between time and distance, but sensory experience suffices to grasp them without a precise definition of time.

Key Terms

البطء
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Speed and slowness are purely temporal concepts requiring a definition of time before they can be understood.
Correction: The commentator shows that speed and slowness are directly perceived by the intellect through the senses, so they do not require a prior concept of time.

Checkpoint

B) By proving that time is not needed for speed
C) By denying that two motions of different speeds can exist
D) By redefining speed and slowness as non-temporal

Key Takeaway
The commentator defends the argument from motion by grounding speed and slowness in sensory perception, avoiding circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own certainty about the existence of time, do you rely more on definitions or on your direct, lived experience of 'before' and 'after'? What would be lost if you could not trust that experience?
?
The commentator grounds speed and slowness in sensory perception, not abstract reasoning. Are there truths about Allah and the unseen that you hold not because of complex arguments but because you feel them directly in your heart? Why do you still doubt them sometimes?
?

Resolving the Contradiction on Past Time
Objection and answer on past time's increase/decrease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that past time's capacity for increase and decrease leads to contradiction is not valid, and how the distinction between general existence and simultaneous existence resolves it.

Core Idea
Increase and decrease require existence in general, not simultaneous coexistence.

This passage presents the third objection from Imam al-Razi and the commentator's response. The objector claims a contradiction: if past time can increase and decrease, it must have a beginning, yet the past never exists all at once. The commentator explains that this is not a contradiction because 'increase and decrease' require only that the past exists in some sense, not that all its parts coexist simultaneously.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the opponent's claim: past time's increase/decrease implies it had a beginning, and the contradiction that the past never existed at any time.
Notice the objector uses the term 'tadbīq' (projection) to argue that increase/decrease requires coexistence.
2
Understand the commentator's key distinction
The commentator denies the contradiction. Find where he says 'existence in general suffices' versus 'coexistence required for tadbīq.' This is the core of the resolution.
The word 'al-jumla' (in general) is crucial.
3
Ask why tadbīq requires coexistence
Reflect on why projection requires simultaneous existence of parts, while increase and decrease do not. This highlights the difference between the two concepts.
4
Apply the distinction to the objection
See how the commentator's distinction shows that the objection conflates two different senses of existence. This resolves the alleged contradiction.
The lesson is about logical precision in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً لما قال الخصم : إِنَّ الزمان الماضي قابل للزيادة والنقصان فيكون له بداية. أجبتم عنه بأن مجموع الماضي لم يوجد في وقت من الأوقات فلا يصح الحكم عليه بقبول الزيادة والنقصان فكيف تحكمون بقبولهما على هذا الإمكان الذي تحاولون إثباته، مع أنه أيضاً لم يوجد في وقت من الأوقات، وهل هذا إلا تناقض؟ قوله: (وهل هذا إلا تناقض لا تناقض، لأنه يكفي لقبولهما الوجود في الجملة بخلاف التطبيق فإنه لا بد فيه من الاجتماع عند الحكيم.
Also, when the opponent said: 'Indeed, past time is subject to increase and decrease, so it has a beginning.' You answered him that the totality of the past did not exist at any time, so judgment of its accepting increase and decrease is not valid. So how do you judge that it accepts them based on this possibility that you are trying to prove, while it also did not exist at any time? Is this not a contradiction? He said: (Is this not a contradiction?) No, it is not a contradiction, because for accepting them, existence in general suffices, unlike the projection [tadbīq] which requires coexistence in the view of the philosopher.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and the commentator's response. The objector argues that if past time is claimed to be capable of increase and decrease, it must have a beginning, leading to a contradiction because the past never exists all together. The commentator replies that this is not a contradiction because 'increase and decrease' only require that the past exists in some general sense, not that all its parts exist simultaneously. The argument about projection (tadbīq) requires coexistence, but that is not needed here.

Key Terms

التطبيق
التناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That increase and decrease logically require the whole past to exist at one time simultaneously.
Correction: The commentator explains that increase and decrease only require existence in general (the past existing as a whole across time), not simultaneous coexistence.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator deny that the objection leads to contradiction?
A) Because past time never exists all together
B) Because increase and decrease require coexistence
C) Because the past is infinite
D) Because the opponent misdefines time

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it conflates two kinds of existence: general existence (enough for increase/decrease) and simultaneous existence (needed for projection/tadbīq).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

How can this lesson's distinction help you become more cautious in judging divine matters?

Objection and Answer on the Existence of Time
The commentator presents an objection of contradiction and the answer regarding the manifest existence of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection of contradiction raised against the possibility of time and the answer that time's manifest existence avoids circularity.

Core Idea
Time's manifest existence proves possibility; no contradiction arises from accepting increase and decrease.

This passage addresses a critical objection: if time is possible and accepts increase and decrease, it must have existed at some point, but it never did — isn't that a contradiction? The commentator presents the answer from the philosophers: time's existence is manifest and known to all, so no circularity occurs. Follow the reasoning carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening lines of the passage identifying the objection: how can time be possible if it never existed? Note the charge of contradiction.
The objector assumes possibility requires actual existence.
2
Examine the answer's core
Identify the response: time's existence is manifest and known to all nations through measurement (days, hours, etc.).
The answer relies on common knowledge.
3
Understand why no contradiction
The passage explains that accepting increase and decrease only requires existence in general, not simultaneous existence, unlike applying a measuring rod.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.
4
Avoid circularity
The answer clarifies that knowledge of time's existence is independent of its essence, so no circularity arises when proving its possibility.
The philosophers' view of time as 'flowing now' supports this.
5
Reflect on the commentary
Note the subtle point about imaginary existence and the disagreement with the author's method. This adds depth to the argument.
This shows the nuance in the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه بقبول الزيادة والنقصان فكيف تحكمون بقبولهما على هذا الإمكان الذي تحاولون إثباته، مع أنه أيضاً لم يوجد في وقت من الأوقات، وهل هذا إلا تناقض؟ ثم أجاب عن الأولين : بأنَّ الزمان ظاهر الوجود والعلم به حاصل فإنَّ الأمم كلهم قدروه بالأيام والساعات والشهور والأعوام والمقصود بيان حقيقته المخصوصة أعني كونه كماً ومقداراً للحركة ولا شك أنَّ العلم بوجود الزمان يكفينا في ثبوت المعية والسرعة والبطء فلا دور.
قوله: (وهل هذا إلا تناقض لا تناقض، لأنه يكفي لقبولهما الوجود في الجملة بخلاف التطبيق فإنه لا بد فيه من الاجتماع عند الحكيم.
قوله : ( ثم أجاب عن (الأولين هذا الجواب على رأي جمهور الفلاسفة، فلا ينافي التحقيق الذي مر أن الوجود هو الآن السيال وخلاصته أنَّ الموقوف عليه وجود الزمان والموقوف بيان حقيقته المخصوصة ووجوده معلوم لكل أحد غير موقوف على العلم بحقيقته فلا دور، وفيه بحث ظاهر إذ ظهور وجوده في حيز المنع والقسمة المذكورة يكفيه الوجود الوهمي . قوله : ( والمقصود بيان حقيقته إلخ) هذا مبني على ما نقله الإمام في المباحث المشرقية عن النجاة من إقامة الدليل المذكور على بيان حقيقته بضم المقدمات التي سيذكرها المصنف بیان مذهب أرسطو لا يتم هذا الجواب على طريقة المصنف، حيث استدل به على وجود الزمان .
Upon it by accepting increase and decrease, so how do you judge their acceptance upon this possibility that you try to prove, while it also did not exist at any time? Is this not a contradiction? Then he answered the first two: that time is manifest in existence and knowledge of it is obtained, for all nations have estimated it by days and hours and months and years, and the intent is to explain its specific essence, i.e., its being a quantity and measure for motion, and there is no doubt that knowledge of the existence of time suffices us in establishing simultaneity, speed, and slowness, so there is no circularity. He said: (And is this not a contradiction?) No contradiction, because acceptance of both requires existence in general, unlike applying the measuring rod, for it necessarily requires gathering according to the philosopher. He said: (Then he answered the first two) This answer is according to the opinion of the majority of philosophers, so it does not contradict the verification that passed that existence is the flowing now, and its summary is that what depends on it is the existence of time and the dependent is the explanation of its specific essence, and its existence is known to everyone, not dependent on knowledge of its essence, so no circularity. And in it is apparent research, for the apparentness of its existence is in the domain of denial and the mentioned division suffices for it imaginary existence. He said: (And the intent is to explain its specific essence, etc.) This is based on what Imam al-Razi transmitted in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya from al-Najat of establishing the mentioned proof for explaining its essence by appending the premises that the author will mention the statement of Aristotle's school, this answer is not complete according to the author's method, where he used it to prove the existence of time.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues: if time is something that accepts increase and decrease (as the philosophers claim), then it must have existed at some point, but it never did; this is a contradiction. The author answers the first two objections by stating that time's existence is manifest and known to all people, who measure it by days, hours, etc. The purpose is to explain its essence (being a quantity of motion), but its existence is known independently, so there is no circularity in using it as a proof. The commentary clarifies that the objection of contradiction is not valid because the acceptance of increase and decrease only requires existence in general, not simultaneous existence of all parts, unlike the application of a measuring rod. The answer about time's manifest existence is based on the philosophers' view (the flowing now) and is sufficient to avoid circularity, though a subtle point remains about imaginary existence. The final comment notes that the argument for explaining time's essence is taken from al-Razi and Aristotle, but it does not fully align with the author's method of proving existence.

Key Terms
زمان (time)
تناقض (contradiction)
وجود (existence)
آن سيال (flowing now)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the objection shows that time cannot be possible because it never existed.
Correction: The answer clarifies that possibility does not require actual existence at every moment; accepting increase and decrease only needs existence in general, not simultaneous existence.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to the first two objections regarding the existence of time?
A) Time does not exist at all.
B) Time's existence is manifest and known to all, so no circularity occurs.
C) Time's essence must be known before proving its existence.
D) The objection is left unanswered.

Key Takeaway
The objection of contradiction fails because time's manifest existence is sufficient for it to be possible, and its essence does not need to be known beforehand.

The passage argues that time's existence is manifest to all nations. But you, personally, have you ever doubted the reality of time when you felt it was 'just passing' or 'going slow'? What would change in your daily worship if you began to see every moment as a real, measurable unit of existence that you cannot reclaim?
?
The objection claims that time's possibility (increase and decrease) and its non-existence lead to contradiction. In your own life, when you procrastinate or delay, are you not implicitly assuming that time is real and that you can 'spend' it later? How does this lesson expose the inconsistency in your actions that treat time as both real and unreal?

Answer to the Third Objection on Increase and Decrease
The author gives the answer to the third objection and discusses a remaining issue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers answer the objection that time's increase and decrease require parts to exist together, and the subtle issue this raises for their other arguments.

Core Idea
Accepting increase and decrease does not demand all parts coexist, but it can undermine other proofs.

This passage presents the answer to the third objection against the reality of time. The objector claims that if time can increase or decrease, it must have all its parts existing simultaneously. The answer uses the example of motion, which has parts that are never all present at once. However, the author notes a remaining problem: if the judgment of increase and decrease does not require existence, then many other arguments that rely on such judgments would be invalidated. The commentary clarifies this delicate point.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer to the objection
Read the first part of the passage which states that something admitting increase and decrease does not need all its parts to exist together. Notice the example of motion from the beginning to the end of a distance.
This is the core of the response.
2
Identify the remaining issue
After the answer, the author flags a problem: if the judgment of increase and decrease does not depend on the existence of the subject, then it would invalidate many of the philosophers' own principles. Find this statement.
3
Understand the commentator's clarification
Read the commentary that follows. It explains that the answer only works if we consider the context of applying the measuring rod, which requires parts to exist together. It then shows that the entailment of invalidation only follows if existence is not required at all, but some existence is necessary.
This resolves the remaining issue partially.

Commentary
This passage gives the answer to the third objection about increase and decrease. The objector claims that if time admits increase and decrease, it must have existed at some point, but it did not. The answer is that a thing that admits increase and decrease does not require all its parts to exist simultaneously—just as motion has parts that are not all present at once. However, the author notes a remaining issue: if the judgment of increase and decrease does not depend on the actual existence of the subject, then many arguments that rely on such judgments (like proofs for place and number) would be undermined. The commentary explains that the answer does not fully remove the contradiction unless the context of 'applying the measuring rod' (which requires simultaneous existence) is considered. The commentator also argues that the entailment of invalidation only follows if existence is not required at all, but some existence (in general) is necessary, so no invalidation occurs.

Priority as an Additional Attribute: The Father-Son Proof
The commentator presents a proof that priority is an additional attribute using the father-son example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why priority must be an attribute additional to the essence, using the example of a father's priority over his son.

Core Idea
Priority is a relational attribute, not the essence itself.

In kalam, attributes are either identical to the essence or superadded. This proof uses the common example of a father and son to show that priority cannot be the essence. The reasoning hinges on the relational nature of priority and its incompatibility with simultaneity, which the essence can accommodate. Let's examine the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then its translation. Notice the clear structure: presentation of the example, argument, and supporting points.
2
Identify the core argument
Focus on the claim: priority is not the father's essence because priority is relational, requiring two terms, while the essence itself does not involve relation.
The father's essence can be considered with or without the son, but priority only appears when both are considered.
3
Understand the non-coexistence proof
The commentator argues that if priority were the essence, it would coexist with simultaneity, but it does not: when the father is simultaneous with the son, priority is absent. Therefore priority is additional.
This is a key logical move: attributes that cannot coexist with their opposite cannot be identical to the essence.
4
Review the commentator's clarifications
Notice how the commentator explains that priority is only realized after the son's existence, and that the proof also shows the father is not taken purely with non-existence.
This deepens your understanding of the relational nature.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude that priority is an additional attribute (amr za'id) separate from the essence. This is a standard position in Ash'ari kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني أن الأب مقدم على الابن ضرورة لأن الأب موجود مع عدم الابن ثم وجد الابن فإذا اعتبر الأب من حيث أنه كان مقارناً لعدمه الذي يعقبه الوجود كان مقدماً عليه، كما أنه إذا اعتبر من حيث أن وجوده مقارن لوجود الابن كان معه وليس ذلك التقدم نفس جوهر الأب لأن التقدم أمر إضافي لا يعقل إلا بين شيئين دون جوهر الأب إذ لا إضافة فيه أصلا ولأن جوهر الأب قد يكون معه أي مع الابن كما صورناه فقد وجد جوهر الأب مع معية الابن ولا شك أن تقدمه على الابن لا يوجد مع معيته له وإليه أشار بقوله : وقيل لا يكون مع أي ما هو متصف بالقبلية، والتقدم لا يكون في تلك الحال متصفاً بالمعية فلا تجامع القبلية المعية كما يجامعها جوهر الأب فتكون القبلية أمراً زائداً على ذاته ولا هو باعتبار عدم الابن معه أي ليس ذلك التقدم عبارة عن أصلا.
قوله : (ثم وجد الابن أشار به إلى أن اتصاف الأب بالتقدم إنما هو بعد وجود الابن إذ الإضافتان توجدان معاً في الشفاء فالمقدم تقدمه أنه له وجود مع عدم شيء آخر لم يكن موجوداً وهو موجود، فهو متقدم عليه إذا اعتبر عدمه وهو معه مه إذا اعتبر وجوده فقط .
قوله : ( نفس جوهر الأب فيكون متقدماً بنفسه لا بتقدم زائد عليه.
قوله : أمراً زائداً على ذاته مفارقاً عنه .
قوله : ( لأن التقدم أمر إضافي هذا الدليل كما يدل على أن التقدم ليس نفس جوهر الأب يدل على أنه ليس الأب مأخوذاً مع عدم الابن سواء اعتبر العدم عدماً مطلقاً أو لاحقاً أو سابقاً لأن المتبادر من قوله : لأن التقدم أمر إضافي أنه إضافي صرف والأب مع عدم الابن ليس إضافياً صرفاً بل هو مشتمل عليه أو مقيد به فتأمل.
قوله : أي ما هو متصف بالقبلية الأظهر في توجيه عبارة المتن المصير إلى حذف المضاف أي قبلية قبل كما ء مثله سيجيء
The second aspect: that the father is necessarily prior to the son, because the father exists with the non-existence of the son, then the son came to exist. So if the father is considered insofar as he was concomitant to his non-existence which existence follows, he is prior to him, as if he is considered insofar as his existence is concomitant to the existence of the son, he is with him. And that priority is not the essence of the father, because priority is a relational matter, not conceivable except between two things, to the exclusion of the essence of the father, for there is no relation in it at all. And because the essence of the father may be with him, i.e., with the son as we depicted, for the essence of the father is found with the simultaneity of the son, and there is no doubt that his priority over the son does not exist with his simultaneity with him. And to this he alluded by his saying: And it is said it is not with whatever is qualified by anteriority, and priority is not in that state qualified by simultaneity, so anteriority and simultaneity do not coexist as the essence of the father coexists, so anteriority is an attribute additional to his essence. And it is not by considering the non-existence of the son with him, i.e., that priority is not an expression of [that] at all. He said: (Then the son came to exist) He alluded by this to the fact that the father's being qualified by priority is only after the existence of the son, for the two relations exist together in al-Shifa'? So the prior's priority is that he has existence with the non-existence of another thing that was not existent while he is existent, so he is prior to him if his non-existence is considered, and he is with him if only his existence is considered. He said: (the essence of the father) so he would be prior by himself, not by an additional priority. He said: an attribute additional to his essence, separate from it. He said: (because priority is a relational matter) This proof indicates that priority is not the essence of the father, and also indicates that the father is not taken with the non-existence of the son, whether the non-existence is considered absolute, subsequent, or prior, because what is understood from his saying 'because priority is a relational matter' is that it is pure relation, and the father with the non-existence of the son is not pure relation, but rather includes it or is restricted by it, so reflect. He said: i.e., what is qualified by anteriority, the most apparent in explaining the phrasing of the text is to assume the elision of the genitive, i.e., prior-like anteriority, as such will come.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that priority (taqaddum) is an attribute additional to the essence, not identical to it. The example: a father is prior to his son. The father exists when the son does not; when the son comes to exist, the father can be considered either as prior (relative to the son's non-existence) or as simultaneous (relative to existence). Priority is not the father's essence because it is a relational matter; the essence itself can be simultaneous with the son. The commentator explains that the father's being qualified by priority occurs only after the son exists (since relation requires two terms). He also clarifies that if priority were the essence, the father would be prior by himself, but he is not. The commentary further notes that this proof also shows that the father is not taken 'with the non-existence of the son' as a pure relation; rather, the phrase involves an implicit qualification. The final remark addresses how to interpret the wording in the original text.

معية (simultaneity)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking the proof is about temporal priority in the physical sense.
Correction: The proof uses the relationship between father and son as an example of logical or essential priority, not necessarily temporal. It is about the metaphysical attribute of priority, not chronological time.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Priority is a relational attribute that cannot be identical to the essence because it can be absent when the essence is present with its opposite (simultaneity).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The father's essence remains the same whether he is prior or simultaneous. What does this say about your own identity—that your 'rank' or 'status' is not your essence? Are you willing to lose all relational attributes for the sake of Allah alone? If so, how will you lower yourself today?
?
You just learned that priority cannot coexist with simultaneity in a single subject. Where in your life do you try to be both prioritized (internally) and connected (with others) in a way that contradicts this metaphysical reality? What will you sacrifice to resolve that contradiction?

Clarifying the Meaning of 'Nor is it by Consideration of Nonexistence with Him'
Grammatical and semantic clarification of a phrase about precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that precedence is not simply the consideration of nonexistence but a separate reality, and how the commentator clarifies the grammar and meaning of the phrase.

Core Idea
Precedence is not just the absence of the son; it is a real attribute.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a potential misunderstanding about the concept of precedence. The original text states that precedence is not the mere consideration of the son's nonexistence with the father. The commentator explains the grammar and emphasizes that precedence is a real attribute, not a relational notion of nonexistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing, then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the phrase 'ولا هو باعتبار عدم الابن معه' and its connection to the preceding sentence.
2
Understand the two grammatical interpretations
The commentator explains that 'لا' can be emphatic negation or act like 'ليس'. Identify both possibilities and see how they affect the meaning.
The choice between the two does not change the overall meaning due to the extra 'باء'.
3
Recognize the role of the extra 'باء'
The 'باء' in 'باعتبار' is considered extra, which simplifies the reading: precedence is not the consideration of nonexistence.
The extra 'باء' emphasizes that precedence is not identical to considering nonexistence.
4
Connect to the broader theological context
Realize that this clarification guards against reducing precedence to a mere relational concept. Precedence is a real attribute of the father, not just the absence of the son.
This aligns with the discussion of divine attributes in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( ولا هو باعتبار إلخ ) عطف على ذلك التقدم وكلمة لا لتأكيد النفي أي ليس ذلك التقدم اعتبار عدم الابن معه ويجوز أن يكون لا بمعنى ليس وهو مع اسمه وخبره معطوف على جملة ليس ذلك التقدم وعلى التقديرين الباء زائدة فيكون المعنى ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره كما هو المقصود بالبيان.
His saying: 'Nor is it by considering, etc.' is a conjunct to that precedence, and the word 'lā' is for emphatic negation, meaning that precedence is not the considering of the son's nonexistence with him. And it is permissible that 'lā' is in the sense of 'laysa', and it with its subject and predicate is a conjunct to the sentence 'that precedence is not the essence of the father'. In both cases, the 'bā' is extra, so the meaning is what the commentator (quddisa sirruhu) mentioned, as is the intended meaning of the exposition.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and semantic clarification of the phrase "ولا هو باعتبار عدم الابن معه" from the original text. The commentator explains that this phrase is conjoined to the previous statement that precedence is not the essence of the father. The word "lā" is used to emphasize negation, meaning that precedence is not the mere consideration of the son’s nonexistence with the father. Alternatively, "lā" could be interpreted as "laysa" (not), and the entire clause is conjoined to the predicate of "not". In both interpretations, the "bā" in "باعتبار" is extra, and the meaning is that precedence is not identical to considering nonexistence. The passage concludes by affirming that this interpretation matches the commentator’s intent.

Correction: This passage clarifies that precedence is not merely the consideration of nonexistence; it is a real attribute that belongs to the father.

Key Takeaway

The Literal Meaning: Nonexistence Can Be Prior or Posterior
Explanation of the literal interpretation and its problematic consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why priority cannot simply be identified with the son's nonexistence, because nonexistence itself can be characterized as prior or posterior.

Core Idea
Nonexistence is not priority; it varies between prior and posterior.

This lesson unpacks the immediate reading of a key phrase in the commentary. The author states that priority and posteriority are among the things that differentiate nonexistence. This raises a puzzle: if nonexistence can be prior, then how can priority be just the consideration of nonexistence? We explore the commentator's initial explanation and the conclusion that priority is not the same as nonexistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice that the commentator begins by giving the apparent meaning of the phrase.
2
Identify the puzzle
The apparent reading suggests that nonexistence (of the son) can be prior or posterior. That would mean priority itself could be described by posteriority — a contradiction. Identify this problem.
3
Follow the resolution
The commentator cites the Imam who says that consideration of existence and nonexistence can be a cause for priority or posteriority. This shows that priority is not identical with nonexistence.
4
Understand the conclusion
The lesson's key takeaway is that priority is a relational notion, not reducible to the nonexistence of the son.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله فالقبلية والبعدية مما يختلف به إلخ ) الظاهر المتبادر من هذه العبارة أن عدم الابن يتصف بهما ويتعدد بهما، فتارة يكون قبل كالعدم السابق وتارة بعد كالعدم اللاحق فلا تكون القبلية نفسه لامتناع اتصاف القبلية بالبعدية وهو المناسب لقوله، وقد يعبر عنه بأن العدم قبل كالعدم بعد يعني أنه في الحالتين على السواء وقد صرح به الشارح قدس سره حيث قال : من العدم يختلف بالقبلية والبعدية وأما ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره من أنه قد يكون موجباً لتقدم الأب وقد يكون موجباً لتأخره فالعبارة اللائقة به مما يختلف بالعدم المعتبر معه فيحتاج إلى أن المراد مما يختلف به أي بإيجابه العدم المعتبر وليت شعري ما الحاجة إلى هذه العناية ولعل قدس سره تابع الإمام في ذلك حيث قال وبالجملة فاعتبار الوجود والعدم قد يكون موجباً للتقدم تارة والتأخر أخرى فعلمنا بهذا أن اعتبار كون الأب متقدماً على الابن ليس هو اعتبار الأب اعتبار وجود وعدم الابن كيف كان انتهى. لكن هذا طريق آخر لبيان مغايرة التقدم لعدم الابن بأن العدم قد يكون موجباً للتأخر كالعدم اللاحق والتقدم لا يكون موجباً للتأخر . اسم قوله : ( أي ليس ذلك التقدم عبارة عن مجرد إلخ ) الظاهر أنه جعل لفظ هو في عبارة المتن لا وراجعاً إلى التقدم وقوله : باعتبار عدم الابن معه أي عدم الابن المعتبر معه على قياس قولهم : العلم حصول الصورة خبر ،لا، ولم يجعل لفظ هو معطوفا على خبر ليس في قوله : وليس ذاك التقدم نفس جوهر الأب ولا لإعادة النفي مع أنه الأنسب لقول المصنف : لأن الأب يعتبر مع العدم إلخ . لأنه هو المطابق لقول : المصنف وبالجملة إلى قوله : فلا تكون نفس العدم على أن هذا الاحتمال قد ظهر بطلانه من قوله فيما سبق لأن التقدم أمر إضافي كما نبهناك عليه وأما انطباق قوله : لأن الأب يعتبر إلخ، فيظهر من قوله : فلا تكون القبلية نفس العدم وإلا كان إلخ فتأمل. قوله : ( فإنَّ العدم المعتبر معه إلخ) كلام المصنف يشعر بأن العدم يختلف بالقبلية والبعدية أعني قد يصير العدم المعتبر مع الأب قبل وقد يصير بعد، فأخرجه الشارح عن ظاهره بان حمله على أن العدم قد يصير سبباً لقبلية الأب، وقد يصير سبباً لبعديته لأن السوق في قبلية الأب وبعديته لا في قبلية العدم وبعديته فقوله به على توجيه الشارح حال من المستتر في يختلف أي ملتبساً به وطريق الالتباس كون العدم موجباً له أو يقال : الباء للتعدية أي يجعله العدم مختلفاً إن جعل مثله قياساً.
His saying: 'For priority and posteriority are among the things by which nonexistence varies, etc.' The apparent and immediate understanding from this expression is that the son's nonexistence is qualified by them and is multiplied by them: sometimes it is prior like anterior nonexistence, sometimes posterior like posterior nonexistence. So priority itself is not something that can be described by posteriority, which is consistent with his saying. It might be expressed by 'nonexistence is prior just as nonexistence is posterior', meaning it is the same in both states. And the commentator (quddisa sirruhu) explicitly stated: 'nonexistence varies by priority and posteriority.' As for what the commentator mentioned that it may be a cause for the father's priority and may be a cause for his posteriority, the appropriate expression is 'varies by the nonexistence considered with him'. So it is necessary that what is meant by 'varies by it' is 'by its causing the considered nonexistence'. I wonder why this effort is needed; perhaps the commentator followed the Imam in that, where he said: 'In sum, consideration of existence and nonexistence may be a cause for priority sometimes and posteriority other times. Thus we know that considering the father as prior to the son is not the consideration of the father with existence and nonexistence of the son however it may be.' But this is another way to show the distinction of precedence from the son's nonexistence: that nonexistence may be a cause for posteriority like posterior nonexistence, while precedence is not a cause for posteriority. ... His saying: (That is, that precedence is not merely, etc.) Apparently he made the word 'huwa' in the original text refer to precedence, and his saying 'by considering the son's nonexistence with him' means the son's nonexistence considered with him, analogous to their saying: 'Knowledge is the form of the object.' He did not make the word 'huwa' conjoined to the predicate of 'laysa' in his saying 'and that precedence is not the very essence of the father', nor to repeat the negation, although that would be more appropriate for the author's saying because the father is considered with nonexistence, etc. ... His saying: (For the considered nonexistence with him, etc.) The author's speech suggests that nonexistence varies by priority and posteriority, meaning that the nonexistence considered with the father can become prior and can become posterior. So the commentator interpreted it against its apparent meaning by understanding that nonexistence can become a cause for the father's priority and a cause for his posteriority because the context is about the father's priority and posteriority, not about the priority and posteriority of nonexistence. So his saying 'by it' according to the commentator's interpretation is a state of the implicit pronoun in 'varies', i.e., accompanied by it, and the way of accompaniment is that nonexistence is a cause for it. Or it is said: the 'bā' is transitive, meaning it makes nonexistence vary, if such an analogy is made.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the phrase 'فالقبلية والبعدية مما يختلف به العدم المعتبر معه'. It begins by interpreting the phrase literally: nonexistence (of the son) can be characterized as prior or posterior, and thus priority itself cannot be identical with nonexistence because priority would then be described by posteriority. The commentator then discusses alternative interpretations: some say nonexistence is prior just as it is posterior. The commentator also addresses a potential issue: does nonexistence cause priority or posteriority? The commentator suggests that the author's words should be understood as nonexistence being a cause for the father's priority or posteriority. Other grammatical possibilities are explored, such as the 'bā' being extra or transitive. The passage concludes with a discussion of the phrase 'فإن العدم المعتبر معه إلخ' and the shift from the literal meaning to a causal interpretation. Overall, the passage aims to show that precedence is not simply the consideration of nonexistence, and that the relation between nonexistence and temporal relations is complex.

Key Terms
قبلية
بعدية
عدم
اعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority is simply the son's nonexistence.
Correction: Nonexistence can be prior or posterior, so it cannot be identical with priority, which is a fixed relation.

Checkpoint
According to the literal interpretation discussed, what problem arises if priority is identified with the son's nonexistence?
A) Nonexistence cannot be qualified as prior or posterior.
B) Priority would then be describable by posteriority, which is impossible.
C) The father would not be prior.
D) Nonexistence would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
Priority is a relational notion distinct from the son's nonexistence, which can vary in temporal characterization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever confuse the absence of something with its priority in your own judgments? When has your assessment of what is prior in your life been shaped by what is absent rather than present?
?
Consider a relationship where you felt someone should be 'prior' to you. Was that priority based on their existence or on something lacking in you? How can this lesson shift your perspective on that relationship?
?
If nonexistence can be prior or posterior, how should that affect your gratitude and patience? Can you think of a blessing that was actually preceded by a nonexistence (a trial) and a trial followed by a nonexistence (a blessing)? How does that change your view of divine wisdom?

The Causal Interpretation: Nonexistence as Cause for Priority or Posteriority
Reinterpretation and grammatical analysis supporting causal reading.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's causal reinterpretation of the phrase and the grammatical analysis that supports it.

Core Idea
Nonexistence causes the father's temporal rank, not that it is the rank itself.

The commentator reinterprets the phrase to avoid the earlier puzzle. Instead of meaning that nonexistence itself varies, he understands it to mean that nonexistence, in its different modalities, causes the father to be considered prior or posterior. This lesson explores that causal shift and the grammatical moves that enable it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the commentator's reinterpretation that nonexistence causes the father's priority/posteriority.
2
Identify the causal claim
The commentator says that the author's words should be understood as 'nonexistence can become a cause for the father's priority or posteriority'. Notice how this avoids the earlier contradiction.
3
Examine the grammatical support
The passage discusses the role of the pronoun 'huwa' and the particle 'bā'. Understanding these grammatical details strengthens the causal interpretation.
4
Review the conclusion
The lesson shows that theological texts often require careful interpretation beyond the literal meaning, using both conceptual and grammatical reasoning.

Priority and Posteriority Are Additional Relations, Not Mere Non-Existence
This passage argues that priority and posteriority are distinct from non-existence and are additional relational attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why priority and posteriority cannot be reduced to non-existence but are additional relational attributes requiring a substrate.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority are real relations, not just non-existence.

This lesson delves into an argument from classical kalam that priority and posteriority are not simply the absence of the other thing. Instead, they are additional relational properties that exist in the real world. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how temporal relationships are understood in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Note the flow of the argument.
The passage uses a reductio ad absurdum to reject the identification with non-existence.
2
Identify the key argument
Find where the author argues that priority cannot be identical to non-existence because otherwise the father would always be prior. Then note the similar argument for posteriority.
The father-son example is crucial.
3
Understand the analogy
The expression 'non-existence before is like non-existence after, but before is not like after' illustrates that non-existence is the same in kind but the relational qualifiers differ.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that priority and posteriority are additional relations requiring a substrate, and that they do not arise from the essences of the things.
This establishes them as extra-mental attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فالقبلية والبعدية مما يختلف به العدم المعتبر معه. فلا تكون القبلية نفس العدم وإلا كان اعتبار العدم مع الأب موجباً لتقدمه أبداً، ولا تكون البعدية أيضاً نفس العدم لمثل ما ذكر. وقد يعبر عنه بأن العدم قبل كالعدم بعد وليس قبل كبعد. بل القبلية والبعدية أمران زائدان على الأمور المذكورة وهما إضافتان فيستدعيان محلا. وقد تبين أن عروض القبلية والبعدية للأب والابن ليس لذاتيهما فلا بد من شيء آخر يتصف بهما لذاته.
And in summary, priority and posteriority are among the things by which the non-existence considered with it varies. So priority is not identical to non-existence, otherwise considering non-existence with the father would necessitate his priority forever, and posteriority is also not identical to non-existence for the like of what was mentioned. And it is sometimes expressed by saying: non-existence before is like non-existence after, but before is not like after. Rather, priority and posteriority are two additional matters beyond the mentioned things, and they are two relations requiring a substrate. And it has become clear that the occurrence of priority and posteriority to the father and son is not due to their essences, so there must be something else that is qualified by them inherently.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is an argument that priority (qabliyya) and posteriority (ba'diyya) are not reducible to non-existence ('adam) but are additional relational attributes. The author begins by stating that non-existence considered relative to the father varies (it can be before or after). Then he argues that priority cannot be identical to non-existence, because if it were, then whenever we consider non-existence with the father, the father would always be prior (since non-existence of the son is always present in some sense), but that is false—the father may be posterior in some respects. Similarly, posteriority cannot be identical to non-existence. He then explains that non-existence before (the son's existence) is like non-existence after (the son's existence) in being non-existence, but the before and after qualifiers differ. The conclusion is that priority and posteriority are two additional matters (amran za'idan) beyond the entities themselves and beyond non-existence; they are relations (idafat) requiring a substrate. Furthermore, the occurrence of priority and posteriority to the father and son is not due to their essences, otherwise they could not be separated—which is false. Therefore, there must be something else (a relation) that inherently qualifies them. The argument proceeds step by step, refuting the reduction of priority to non-existence and establishing it as an extra-mental relation.

Key Terms
قبلية
بعدية
عدم
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think priority is simply the non-existence of the later thing.
Correction: The passage shows that non-existence can be both before and after, so priority (which is always before) cannot be identical to non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is priority not identical to non-existence?
A) Because non-existence is always before existence.
B) Because non-existence can be both before and after, while priority is always before.
C) Because priority requires a substrate, but non-existence does not.
D) Because the father's priority is an essential attribute.

Key Takeaway
Priority and posteriority are real relations, not mere absences, and they require a substrate that is qualified by them inherently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You think of your past as simply 'gone'—mere non-existence. But this lesson teaches that priority (qabliyya) is a real relation, not just absence. How does this challenge your attitude toward your past? What would it mean for you to treat your past as a real relation with a substrate in your present self? Commit to one way you will actively acknowledge your past as a real part of your identity tomorrow.
?
The passage says that priority and posteriority are not due to the essences of things. Consider your own relationships: do you define yourself by being 'before' or 'after' others (e.g., older/younger, earlier/later)? What is the inherent quality in you that grounds these relations? When you meet someone who is 'after' you in status, do you see them through a relation or as a person with their own essence? How will you change that perspective today?
?
The argument uses a reductio: if priority were non-existence, absurdity follows. In your life, have you ever reduced a real relationship (like a past mistake or a future goal) to mere 'nothing'? How does this lesson call you to re-evaluate that? What concrete step can you take this week to acknowledge the reality of that relation and the substrate it requires?
