Priority and Posteriority Are Not Identical to Non-Existence
Al-Jurjani argues that priority and posteriority are not non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why priority and posteriority cannot be identical to non-existence, based on Al-Jurjani's argument.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority are not the same as non-existence.

Al-Jurjani begins by addressing a potential confusion: that priority and posteriority might be nothing but non-existence. If that were the case, then considering non-existence with the father would always make him prior, which is not true. Similarly for posteriority. This lesson examines the argument that priority and posteriority are distinct from non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of parentheses and the concise style.
2
Identify the main claim and hypothetical
The passage claims priority is not non-existence. It then presents a hypothetical: if priority were non-existence, then considering non-existence with the father would necessitate his priority forever. Understand why this is problematic.
3
Consider the example of non-existence before and after
Al-Jurjani gives an example: non-existence before the son's existence and non-existence after are both non-existence, but the priority of the prior is not like the posteriority of the posterior. This shows that priority and posteriority differ from non-existence itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعديته كما عرفت ( فلا تكون ( القبلية ) نفس العدم ) وإلا كان اعتبار العدم مع الأب موجباً لتقدمه أبداً، ولا تكون البعدية أيضاً نفس العدم لمثل ما ذكر ( وقد يعبر عنه ) هذا الذي ذكرناه من أن العدم يختلف بالقبلية والبعدية ) بأن العدم قبل ) أي قبل وجود الابن ( كالعدم بعد ( أي بعد وجوده ) وليس قبل كبعد ) أي ليس قبلية القبل كبعدية البعد فلا يكون شيء منهما نفس العدم كما أنَّ القبلية ليست نفس الأب وحده ولا مأخوذاً مع عدم الابن والبعدية أيضاً ليس نفس الابن وحده ولا مأخوذاً مع وجود الأب، بل القبلية والبعدية أمران زائدان على الأمور المذكورة وهما إضافتان فيستدعيان محلاً، وقد تبين أن عروض القبلية والبعدية للأب والابن ليس لذاتيهما وإلا لامتنع انفكاكهما عنهما وهو باطل لما فلا بد من شيء آخر يتصف بهما لذاته
And its posteriority as you know: 'priority is not the same as non-existence', otherwise considering non-existence with the father would necessitate its priority forever; nor is posteriority the same as non-existence for the like of what was mentioned. And it may be expressed by this that we have said, that non-existence differs by priority and posteriority: that non-existence before - i.e., before the existence of the son - is like non-existence after, i.e., after his existence, but the before is not like the after, i.e., the priority of the prior is not like the posteriority of the posterior; thus neither of them is the same as non-existence, just as priority is not the father alone nor taken with the non-existence of the son, and posteriority is also not the son alone nor taken with the existence of the father. Rather, priority and posteriority are two additional matters beyond the mentioned things, and they are two relations, so they require a locus. And it has become clear that the occurrence of priority and posteriority to the father and the son is not due to their essences, otherwise their disjunction from them would be impossible, which is false; therefore, there must be something else that is qualified by them essentially.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a proof that priority (قبلية) and posteriority (بعدية) are not identical to non-existence (عدم) and are not mere accidents of the essences of the father and son. Al-Jurjani begins by stating that priority is not the same as non-existence, for if it were, considering non-existence with the father would require it to be prior forever, which is not the case. Similarly, posteriority is not non-existence. He then explains that non-existence before and after are alike in being non-existence, but the priority of the prior differs from the posteriority of the posterior. Thus, neither priority nor posteriority is identical to non-existence. Moreover, priority is not the father alone, nor the father taken with the non-existence of the son; posteriority is not the son alone, nor the son taken with the existence of the father. Therefore, priority and posteriority are additional attributes (أمران زائدان) that are relational (إضافتان) and require a locus (محل). He concludes that their occurrence to the father and son is not due to the essences of the father and son themselves; otherwise, they could not be separated from them, but they can be separated (e.g., a father can exist without being prior to a son if no son exists). Hence, there must be some other entity that is qualified by these relations essentially. This sets up the need for time (زمان) as that substrate.

Key Terms
قبلية
بعدية
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority and posteriority are just non-existence.
Correction: If they were non-existence, then non-existence would always imply priority, which is not the case.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is priority not identical to non-existence?
A) Because non-existence is not related to priority
B) Because if priority were non-existence, then considering non-existence with the father would make him always prior
C) Because priority is the father alone
D) Because priority is a mental consideration only

Key Takeaway
Priority and posteriority are not identical to non-existence; they are distinct attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated your past sins as merely 'non-existent' now, ignoring that their posteriority still shapes your present accountability before Allah? How might recognizing the reality of posteriority change your attitude toward repentance?
?
In your daily choices, do you consider the priority of your intentions over their outcomes as a real attribute for which you will be judged, or do you dismiss them as nothing? What would it mean for you to take that priority seriously starting today?

Priority and Posteriority Are Additional Relational Attributes
Al-Jurjani proves that priority and posteriority are additional relational attributes requiring a substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positive claim that priority and posteriority are additional relational attributes that require a substrate (locus).

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority are additional relations, not reducible to essences or non-existence.

Having established that priority and posteriority are not non-existence, Al-Jurjani now builds the positive case. He argues that they are not simply the father or son, nor are they conjunctions of these with existence or non-existence. Rather, they are two additional relational attributes that need a substrate to inhere. This substrate is identified as time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the negative arguments
The passage begins by stating what priority and posteriority are not: priority is not the father alone nor the father with non-existence of the son; posteriority is not the son alone nor the son with existence of the father.
2
Grasp the positive claim
Al-Jurjani then asserts that priority and posteriority are two additional matters (أمران زائدان) that are relations (إضافتان). Understand what 'additional' means here: they are not reducible to the essences of father or son.
3
Understand the proof that they require a locus
The passage argues that the occurrence of priority and posteriority to the father and son is not due to their essences, because if it were, they could never be separated from them. Since they can be separated (e.g., a father without a son has no priority), there must be something else that is qualified by them essentially. That something is time.

Refuting Priority as an External Existent
Refutation of priority as an existent attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why priority (qabliyya) cannot be an existent attribute in external reality, as it is an accidental quality of non-existence.

Core Idea
Priority is not a real attribute; it is merely a mental concept tied to non-existence.

This lesson examines a key argument in Islamic theology: that temporal priority is not an independently existing attribute but rather a relational concept that applies to non-existence. The author refutes the claim that priority is an external existent by showing that it describes the non-existence of an event before its occurrence. Thus, the object described by priority (non-existence) is itself non-existent, so the attribute cannot be external.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the compact style and the use of technical terms.
2
Identify the key claim
The author claims that priority is an accident of non-existence. Find where this is stated.
The phrase 'فيكون التقدم عارضاً للعدم' is crucial.
3
Understand the reasoning
The author argues that what is an attribute of non-existence cannot be an external existent. Trace this logical step.
4
Consider the commentator's remark
The commentator notes that the proof is not fully valid because non-existence is not really described by priority. Reflect on this nuance.
This shows the depth of scholarly discussion.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Many think priority is a real attribute; the passage shows it is a mental construct.
This ties to the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجود له في الخارج فإن عدم الحادث مقدم على وجوده ذلك التقدم الذي ذكرتموه في الأب والابن قطعاً فيكون التقدم عارضاً للعدم وصفة له وما يعرض للعدم ويكون صفة له لا يكون أمراً موجوداً محققاً في الخارج بل يكون أمراً موهوماً اعتبارياً فلا يستدعي محلاً موجوداً فلا يلزم أن يكون معروض القبلية بالذات موجوداً خارجياً كما ادعيتموه . قوله: (فإن عدم إلخ) سند للمنع المستفاد من المقدمة الثانية أي لا نسلم أنَّ القبلية والبعدية موجودتان حتى يلزم وجود موصوفهما فإنَّ عدم الحادث موصوف بالقبلية وليست موجودة وقد ظهر لك مما سبق أنَّ السند لا يصلح للسندية لأن عدم الحادث ليس موصوفاً بها حقيقة وإن كان مقارناً لها .
There exists for it in external reality. For the non-existence of the originated is prior to its existence – that priority which you mentioned in the father and the son – absolutely. So priority becomes an accident of non-existence and a quality of it. And what accrues to non-existence and is a quality of it is not an actually existing thing in external reality, but rather is an imaginary, conventional thing. So it does not require an existing substratum. Thus it does not follow that the substratum of priority by essence is an external existent, as you claimed. His saying: ('For non-existence etc.') is a proof for the denial derived from the second premise, i.e., we do not concede that priority and posteriority exist such that the existence of their described object would follow. For the non-existence of the originated is described by priority, and it is not existent. And it has become clear to you from what preceded that the proof is not valid as a proof because non-existence of the originated is not described by it in reality, even if it is concomitant with it.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a claim that priority (qabliyya) and posteriority (ba'diyya) are existing attributes in external reality. The author begins by stating that priority is not an independent existent; rather, it is an accidental attribute of non-existence. He argues that the priority of the non-existence of an originated event before its existence is indisputable, but this priority is a quality of non-existence, not of an existing thing. Since non-existence is not an external entity, its attribute cannot be externally existing. Therefore, the thing that is subject to priority (the substratum of priority) does not need to be an external existent. The commentary then explains that this argument is a proof for denying that priority and posteriority are existent. The commentator clarifies that the denial is based on the second premise: that what is described as having priority (like the non-existence of an event) is itself non-existent, so the attribute cannot be existent. He also notes that the non-existence of the originated is described by priority only in a metaphorical sense, not in reality, even though it is concomitant with it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority and posteriority are real attributes existing in external reality.
Correction: They are merely mental concepts that apply to non-existence, not external entities.

Checkpoint

A) Because priority is a relation that requires two existents.
B) Because priority attaches to non-existence, which is not an external entity.
C) Because priority is eternal and unchanging.
D) Because priority is only perceived by the senses.

Key Takeaway
Priority is not an external attribute; it is a conventional concept that describes the relation of non-existence to existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

You often think of Allah's existence as 'prior' to all creation. Based on this lesson, is that priority an external attribute of Allah or a mental relation from our limited perspective? How does this affect your awe and reliance on Him as the Eternal who is not bound by our concepts of time?

Priority and Posteriority by Essence
The author explains the nature of the entity that has priority and posteriority by its very essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what it means for an entity to have priority and posteriority by essence and why such an entity cannot have its order reversed.

Core Idea
Some things have a fixed order by their very nature—they cannot be reversed.

This lesson unpacks a key definition from the author: the entity that is characterized by priority and posteriority by its own essence. The passage clarifies that such an entity is a single thing whose parts are inherently ordered, and this order cannot be swapped. We also see how this applies to the example of father and son, which the author uses to illustrate the concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice that the author defines the subject in the first sentence and then provides an example in the second.
The translation uses 'imagination' for 'وهم'.
2
Identify the definition of 'priority and posteriority by essence'
Focus on the first sentence. The author defines it as a single thing that, when divided mentally into two parts, one part is inherently prior and the other posterior, and this order cannot be reversed.
The phrase 'لذاته' indicates that the priority is due to its essence, not external factors.
3
Consider the example of father and son
The second sentence gives an example: the son cannot become prior to the father, and the father cannot become posterior to the son, because their essences necessitate this order.
Later lessons will show that this order is actually derived from time, not intrinsic to father and son themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (تلحقه القبلية والبعدية لذاته) بمعنى أنَّ هاهنا شيئاً واحداً إذا قسمه الوهم إلى جزأين يحكم بأن أحدهما قبل الآخر لأجل ذاته بحيث يمتنع ما أن يصير هو قبل بعد وبالعكس. قوله: (والابن وهو شيء لا يمكن أن يصير قبله بعد ولا بعده قبل) لأن ما تقتضيه ذات الشيء يستحيل انفكاكه عنه.
His saying: ('priority and posteriority attach to it by essence') means that here there is a single thing, when the imagination divides it into two parts, it judges that one of them is before the other due to its essence, such that it is impossible that it becomes before after and after before. His saying: ('and the son is a thing that cannot become before after nor after before') because what the essence of a thing necessitates is impossible to be separated from it.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines the entity that is characterized by priority and posteriority by essence. The author explains that such an entity is a single thing that, when the imagination divides it into two parts, one part is inherently judged to be prior and the other posterior, and this relation cannot be reversed. For example, a thing that is essentially prior can never become essentially posterior. Then the author quotes an additional statement: the son cannot become prior to the father because the father's essence necessitates priority over the son, and what the essence necessitates cannot be separated from it. This is the key property of time: it is that thing whose parts have a fixed order of priority and posteriority by essence.

Key Terms
قبلية
بعدية
ذات
وهم
جزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that father and son have priority and posteriority by their own essences intrinsically.
Correction: The passage uses this as an example, but later the author clarifies that such priority is derived from time; only time itself has priority and posteriority by essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does it mean for something to have priority and posteriority by essence?
A) It can change order over time.
B) Its parts are inherently ordered such that the order cannot be reversed.
C) It is conventionally considered prior.
D) It is imaginary.

Key Takeaway
Priority and posteriority by essence means a thing whose parts are inherently ordered in a way that cannot be reversed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an order in your relationship with your Creator that you treat as reversible—like a priority you think you can change? What would it mean to accept it as fixed by essence?
?
Think of a deeply ingrained habit or belief you thought you could reverse but now see as part of your core nature. Are you ready to embrace that unchangeable order, or do you still resist it?
?
The father-son example shows that essence dictates an irreversible order. In your daily life, what relationships or responsibilities are you treating as reversible that actually demand a fixed priority from you?

Ordinary Things Can Change Their Priority Status
The author argues that ordinary things can reverse their priority order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ordinary things like father and son do not have essential priority and can have their order reversed.

Core Idea
The substance of a father is indifferent to being before or after the son.

This lesson clarifies an important distinction: some priority is essential and fixed, while other priority is conventional and changeable. The author uses the example of father and son to show that their substances do not demand a particular order. This helps us understand the nature of temporal priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the author's claim about conventional priority and the example of father and son.
Pay attention to the phrase 'متعارف' (conventionally).
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that things described by priority/posteriority can change: what was before can become after and vice versa. Underline this statement.
3
Understand the reasoning
The author uses the substance (جوهر) of the father and son. Considered by themselves, their substances have no inherent order; they can exist before or after each other.
This shows they do not have essential priority.
4
Contrast with essential priority
Think about how this differs from entities that have priority by essence (like Allah over creation). The father and son are merely temporally ordered.
This distinction is crucial in kalam.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you grasp why the father can be posterior to the son.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وأما هذه الأشياء التي توصف في المتعارف بالقبلية والبعدية فيمكن فيها ذلك أعني أن يصير قبلها بعد وبعدها قبل) لأنا لو فرضنا جوهر الأب من حيث هو جوهر لا يمتنع أن يوجد قبل ذلك أي قبل الابن ولا بعده بل نسبة جوهره إلى القبلية والبعدية على سواء وكذا الحال في جوهر الابن فإنه من حيث هو أن يوجد قبل الأب أو بعده.
His saying: ('As for these things which are conventionally described by priority and posteriority, that is possible for them, i.e., that what was before becomes after and what was after becomes before') for if we suppose the substance of the father insofar as it is a substance, it is not impossible that it exists before that, i.e., before the son, nor after it; rather the relation of its substance to priority and posteriority is equal. And the same applies to the substance of the son, for it, insofar as it is, may exist before the father or after him.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage contrasts ordinary things (like a father and son) with the entity that has priority by essence (i.e., time). The author quotes the statement that ordinary things, which are conventionally described as prior or posterior, can have their order reversed: what was before can become after and vice versa. The explanation is that if we consider the substance (jawhar) of the father by itself, it is not impossible for it to exist before the son or after the son. The substance's relation to priority and posteriority is equal; it does not demand a fixed order. Similarly, the son's substance could exist before the father. Therefore, these ordinary things do not have priority by essence; they are only temporarily prior or posterior due to something else (time).

Key Terms
جوهر
أب
ابن
متعارف
تقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Father and son are essentially prior and posterior; their order is fixed.
Correction: The passage shows that their substances do not necessitate any order; they can be reversed if time changes.

Checkpoint
Why can the father become posterior to the son according to the passage?
A) Because the father's essence requires it.
B) Because the substance of the father has an equal relation to priority and posteriority.
C) Because the father ages.
D) Because time itself changes.

Key Takeaway
Ordinary things like father and son do not have essential priority; their substances are equally related to being before or after.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a relationship or priority in your life that you assume is fixed and unchangeable. How does the lesson about father and son — where even that relationship is indifferent in substance — challenge you to examine whether your assumption is truly essential or merely conventional? What would change if you accepted its contingency?
?
The father's substance is indifferent to being before or after the son. Are there doctrines or habits in your practice of Islam that you treat as necessary but are actually indifferent based on their substance? How would you discern the difference, and what would it cost you to let go of a fixed order?
?
If the priority between father and son is not inherent to their substances, what does that imply about your own subconscious ranking of people, duties, or beliefs? Ask yourself: Am I treating something as essentially prior that is actually reversible? What would it look like to reorder my life according to what is truly essential in Allah's sight?

Ordinary Things Derive Priority from Time
Conclusion: ordinary things owe priority to time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that priority and posteriority in ordinary things are derived from time, which has priority by essence.

Core Idea
Time gives priority to things, not their own essences.

This passage concludes a discussion on how ordinary things like father and son are considered prior or posterior. The author clarifies that their temporal order is not due to their essences but because of the times they occupy. Understanding this distinction helps avoid confusion about the nature of priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the concise style of the author.
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that priority and posteriority attach to things due to something else (time) that has these properties by essence.
3
Apply the example
The father is called prior because he exists in a prior time; the son is posterior because he exists in a later time.
This illustrates the claim concretely.
4
Consider the essences alone
If we consider the two essences (substances) by themselves, without time, they have no inherent priority.
This reinforces that priority comes from time.
5
Connect to broader lesson
Time is the fundamental ground of temporal order; ordinary things borrow their temporal attributes from it.
This is a key principle in Islamic theology.

قوله: (فهذه الأشياء إنما يلحقها التقدم والتأخر بسبب ذلك الأمر الذي تلحقه القبلية والبعدية لذاته) فكان الأب متقدماً لكونه في زمان متقدم والابن متأخراً لكونه في زمان متأخر. قوله: (بل اعتبر الذاتان) أعني جوهري الأب والابن من حيث أحدهما.

What Time Is Not: Negating Essential Priority and Posteriority
The author negates two problematic views of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time cannot be a single thing that has priority and posteriority due to its essence or through its parts.

Core Idea
Time is not a simple thing with inherent priority and posteriority.

Before classifying what time might be, the author first eliminates two problematic views. This lesson examines those negations to clarify that time is not a single entity that entails opposites by essence, nor does it gain priority through its parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening negations
Read the sentence carefully: 'Not that a single thing has priority and posteriority due to its essence... nor that a single thing has priority and posteriority by virtue of its parts...'. Note that two distinct possibilities are being rejected.
The objections following each negation highlight logical contradictions.
2
Understand the first negation
The first negation rejects the idea that a single thing could have both priority and posteriority from its own essence, because that would require it to entail opposites. Ask yourself: Can one thing be both prior and posterior by its own nature?
3
Understand the second negation
The second negation rejects the idea that priority comes from parts. If so, the thing would need to be a discrete quantity with parts coexisting intellectually, which is not applicable to time.
This prepares the ground for a different kind of classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا أنَّ شيئاً واحداً يعرض له القبلية والبعدية لأجل ذاته حتى يرد أن شيئاً واحداً كيف يقتضي المتنافيين لذاته ولا أنَّ شيئاً واحداً يعرض له القبلية والبعدية باعتبار أجزائه في الخارج لأجل ذاته حتى يرد أنه يلزم أن يكون ذلك الشيء كماً منفصلاً وأن تجتمع أجزاؤه في الوجود العقلية سبعة لأن الزمان إما أمر معين أو غير معين وعلى الأول إما واجب أو ممكن والممكن إما جوهر أو عرض والجوهر إما مجرد أو جسم أو جسماني، والعرض إما قار أو غير قار، والاحتمالات الثلاثة لم يذهب إليها أحد.
Not that a single thing has priority and posteriority due to its essence such that it would be objected how a single thing can entail two opposites due to its essence, nor that a single thing has priority and posteriority by virtue of its parts in external reality due to its essence such that it would be objected that that thing would be a discrete quantity and that its parts would coexist in intellectual existence. Seven [possibilities] because time is either determinate or indeterminate; and on the first, either necessary or contingent; and contingent either substance or accident; and substance either abstract, body, or corporeal; and accident either stable or non-stable. And the three possibilities no one has held.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage clarifies that time is not a single thing that has priority and posteriority by its essence in a problematic sense, nor does it have priority by virtue of its parts. Then it provides a logical classification of possible views on time. The classification begins with time being either determinate (mu'ayyan) or indeterminate (ghair mu'ayyan). If determinate, it is either necessary (wajib) or contingent (mumkin). If contingent, it is either substance (jawhar) or accident ('arad). Substance is further divided into abstract (mujarrad), body (jism), or corporeal (jismani). Accident is either stable (qarr) or non-stable (ghair qarr). This yields seven possibilities in total. The author notes that three of these possibilities are not held by anyone. This classification helps in understanding the different philosophical positions on time.

The Seven-Fold Classification of Time
The author enumerates seven logical possibilities for time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical classification of possible views on time into seven categories, and know which ones are actually held.

Core Idea
Time's possibilities are systematically divided into seven logical categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage

The classification is hierarchical, not flat.
2

Determinate: 2 (necessary and contingent). Contingent: 2 (substance and accident). Substance: 3 (abstract, body, corporeal). Accident: 2 (stable, non-stable). Essential Requisite and Impediments
The author qualifies the statement about essential inseparability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that an essential property is inseparable only when the essence necessitates it completely, and that an impediment can cause it to be absent when the exigency is incomplete.

Core Idea
An essential property is inseparable only when the essence demands it fully.

In kalam logic, it is often stated that what a thing's essence necessitates cannot be separated from it. However, this statement requires a crucial qualification: it holds only when the exigency is complete. This lesson unpacks the precise meaning of this principle and introduces the role of impediments in preventing the required property from appearing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Notice the technical terms used.
The passage is a short clarification, so pay attention to the conditional 'if'.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the terms: essence (ذات), exigency (اقتضاء), complete (تمام), impediment (مانع), and separation (انفكاك). Understand their meanings in this context.
These terms are foundational in kalam reasoning about essential properties.
3
Analyze the water example
The author gives the example of water and coldness. Explain why coldness may be absent from water when an impediment (like heat) is present.
This example illustrates that the exigency of coldness from water is not complete in all circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( لأن ما تقتضيه ذات الشيء يستحيل انفكاكه عنه أي اقتضاء تاماً كما يتبادر عند الإطلاق وأما إذا لم يكن الاقتضاء تاماً فقد ينفك المقتضي عنه لمانع كتخلف البرودة عن الماء.
His saying: ('because what the essence of a thing necessitates is impossible to be separated from it') i.e., a complete exigency, as is understood upon absolute usage. But if the exigency is not complete, the required may be separated from it due to an impediment, as the lack of coldness from water.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a brief terminological clarification. It explains a statement that what the essence of a thing necessitates cannot be separated from it. The commentator specifies that this applies only when the exigency (iqtida') is complete (tamm). When the word 'exigency' is used absolutely, it implies completeness. However, if the exigency is not complete, the required thing may not follow due to an impediment (mani'). An example is water: water normally necessitates coldness, but if an impediment exists (like fire), the coldness may be absent. This is a standard distinction in kalam logic about essential vs. accidental properties.

Key Terms

اقتضاء
تمام
مانع
انفكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that every essential property always accompanies its subject without exception.
Correction: The author clarifies that only when the exigency is complete is the property inseparable; otherwise an impediment can prevent it.

Checkpoint

A) When the exigency is incomplete.
B) When the essence is abstract.
C) When the exigency is complete.
D) When there is no impediment.

Key Takeaway
An essential property is inseparable only if the essence's exigency is complete; otherwise an impediment may cause it to be absent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

What impediment in your heart is causing your good nature to be suppressed? What must you burn away like fire to let the coldness of faith return?
?
If God's attributes are free from any impediment, His mercy is always complete. Do you truly believe His mercy can reach you despite your shortcomings? Or do you let the impediment of despair block that reality from transforming your life?

Non-Existence Cannot Bear Temporal Attributes: The Third Answer
The author presents the third answer to an objection, arguing that non-existence cannot be a substrate for temporal precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why non-existence cannot be a substrate for temporal priority or posteriority according to the author's third answer.

Core Idea
Non-existence is pure negation; it cannot receive existential attributes like priority.

The author presents a refined argument against the objection that non-existence must be described as temporally prior or posterior. He argues that temporal attributes are existential, and non-existence, being sheer negation, cannot be their substrate. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the distinction between three cases of prior and posterior.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the key terms: 'adam (non-existence), taqaddum (priority), ta'akhkhur (posteriority), zaman (time), wujud (existence), and 'urud (accidence). Recognize that priority and posteriority are considered existential (wujudiyya).
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Grasp the three-case distinction
The author explains: if both prior and posterior are times, no external time is needed; if neither is time, both need time; if one is time and the other not, only the non-time needs time. Understand this logical framework.
This sets up the application to non-existence.
4
Apply to non-existence
Apply the framework: non-existence of time is not time, so it would need time for priority/posteriority. But since priority/posteriority are existential, and non-existence is pure negation, it cannot be their substrate. Thus the opponent's claim fails.
The argument refutes the need for time in describing non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فجاز أن يكون تقدم وجوده على عدمه أو تقدم عدمه على وجوده كذلك أي يكون التقدم والتأخر بين وجوده وعدمه ليس بالزمان كما أجزاء الزمان، وفيه نظر لما تقدم من أنّ التقدم بين أجزائه تقدم زماني لكنه ليس بزمان زائد على المتقدم والمتأخر بل بزمان هو عينهما لأن التقدم والتأخر فيما بينها ناشئان من ذواتها بخلاف عدم الزمان فإنه لا يقتضي لذاته لا تقدماً ولا تأخراً بل لا بد أن يكون معه زمان ليعرض له التقدم أو التأخر بحسبه وتحريره أن كل واحد من المتقدم والمتأخر إذا كان زماناً لم يحتج في شيء منهما إلى زمان زائد عليه وإذا لم يكن شيء منهما زماناً احتيج فيهما إلى الزمان وإذا كان أحدهما زماناً والآخر ليس بزمان احتيج في الآخر إلى الزمان دون الأول وما نحن بصدده من هذا القبيل. الوجه الثالث من وجوه الجواب أن حكمكم بأن عدمه بعد وجوده أو قبل وجوده ليس إلا بالزمان إنما يصح أن لو كان العدم معروضاً للتأخر أو للتقدم وإنه أي كونه معروضاً لما ذكر محال عندكم فإنه أي التأخر أمر وجود على رأيكم وكذلك التقدم إذ لولاه لم يمكن لكم إثبات الزمان بثبوت التقدم والتأخر وما لا ثبوت له بوجه ما فإنه نفي محض وعدم صرف أعني عدم الزمان كيف يعرض له التقدم والتأخر الوجوديان اللهم إلا بحسب الفرض الذهني الذي لا يطابق الواقع ولا يعتد به أصلاً، وإذا لم يكن العدم معروضاً للتأخر بحسب نفس الأمر لم يتم ذلك البيان
Then it is possible that the priority of its existence over its non-existence, or the priority of its non-existence over its existence, is likewise, i.e., the priority and posteriority between its existence and non-existence is not by time as are the parts of time. And there is a consideration in it due to what preceded: that the priority between its parts is temporal priority, but it is not a time additional to the prior and posterior; rather it is by time that is identical to them because the priority and posteriority between them arise from their essences, unlike the non-existence of time, for it does not necessitate from itself any priority or posteriority; rather there must be with it a time so that priority or posteriority may accrue to it accordingly. And the gist is that each of the prior and posterior, if it is time, does not need in either of them an additional time; and if neither of them is time, both need time; and if one of them is time and the other is not time, the other needs time without the first. And what we are dealing with is of this kind. The third aspect of the answers: that your judgment that its non-existence after its existence, or before its existence, is only by time, would be valid only if non-existence were a substrate for posteriority or for priority. But that it is a substrate for what is mentioned is impossible in your view, because posteriority is an existential thing in your opinion, and likewise priority, since otherwise you could not establish time by affirming priority and posteriority. And that which has no existence in any respect is pure negation and sheer non-existence, I mean the non-existence of time; how can existential priority and posteriority accrue to it? Except by mental assumption that does not correspond to reality and is not taken into account at all. And if non-existence is not a substrate for posteriority in reality, then that proof is not established.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection concerning the temporal attribution of non-existence. The author presents the third answer to an objection, arguing that non-existence (al-'adam) cannot be a substrate (ma'rud) for temporal priority (taqaddum) or posteriority (ta'akhkhur). He begins by explaining that if both the prior and posterior are themselves times, no external time is needed for their temporal relation. However, if neither is time, an external time is required. In the case at hand, non-existence of time is not itself time, so one might think it needs time for priority or posteriority. But the author argues that the opponent's own view holds that priority and posteriority are existential attributes (umur wujudiyya). Non-existence, being pure negation, cannot bear such attributes except by mental fiction, which has no real basis. Therefore, the claim that non-existence must be described as prior or posterior only by time is invalid. The author concludes that since non-existence is not a real substrate for posteriority in reality, the proof that it requires time fails.

تقدم
تأخر
زمان
وجود
عروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence can be described as temporally prior or posterior.
Correction: Since priority and posteriority are existential, and non-existence is pure negation, non-existence cannot be their substrate.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can non-existence not be described as temporally prior or posterior?
A) Because non-existence has no beginning or end
B) Because priority and posteriority are existential attributes and non-existence is pure negation
C) Because time does not apply to non-existence
D) Because all things are eternal

Key Takeaway
Non-existence, being pure negation, cannot be a substrate for existential attributes like temporal priority or posteriority, so it cannot be described as temporally prior or posterior.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the time before your own existence, do you unconsciously imagine that non-existence as a 'time' or 'state' with qualities? How does this lesson expose that as a mental fiction, and what does that mean for how you view your own origin?
?
The author argues that non-existence cannot bear existential attributes. Consider your fear of death or annihilation. Are you attributing to non-existence properties like 'pain', 'loss', or 'darkness' that it cannot actually possess? How would your fear change if you truly internalized that al-'adam is pure negation?
?
This lesson uses logical precision to distinguish between reality and mental assumption. In what areas of your faith or daily life might you be confusing a mental construct for reality? What would it cost you to realign your thinking with the truth?

Time as the Outermost Sphere: Argument and Refutation
Author presents and refutes the second opinion on time as outermost sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument identifying time with the outermost sphere is logically invalid due to equivocation and syllogistic form.

Core Idea
An invalid syllogism cannot prove time is the outermost sphere.

In this passage, al-Ījī (or the commentator) presents the second opinion on the nature of time: that it is the outermost sphere. The argument draws an analogy between the sphere's encompassing of bodies and time's encompassing of events. However, the author immediately points out a critical logical flaw—the reasoning is a textbook fallacy. Let's examine the argument and its refutation together.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice the structure: statement of opinion, then the argument, then the refutation.
2
Identify the argument structure
The argument uses two premises: (1) The outermost sphere encompasses all moving bodies, (2) Time encompasses all moving bodies. The conclusion: time is the outermost sphere. This is a second-figure syllogism with two affirmative premises.
The second figure requires one negative premise to be valid.
3
Understand the refutation
The author points out two fatal flaws: (1) The syllogistic form (two affirmatives in second figure) is invalid, and (2) the term 'encompassing' is used equivocally—physical vs. temporal—so the middle term is not unified.
This is a classic example of logical fallacy analysis in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها أنه الفلك الأعظم لأنه محيط بالكل أي بكل الأجسام المتحركة المحتاجة إلى مقارنة الزمان، كما أن الزمان محيط بها أيضاً وهو استدلال بموجبتين من الشكل الثاني فلا كما ينتج علم في موضعه على أنَّ الإحاطة المذكورة في المقدمتين مختلفة المعنى قطعاً فلا يتحد الوسط أيضاً
And the second of them (i.e., the opinions on the reality of time) is that it is the outermost sphere, because it encompasses the whole, i.e., all moving bodies that need to be concomitant with time, just as time also encompasses them. And this is an inference by two affirmative premises of the second figure, so it produces no certainty, especially since the encompassing mentioned in the two premises is certainly different in meaning, so the middle term is not unified.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the second opinion on the reality of time, namely that time is the outermost sphere (al-falak al-a'zam). The argument given is that the sphere encompasses all moving bodies that need time, just as time encompasses them. The author immediately refutes this by noting that the reasoning is a syllogism with two affirmative premises in the second figure, which is invalid and yields no certainty. Furthermore, the term 'encompassing' (ihata) in the two premises has different meanings: for the sphere it means physical surrounding, and for time it means temporal concomitance. Thus the middle term is not unified, making the argument fallacious.

The author presents and refutes the third view that time is motion of the outermost sphere.

By the end of this lesson, you will understand why identifying time with motion is logically invalid and why motion's attributes differ from time's.

Core Idea
Time is not motion because motion has speed and slowness, but time does not.

In this passage, the author continues his refutation of false views about time. He presents the third opinion—that time is the motion of the outermost sphere—and then demonstrates its logical weakness and conceptual mismatch.

Aristotle's View: Time as the Measure of Motion
Aristotle presents his definition of time and its proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aristotle's definition of time as the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere, his proof based on quantity and the impossibility of indivisible parts, and how this view fits into the broader discussion.

Core Idea
Time is a quantity measuring cosmic motion, not a series of instants.

This passage presents the fourth and most celebrated opinion on the nature of time, that of Aristotle and his followers. Aristotle argues that time is the measure of the motion of the outermost celestial sphere. He offers a proof based on the properties of quantity and the logical impossibility of time being composed of indivisible instants. Let's examine his argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice the concise argumentative style.
2
Identify Aristotle's claim
Pinpoint the definition: time is the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere. This is the central thesis.
3
Understand the proof from quantity
Aristotle argues that time admits increase and decrease, so it is a quantity. Equality and inequality are properties of quantity, confirming this.
This step shows how time fits into the category of quantity.
4
Understand the rejection of indivisible parts
Since indivisible parts (juz' la yatajazzi') are impossible, time cannot be composed of successive instants. Otherwise, that would imply the existence of such parts.
This refutes the atomist view of time.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Aristotle ties time to the motion of the cosmos. This implies time is continuous and cosmic, not merely subjective or psychological.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها وهو المشهور فيما بين القوم ما ذهب إليه أرسطو ومن تبعه من أنه مقدار حركة الفلك الأعظم واحتج أرسطو على ذلك بأنه أي الزمان متفاوت بالزيادة والنقصان فهو كم لما مر من أن المساواة والمفاوتة من خواصه وقد ثبت بالبرهان امتناع الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ وتركب الجسم منه فلا يكون الزمان مركباً من آنات متتالية وإلا تركب الجسم من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ
And the fourth of them, which is famous among the people, is what Aristotle and his followers maintained: that time is the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere. Aristotle argued for that by [saying] that time is varying by increase and decrease, so it is a quantity, as has passed that equality and inequality are among its properties; and it has been established by proof the impossibility of the indivisible part and the composition of body from it; therefore time is not composed of successive instants, otherwise body would be composed of indivisible parts.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth and most famous opinion on time: the view of Aristotle and his followers that time is the measure (miqdar) of the motion of the outermost sphere. The proof is that time admits increase and decrease, so it is a quantity (kamm). Since it has been proven that indivisible parts (juz' la yatajazzi') cannot exist and body is composed of them, time cannot be composed of successive instants (anāt). If it were, then body would be composed of indivisible parts, which is impossible. The passage then continues with a gloss that presents a famous objection by Nasir al-Din al-Tusi: that motion requires speed and slowness, which presuppose time, so before the motion of the outermost sphere there would be time—a circularity. The answer is that speed and slowness are necessary concomitants of motion, posterior to it in essence, and time is likewise posterior as its measure; thus no actual precedence of time over the motion occurs, only the essential precedence of one property over another.

Key Terms
مقدار
حركة
فلك أعظم
أرسطو
آن
جزء لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main argument for time being the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere?
A) Time is eternal and so is motion
B) Time admits increase and decrease, so it is a quantity, and time cannot be composed of instants
C) The sphere encompasses everything
D) Time is non-stationary like motion

Key Takeaway
Aristotle argued that time must be a continuous quantity measuring the motion of the heavens, not a series of discrete instants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is not a series of disconnected instants but a continuous measure of motion, how does that challenge the way you treat your daily schedule? Are you living each moment as part of a seamless journey toward God, or as isolated events that you can compartmentalize?
?
Aristotle ties time directly to the motion of the cosmos. When you think about your own life, what 'motion' is your time measuring? Is it the motion of your desires, your duties, or your spiritual journey? Be honest: what would the measure of your life's motion reveal about your ultimate direction?
?
The proof shows that time cannot be broken into tiny, independent parts—it flows as a whole. How does this reality confront your habit of postponing repentance or good deeds? Since time is continuous and indivisible, how can you justify putting off a single 'instant' of obedience to Allah?

The Second Doctrine: Time is the Celestial Sphere
Introduces and refutes the view that time is the outermost celestial sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the view that time is the outermost celestial sphere is logically unsound.

Core Idea
A valid argument requires univocal terms; 'encompassing' is equivocal here.

In this lesson, we examine the second major doctrine on the nature of time: that time is identical to the outermost celestial sphere. The proponents argue from encompassing, but the refutation shows a logical flaw. We'll see how kalam uses syllogistic reasoning to critique such views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get the overall flow.
Notice the compressed style of kalam arguments.
2
Identify the premises and middle term
The passage presents a syllogism: the sphere encompasses all bodies, time encompasses all bodies, therefore time is the sphere. Identify the two premises and the middle term 'encompassing'.
3
Understand the refutation
The author points out two problems: the syllogism has two affirmative premises in the second figure (invalid form) and the term 'encompassing' has different meanings (spatial vs. temporal). Reflect on why this makes the argument unsound.
This connects to the core idea of univocal terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها) أي ثاني المذاهب التي في حقيقة الزمان ) أنه الفلك الأعظم لأنه محيط بالكل أي بكل الأجسام المتحركة المحتاجة إلى مقارنة الزمان، كما أن الزمان محيط بها أيضاً ) وهو استدلال بموجبتين من الشكل الثاني ) فلا كما ينتج علم في موضعه على أنَّ الإحاطة المذكورة في المقدمتين مختلفة المعنى قطعاً فلا يتحد الوسط أيضاً
And the second of them, meaning the second of the doctrines concerning the reality of time, is that it is the outermost celestial sphere because it encompasses everything, i.e., all moving bodies that need to be accompanied by time, just as time also encompasses them. And it is a syllogism with two affirmative premises in the second figure, so it does not produce knowledge in its place, because the encompassing mentioned in the two premises is certainly different in meaning, so the middle term is not unified.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines the second doctrine on the nature of time, namely that time is the celestial sphere itself. The proof offered is that both time and the sphere encompass all moving bodies. The author then refutes this by pointing out that the argument is a syllogism in the second figure with two affirmative premises, which is invalid. Moreover, the term 'encompassing' has different meanings in the two premises: the sphere encompasses spatially, while time encompasses by accompanying existence. Thus the middle term is not univocal, and the syllogism fails.

Time as Celestial Motion: Refutation
States and refutes the view that time is motion of the celestial sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third doctrine that time is the motion of the celestial sphere and the two arguments used to refute it: the logical flaw in the syllogism and the disanalogy of speed and slowness.

Core Idea
Time is not motion, because motion has speed but time does not.

The third doctrine identifies time with the motion of the celestial sphere, relying on their shared non-stability. The refutation exposes a logical flaw in the argument and a critical disanalogy: motion, unlike time, admits of speed and slowness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the argument presented and the refutation.
Pay attention to the structure: doctrine, reason, critique.
2
Identify the logical flaw
The author points out that the argument is a second-figure syllogism with two affirmative premises, which is invalid. Understand why this form fails.
3
Understand the disanalogy
Motion can be described as fast or slow, but time cannot. This shows time and motion are not identical despite both being non-stable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها أنه حركة الفلك الأعظم لأنها غير قارة كما أن الزمان غير قار أيضاً وهو أعني هذا الاستدلال من جنس ما قبله فإنه أيضاً استدلال بموجبتين من الشكل الثاني فلا يصح كيف والحركة توصف بالسرعة والبطء حقيقة، بخلاف الزمان
And the third of them is that it is the motion of the outermost celestial sphere, because it is non-stable just as time is also non-stable. And this argument, I mean this inference, is of the same kind as the previous one, for it is also a syllogism with two affirmative premises in the second figure, so it is not valid. How so? While motion is truly described by swiftness and slowness, unlike time.

The author refutes it by noting that the argument suffers from the same logical flaw as the previous one: it is a second-figure syllogism with two affirmative premises. Additionally, motion inherently possesses the properties of speed and slowness, but time does not, so they cannot be identical.

The Argument: Time as the Measure of the Fastest Motion
Argument that time is the measure of the fastest motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Aristotelian definition of time as the measure of motion is argued using the analogy of measuring larger quantities by smaller ones and the identification of the fastest motion (the daily rotation of the celestial sphere).

Core Idea
Time is measured by the fastest motion, just as larger distances are measured by smaller units.

This passage presents a concise argument for the classical definition of time as the measure of motion. It begins with the observation that smallness of time implies speed of motion, and then uses the principle that larger quantities are measured by smaller ones. The argument identifies the fastest motion—the daily rotation of the celestial sphere—as the standard unit of time, from which all other motions are measured. Understanding this argument is essential for engaging with subsequent objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice the logical flow from 'smallness of time' to the conclusion that time is the measure of the daily motion.
The passage uses an analogy with physical measurements.
2
Understand the measurement analogy
The argument states that larger quantities are measured by smaller ones (e.g., parasang measured by spears, spears by cubits). How does this analogy apply to time and motion?
The smallest unit of time corresponds to the fastest motion.
3
Identify the fastest motion
The passage claims the fastest motion is the daily motion of the 'greatest sphere' (celestial sphere). Why is this considered the fastest?
This is based on Aristotelian cosmology.
4
See how all motions are measured
Time is defined as the measure of the daily motion. This motion is measured primarily, and all other motions are measured secondarily by comparison. Reflect on what this implies about the nature of time.
The argument aims to establish time as a quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلة الزمان تقتضي سرعة الحركة، وحينئذ أمكن أن يقدر به الحركات كلها ( لأن الأكبر) بحسب المقدار ( يقدر بالأصغر ولا يعكس فيقال : هذا الفرسخ كذا رمحاً، وهذا الرمح كذا ذراعاً، وهذا الذراع كذا أصبعاً فإن الأصغر يعد الأكبر) لاشتمال الأكبر على مثل الأصغر مع زيادة ) والأكبر لا يعد الأصغر لاستحالة اشتماله على مثل الأكبر ( وقد علمت أن أسرع الحركات الموجودة ( هي الحركة اليومية) التي ) هي حركة الفلك الأعظم ( فالزمان مقدار الحركة اليومية ) فيقدر به تلك الحركة أولا، وبالذات وسائر الحركات ثانياً وبالعرض ( وهو المطلوب والاعتراض عليه أنه مبني على أمور كلها ممنوعة الأولى كل قابل للتفاوت كم، وإنما يصح أن لو بین أنه قابل للتفاوت لذاته ) ولم يبين ذلك في الزمان ( الثاني امتناع الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ) وإلا لجاز كون الزمان كماً منفصلاً وما استدل به علی امتناع الجزء مردود كما ستعرفه ( الثالث امتناع عدمه ) إذ لو جاز عدم الزمان لجاز أن يكون مقدار الحركة مستقيمة منقطعة ( والدليل ) الذي استدل به على امتناع عدمه قد عرفت ما فيه من الخلل ( الرابع أن بين كل حركتين سكوناً فإنه إذا لم يجب ذلك جاز أن تكون تلك الحركة المستقيمة مستمرة بلا انقطاع على طريقة الرجوع والانعطاف ويكون الزمان مقدارها وما يتمسك به في إثبات السكون بينهما ستقف على فساده ( الخامس أن له ) أي للزمان ( محلاً إما لوجوده أو لعرضيته والأولى أن يترك هذا الترديد ويقال : لوجوده وعرضيته، فإن اقتضاءه محلاً موجوداً يتوقف على وجوده وعرضيته معاً ( ولم يثبتا )
For the smallness of time necessitates speed of motion, and then it is possible to measure all motions by it, because the larger in terms of quantity is measured by the smaller, and not vice versa. It is said: this parasang is so many spears, this spear is so many cubits, this cubit is so many fingers, for the smaller counts the larger, because the larger contains the like of the smaller with increase, and the larger does not count the smaller because it is impossible for it to contain the like of the larger. And you have known that the fastest of existing motions is the daily motion, which is the motion of the greatest sphere. So time is the measure of the daily motion, so that motion is measured by it firstly and essentially, and other motions secondly and accidentally. That is what is sought. And the objection against it is that it is based on matters, all of which are denied: The first: every [thing] that admits variation is a quantity, and it is only valid if it is shown that it admits variation by itself, and that has not been shown for time. The second: the impossibility of the indivisible part, otherwise time could be a discrete quantity, and what has been adduced for the impossibility of the part is rebutted, as you will know. The third: the impossibility of its non-existence, for if non-existence of time were possible, then the measure of motion could be a straight interrupted [motion], and the proof adduced for the impossibility of its non-existence—you have known what defect is in it. The fourth: that between every two motions there is a rest, for if that were not necessary, then that straight motion could be continuous without interruption in the manner of return and turning, and time would be its measure, and what is clung to in proving rest between them—you will come to know its falseness. The fifth: that it [time] has a substrate, either for its existence or for its accidentality, and it is better to leave this hesitation and say: for its existence and accidentality, for its requiring an existing substrate depends on both its existence and accidentality together, and neither has been proven.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the Aristotelian definition of time as the measure of motion, followed by an objection listing five premises that the objector denies. The argument begins by establishing that the fastest motion—the daily motion of the celestial sphere—serves as the unit for measuring all other motions, analogous to how smaller units (like fingers) measure larger ones (like cubits). Time, then, is defined as the measure of this fastest motion, with other motions measured secondarily. The objection rejects this definition by attacking its underlying assumptions. The first premise is that time admits variation (and thus is a quantity), but this is unproven. The second premise assumes the impossibility of indivisible parts (atoms of time), but this is disputed. The third premise claims time cannot be non-existent, but its proof is flawed. The fourth premise asserts a necessary rest between motions to avoid an infinite regress of continuous motion, but this is also falsifiable. The fifth premise requires time to have an existing substrate (like motion) due to its existence and accidentality, but neither time's existence nor its accidentality has been established. The objector systematically denies each premise, thereby undermining the definition.

الزمان
الحركة
المقدار
الأصغر
الأكبر
الحركة اليومية
الفلك الأعظم

Key Takeaway

?
The argument assumes the fastest motion is the measure. In your own life, do you let the fastest, most urgent tasks measure your time, or do you step back to reflect on what is most important? How can you resist being driven by mere speed?
?
You have known that the fastest motion is the daily rotation of the sphere. But what if the fastest motion in your heart is turning away from Allah? What is the daily rotation of your soul—toward Him or away? If your time is measured by that motion, where are you heading?

The Objection: Five Denied Premises Against the Definition of Time
Objection listing five denied premises of the time definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand five specific premises on which the definition of time as the measure of motion relies, and why each premise is disputed by the objector.

Core Idea
The definition of time fails because its five premises are all unproven.

After presenting the argument that time is the measure of the fastest motion, the text introduces a powerful objection. The objection claims that the argument rests on five distinct premises, each of which is denied. These premises range from the quantitative nature of time to its need for an existing substrate. By examining each premise, we see the fragility of the definition and the challenges it faces. This objection showcases the rigorous dialectical style of Islamic kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Notice the numbered list of five 'matters' (أمور) that are deemed untenable.
The passage uses the phrase 'مبني على أمور كلها ممنوعة'.
2
Analyze the first premise
The first premise states that time admits variation and is thus a quantity. Why is this disputed? The objector says it hasn't been proven that time admits variation by itself.
This premise is about the nature of time as a continuous quantity.
3
Analyze the second premise
The second premise denies the indivisible part (atom) of time. If indivisible parts are possible, time could be discrete. Why does the objector reject its impossibility?
This relates to the debate on atomism in time.
4
Analyze the remaining premises
The third premise argues that time cannot be non-existent, the fourth that there is a rest between every two motions, and the fifth that time has a substrate. Consider each objection.
These premises address the continuity and ontology of time.
5
Reflect on the overall objection
The objector concludes that none of the premises have been proven, so the definition of time as the measure of motion is unfounded. Think about whether any of these objections could be answered.
This is a typical debater's move in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلة الزمان تقتضي سرعة الحركة، وحينئذ أمكن أن يقدر به الحركات كلها ( لأن الأكبر) بحسب المقدار ( يقدر بالأصغر ولا يعكس فيقال : هذا الفرسخ كذا رمحاً، وهذا الرمح كذا ذراعاً، وهذا الذراع كذا أصبعاً فإن الأصغر يعد الأكبر) لاشتمال الأكبر على مثل الأصغر مع زيادة ) والأكبر لا يعد الأصغر لاستحالة اشتماله على مثل الأكبر ( وقد علمت أن أسرع الحركات الموجودة ( هي الحركة اليومية) التي ) هي حركة الفلك الأعظم ( فالزمان مقدار الحركة اليومية ) فيقدر به تلك الحركة أولا، وبالذات وسائر الحركات ثانياً وبالعرض ( وهو المطلوب والاعتراض عليه أنه مبني على أمور كلها ممنوعة الأولى كل قابل للتفاوت كم، وإنما يصح أن لو بین أنه قابل للتفاوت لذاته ) ولم يبين ذلك في الزمان ( الثاني امتناع الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ) وإلا لجاز كون الزمان كماً منفصلاً وما استدل به علی امتناع الجزء مردود كما ستعرفه ( الثالث امتناع عدمه ) إذ لو جاز عدم الزمان لجاز أن يكون مقدار الحركة مستقيمة منقطعة ( والدليل ) الذي استدل به على امتناع عدمه قد عرفت ما فيه من الخلل ( الرابع أن بين كل حركتين سكوناً فإنه إذا لم يجب ذلك جاز أن تكون تلك الحركة المستقيمة مستمرة بلا انقطاع على طريقة الرجوع والانعطاف ويكون الزمان مقدارها وما يتمسك به في إثبات السكون بينهما ستقف على فساده ( الخامس أن له ) أي للزمان ( محلاً إما لوجوده أو لعرضيته والأولى أن يترك هذا الترديد ويقال : لوجوده وعرضيته، فإن اقتضاءه محلاً موجوداً يتوقف على وجوده وعرضيته معاً ( ولم يثبتا )
For the smallness of time necessitates speed of motion, and then it is possible to measure all motions by it, because the larger in terms of quantity is measured by the smaller, and not vice versa. It is said: this parasang is so many spears, this spear is so many cubits, this cubit is so many fingers, for the smaller counts the larger, because the larger contains the like of the smaller with increase, and the larger does not count the smaller because it is impossible for it to contain the like of the larger. And you have known that the fastest of existing motions is the daily motion, which is the motion of the greatest sphere. So time is the measure of the daily motion, so that motion is measured by it firstly and essentially, and other motions secondly and accidentally. That is what is sought. And the objection against it is that it is based on matters, all of which are denied: The first: every [thing] that admits variation is a quantity, and it is only valid if it is shown that it admits variation by itself, and that has not been shown for time. The second: the impossibility of the indivisible part, otherwise time could be a discrete quantity, and what has been adduced for the impossibility of the part is rebutted, as you will know. The third: the impossibility of its non-existence, for if non-existence of time were possible, then the measure of motion could be a straight interrupted [motion], and the proof adduced for the impossibility of its non-existence—you have known what defect is in it. The fourth: that between every two motions there is a rest, for if that were not necessary, then that straight motion could be continuous without interruption in the manner of return and turning, and time would be its measure, and what is clung to in proving rest between them—you will come to know its falseness. The fifth: that it [time] has a substrate, either for its existence or for its accidentality, and it is better to leave this hesitation and say: for its existence and accidentality, for its requiring an existing substrate depends on both its existence and accidentality together, and neither has been proven.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the Aristotelian definition of time as the measure of motion, followed by an objection listing five premises that the objector denies. The argument begins by establishing that the fastest motion—the daily motion of the celestial sphere—serves as the unit for measuring all other motions, analogous to how smaller units (like fingers) measure larger ones (like cubits). Time, then, is defined as the measure of this fastest motion, with other motions measured secondarily. The objection rejects this definition by attacking its underlying assumptions. The first premise is that time admits variation (and thus is a quantity), but this is unproven. The second premise assumes the impossibility of indivisible parts (atoms of time), but this is disputed. The third premise claims time cannot be non-existent, but its proof is flawed. The fourth premise asserts a necessary rest between motions to avoid an infinite regress of continuous motion, but this is also falsifiable. The fifth premise requires time to have an existing substrate (like motion) due to its existence and accidentality, but neither time's existence nor its accidentality has been established. The objector systematically denies each premise, thereby undermining the definition.

Correction: The objection is specifically against the definition of time as the measure of motion, not against time per se.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The objector denies each premise by pointing out it is unproven, not by proving its opposite. When you hold a belief—especially about time, existence, or God—do you ever examine whether it rests on unproven assumptions? ?
The objector challenges premises that many take for granted. Is there a truth in your faith that you accept simply because it is familiar, rather than because you have genuinely grappled with its proofs? Refuting the Restriction of Time to Changing Things
Objection and refutation regarding the exclusive application of time to changing things

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun refute the claim that time applies only to changing things, and why they assert that time also applies to the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
Time applies to God as it applies to motions — there is no difference.

In this passage, an objection is raised that time only relates changing things to each other, not to constant beings like God. The response dismisses this as empty rattling, pointing out that we have already established that time occurs for the Necessary Being just as it occurs for motions. This lesson unpacks that rebuttal and its foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector defines three terms: zaman (time) for two changing things, dahr for a changing and a constant, and sarmad for two constants. Then he concludes that time is an accident only of changing things. Notice how he builds from definitions to a conclusion.
This is a typical kalam objection from a rival position.
2
Understand the response: 'qa'qa'a'
The answer dismisses the entire objection as 'qa'qa'a' — empty rattling with no substance. Look at the commentary's explanation: because we have proven that time occurs for the Necessary Being, the restriction to changing things is meaningless.
The term qa'qa'a originally imitates the sound of weapons, used here to mean idle talk.
3
Grasp the core reasoning
The key point is that time is not limited to changing things; it has been established for the Necessary Being (God) without any difference from its occurrence for motions. This is the doctrinal foundation that overrides the objection.
This connects to the broader kalam argument about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: نسبة المتغير إلى المتغير هو الزمان، ونسبة المتغير إلى الثابت هو الدهر، ونسبة الثابت إلى الثابت هو السرمد، فالزمان عارض للمتغيرات دون الثابتات. قلنا: ما ذكرتموه قعقعة، وهي في الأصل حكاية صوت السلاح وغيره، ما تحتها طائل وفائدة. قوله: (ما ذكرتموه قعقعة) لأنا أثبتنا عروضه للواجب تعالى كعروضه للحركات من غير تفاوت، فالقول بعروضه للمتغيرات دون الثابتات قول لا معنى له، فيكون قعقعة. والشن القربة الخلو، والجمع الشنان، كذا في الصحاح.
For if it is said: The relation of the changing to the changing is time, and the relation of the changing to the constant is dahr, and the relation of the constant to the constant is sarmad, so time is an accident of changing things without constant things. We say: What you mentioned is rattling (qa'qa'a), which originally is the imitation of the sound of weapons and other things, having no substance or benefit. His words: (What you mentioned is rattling) because we have proven its occurrence for the Necessary (al-wajib), exalted, like its occurrence for motions without difference, so the statement that it occurs for changing things without constant things is a meaningless statement, so it is rattling. And al-shan is the waterskin, empty, plural shinan, as in al-Sihah.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector defines three terms: zaman (time) as the relation between two changing things, dahr as the relation between a changing and a constant, and sarmad as the relation between two constants. From this he concludes that time is only an accident (عارض) of changing things, not constant things. The answer rejects this outright, calling it 'قعقعة' (empty rattling). The commentary explains that we have already established that time occurs for the Necessary Being (God) just as it occurs for motions, without any difference. Therefore, claiming that time only applies to changing things is meaningless. The word قعقعة is explained as the sound of weapons, and a reference is made to the proverb 'لا تقعقع لي بالشنان' (Do not rattle the waterskin at me), indicating empty talk.

Correction: The passage affirms that time applies to the Necessary Being (God) in the same way it applies to motions, so the restriction of time to changing things is false.
