The Ash'ari Definition of Time
Al-Jurjani defines time and illustrates reciprocal estimation with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of time as a renewing known thing used to measure vague durations, and how estimation can be reciprocal depending on what is known to the listener.

Core Idea
Time is a relational measurement based on known events, not a fixed entity.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents the Ash'ari view on time, breaking from the philosophers' concept of a continuous flow. He defines time as a renewing known measure used to specify vague durations, showing its relativity through examples from different cultures. This lesson unpacks the definition and the principle of reciprocal estimation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the author's own words.
Notice the key terms: متجدد (renewing), معلوم (known), مبهم (vague).
2
Identify the core definition
Locate the sentence that states the Ash'ari definition of time. Understand the key components: time is a renewing known thing used to measure another renewing vague thing.
3
Understand reciprocal estimation
The passage explains that estimation can go both ways. Find the example of Zaid's coming and sunrise and see how the known event changes based on the listener.
4
Examine the cultural examples
The author gives examples from a reciter, a woman, a boy, and a Turk. Notice how each uses a known event to measure an unknown duration.
These examples show that 'time' is relative to what is known in each culture.
5
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: What does this definition say about the nature of time? How does it differ from a view of time as an independent entity?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسها) أي خامس المذاهب في حقيقة الزمان ) مذهب الأشاعرة ) وهو ( أنه متجدد ) معلوم ) يقدر به (متجدد) مبهم إزالة لإبهامه ) وقد يتعاكس التقدير بين المتجددات فيقدر تارة هذا بذاك وأخرى ) ذاك بهذا وإنما يتعاكس ( بحسب ما هو متصور ) ومعلوم ( للمخاطب فإذا قيل ) مثلاً (متى جاء زيد ؟ يقال : عند طلوع الشمس إن كان المخاطب الذي هو السائل ( مستحضراً لطلوع الشمس) ولم يكن مستحضراً لمجيء زيد كما دل عليه سؤاله ( ثم إذا قال غيره : متى طلوع الشمس؟ يقال: حين جاء زيد لمن كان مستحضراً لمجيء زيد ) دون طلوعها الذي سأل عنه ) ولذلك ) أي لأن الزمان متجدد معلوم يقدر به متجدد مبهم ( اختلف ) الزمان ( بالنسبة إلى الأقوام) فيقدر كل واحد منهم المبهم بما هو معلوم عنده ( فيقول القارئ لآتينك قبل أن تقرأ أم الكتاب و تقول ) المرأة لبث فلان عندي قدر ما تغزل كبة و ) يقول ( الصبي ينطبخ البيض إذا عددت ثلاثمائة ويصير نيم برشت إذا عددت ستين فإن أول ما يتعلمه الصبيان هو الحساب (و) يقول (التركي) قعد فلان عندي ( بقدر ما ينطبخ (مرجل) أي قدر من نحاس ( لحماً وعلى هذا كل من الأقوام ) بحسب ما هو مقدر معلوم (عنده يقدر غيره )
And the fifth of the schools on the reality of time is the school of the Ash'aris, and it is that it (time) is a renewing known thing by which another renewing vague thing is measured to remove its vagueness. And the estimation may be reciprocal between renewing things: sometimes this is measured by that, and sometimes that is measured by this. It is reciprocal according to what is perceived and known by the addressee. For example, when it is said: 'When did Zaid come?', it is said: 'At sunrise' if the addressee, who is the questioner, has sunrise present in mind and does not have Zaid's coming present in mind, as indicated by his question. Then when another says: 'When does sunrise occur?', it is said: 'When Zaid came' to one who has Zaid's coming present in mind, not the sunrise about which he asked. Therefore, because time is a renewing known thing by which a renewing vague thing is measured, time differs relative to peoples. Each one of them measures the vague thing by what is known to him. The reciter says: 'I will come to you before you recite the Fatiha.' The woman says: 'So-and-so stayed with me as long as it takes to spin a ball.' The boy says: 'The egg cooks when I count to three hundred, and it becomes nim-birisht when I count to sixty, for the first thing boys learn is arithmetic.' The Turk says: 'So-and-so sat with me as long as it takes to cook meat in a copper pot.' And in this way, every people, according to what is estimated and known to them, estimate other things.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage defines the Ash'ari view of time and illustrates it with examples. It begins by stating the Ash'ari position: time is a renewing (mutajaddid) known thing by which another renewing vague thing is measured to remove its vagueness. The author then explains that the estimation can be reciprocal between two renewing things, depending on which one is known to the listener. For instance, when someone asks 'When did Zaid come?', the answer 'at sunrise' uses a known event (sunrise) to locate an unknown event (Zaid's coming). Conversely, if another asks 'When is sunrise?', the answer 'when Zaid came' uses the now-known event. This shows that time is relative to what is known. The author then provides cultural examples: a reciter uses reading the Fatiha, a woman uses spinning a ball, a child uses counting a particular number (since children first learn arithmetic), and a Turk uses cooking meat in a copper pot. Each group measures the vague duration by a known, specific event. The passage thus elucidates the Ash'ari doctrine that time is not a fixed, continuous entity but a mental construct based on known events.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is an absolute, continuous entity independent of events.
Correction: The Ash'ari view holds that time is a relational measure based on known renewing things.

Checkpoint

A) A fixed, continuous entity
B) A renewing known thing used to measure another renewing vague thing
C) An eternal substance
D) The movement of the celestial sphere

Key Takeaway
Time, in the Ash'ari view, is a relational measurement that depends on known events to remove vagueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you measure the passing of your days by worldly achievements (like a job promotion or a milestone), what 'known event' are you using to make your life feel meaningful? Is that known event truly reliable in the sight of Allah, or is it just a familiar cultural habit?
?
The Ash'ari definition shows that time differs among peoples based on their known events. How does your own social circle or cultural background shape what you consider 'urgent' or 'valuable' in your worship? Are you letting the world's clock dictate your spiritual priorities?
?
You use 'known events' to remove vagueness from your plans and memories. But what about the most vague event of all: your death? What known event are you using to prepare for it—or are you avoiding the measurement altogether? If you are not measuring your life by the Hereafter, what are you really measuring it by?

Objection to Ash'ari View of Time and Its Answer
Objection to Ash'ari definition and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main objection raised against the Ash'ari definition of time and how the Ash'ari responds by clarifying that time is an imaginary concept, not a real entity.

Core Idea
The objection fails because it mistakes an imaginary concept for a real existent.

Having discussed the Ash'ari view that time is an imaginary thing abstracted from the relation of events, the author now presents an objection that challenges this definition. The objection argues that if time is identified with the renewing thing or with conjunction, it leads to absurdities. The answer clarifies that these criticisms only apply if one takes the words literally, whereas the real intent is that time is a mental construct. Understanding this exchange deepens our grasp of the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection against the Ash'ari definition
Start by reading the first part of the passage where the objection is presented. Notice the two versions of the definition that are attacked: time as the renewing thing itself and time as conjunction or companionship.
The objector tries to show that both options lead to contradictions.
2
Identify the Ash'ari response
Then read the commentator's response, which explains that the objection only applies if the Ash'ari words are taken literally. The intended meaning is that time is an imaginary thing (mawhum) abstracted from events.
The key distinction is between literal interpretation and intended meaning.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this defense is important for the Ash'ari position. It shows that their definition is not vulnerable to such objections because time is not a real entity but a mental construct.
This ties back to the broader discussion on the nature of time.

ويرد عليه أنه إن جعل الزمان عبارة عن نفس ذلك المتجدد لزم أن يكون أمراً موجوداً لا موهوماً كما هو مذهبهم. وأيضاً إذا كان ذلك المتجدد في نفسه وقتاً، فإذا بقي مدة وهو واحد بعينه وجب أن يكون مدة الابتداء وقتاً واحداً بعينه؛ وهو باطل قطعاً، وإن جعل عبارة عن الاقتران والمعية فلا شك أن كل مقترنين إنما يقترنان في شيء وأن كل معينين فهما في أمر ما معاً فذلك الشيء الذي فيه المعية هو الوقت الذي يجمعهما ويمكن أن يجعل كل منهما دالاً عليه بل يمكن أن يدل عليه بغيرهما من الأمور الواقعة فيه فليست المعية نفس ما يقع فيه الحوادث بل هي عارضة لها مقيسة إلى ماتقع فيه وكذلك القبلية والبعدية وذلك مما لا يشتبه على متأمل فأصحاب هذا المذهب جعلوا أعلام الأوقات أوقاتاً ولذلك يتعاكس التوقيت عندهم وإذا اعتبر ما هو وقت في الحقيقة امتنع التعكيس في التوقيت . ثم قوله : ( ويرد عليه إلخ ) إشارة إلى ما ذكره بقوله : ثم التحقيق ما قد عرفته إلخ . قوله : ( ويرد عليه إلخ) هذا الإيراد إنما يرد أن لو أجرى كلامهم على ظاهره أما لو قيل : إِنَّ مقصودهم أنه أمر موهوم ينتزعه الوهم من تصور مقارنة الحوادث، وتقدم بعضها على بعض؛ وتأخره عنه، ولا سبيل إلى فهمه وتعيينه إلا باعتبار الحوادث التي يجعلها القوم إعلاماً له فلا إيراد عليهم. قوله : ( كما هو مذهبهم في الشفاء جعل هذه المذاهب مقابلاً لمذهب كونه أمراً وهمياً وقال : إِنَّ أصحاب هذا القول يجعلون الزمان موجوداً على أنه أمر واحد في نفسه. قوله : ( وقد سبق ما يتعلق بالتقصي إلخ) إشارة إلى ما نقل من المباحث المشرقية من أنَّ الزمان الموجود عندهم هو الآن السيال المنطبق على الحركة بمعنى التوسط . قوله : ( وجب أن يكون مدة البقاء ومدة الابتداء وقتاً واحداً بعينه أراد لزوم كونهما واحداً بالذات فلا يجدي اعتبار التغاير باعتبار التجدد كما في الآن المستمر الغير المستقر.
And an objection is raised against it: if time is made to be the same as that renewing thing itself, then it would necessarily be an existent thing, not an imaginary thing, as is their school. Also, if that renewing thing is in itself a time, then if it remains for a duration while being one and the same, then the duration of the beginning would necessarily be one and the same time; that is absolutely false. And if it is made to be conjunction and companionship, then there is no doubt that every two things that are in conjunction are in something together, and that every two determined things are together in some matter; that thing in which companionship exists is the time that unites them. Each of them can be made an indicator of it, and indeed it can be indicated by other things occurring in it. So companionship is not the same as that in which events occur; rather it is an accident to them, measured relative to that in which they occur. Likewise priority and posteriority. That is not obscure to a thoughtful person. The proponents of this school made the signs of times into times themselves, and therefore timing is reciprocal for them. But if one considers what is time in reality, reciprocity in timing is impossible. Then his saying: 'And an objection is raised against it, etc.' is a reference to what he mentioned in his saying: 'Then the investigation is what you have known, etc.' His saying: 'And an objection is raised against it, etc.' This objection only applies if their words are taken literally. But if it is said: Their intent is that it is an imaginary thing which the imagination abstracts from the conception of the conjunction of events, and the priority of some over others, and their posteriority; and there is no way to understand it or determine it except by considering the events which people make as signs of it, then there is no objection against them. His saying: 'As is their school in al-Shifa' made these schools a counterpart to the school of its being an imaginary thing and said: The proponents of this view make time existent as one thing in itself.' His saying: 'And there has preceded what relates to investigation, etc.' is a reference to what is transmitted from the Eastern investigations that time as it exists for them is the flowing now which corresponds to motion in the sense of intermediacy. His saying: 'It would be necessary that the duration of remaining and the duration of beginning be one and the same time' – he meant the necessity of their being one in essence, so considering diversity through renewal is of no avail, as in the continuous but unstable now.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the Ash'ari definition of time and then gives the reply. The objector argues: if time is identified with the renewing thing itself, then it would be an actual existent, whereas the Ash'aris hold it to be imaginary. Furthermore, if that renewing thing remains the same over a duration, then the whole duration would be one single time, which is absurd. If instead time is defined as conjunction (iqtiran) and companionship (ma'iyya), then any two paired events are together in something, and that something is the time uniting them. The objector then points out that if time were a real entity, then reciprocity of timing (using different events as measures of time) would be impossible, because a real time cannot be both before and after another. The commentator answers by clarifying that the objection only holds if the Ash'ari words are taken literally. However, their true intent is that time is an imaginary thing (mawhum) that the mind extracts from the relation of events. It is not a real entity. Therefore, the objection collapses. The commentator further explains that some later Ash'aris, as cited in al-Shifa', hold that time is a single existent thing, but the earlier view is that it is imaginary, and there is no obligation to accept that time is a real existent. The passage thus refutes the objection by distinguishing between literal interpretation and the intended meaning.

موهوم
وهم
اقتران
معية
توقيت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection disproves the Ash'ari view entirely.
Correction: The answer shows the objection only applies if time is considered a real entity, but the Ash'aris hold it to be imaginary, so the objection fails.

Checkpoint
According to the answer given in the passage, why does the objection to the Ash'ari definition of time fail?
A) Because time is a real entity and the Ash'aris agree
B) Because the Ash'aris intended time as an imaginary thing, not a real existent
C) Because the objection uses false premises
D) Because the Ash'aris reject the idea of reciprocal estimation

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view of time as an imaginary concept resists objections that mistake it for a real entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is merely an imaginary construct abstracted from events, then your 'past' and 'future' exist only in your mind. Does that realization change how you view your past sins or your anxiety about the future? Will you now treat your present moment as the only real arena for obedience to Allah?
?
The objection to the Ash'ari view failed because it assumed time must be a real container. But you live as though time is a real entity—rushing, procrastinating, regretting. Are you willing to examine that assumption? What would it mean for your daily life if you truly internalized that time is not an independent reality but a mental abstraction from the events Allah creates? Will you stop letting 'lack of time' be an excuse for neglecting your duties to Allah?

The Sensory Proof for the Existence of Place
Author argues for existence of place via sensory indication and defines common and philosophical usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of place is argued based on sensory indication, and how place is defined in common and philosophical terms.

Core Idea
Place is proven by pointing, but its reality depends on definition.

This lesson presents the foundational argument for the existence of place (makan) in Islamic philosophy and theology. The author asserts that place exists because we can point to it with sensory indication, using words like 'here' and 'there'. The commentator then clarifies how the term 'place' is used by the common people versus the philosophers, setting the stage for a deeper discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of the author and the detailed commentary.
2
Identify the key argument
Find the statement that place exists 'necessarily because it is pointed to by sensory indication with 'here' and 'there'. This is the core proof.
3
Distinguish common vs. philosophical usage
The commentator explains that 'place' in common usage refers to a container, while philosophers have a more specific definition. Note the examples given: vessel for drink, house for people.
The passage also mentions that place in the technical sense is 'nothing beyond what common people know'.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: If place is just what common people know, does the sensory proof prove a real entity or just a mental concept? This sets up the next lesson.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل ] (في المكان أورده عقيب الزمان لمناسبته إياه في تعلقهما بالحركة ولكونه راجعاً إلى أقسام الكم المتصل على بعض الأقوال وبين أولاً وجوده ثم أشار إلى حقيقته فقال : ( وهو موجود ( ضرورة أنه مشار إليه إشارة حسية ) بهنا وهناك و ضرورة قوله : ( عبارة عن الاقتران أي عن المتجدد من حيث الاقتران والمعية . قوله : ( في المكان في الشفاء لفظ المكان قد يستعمله العامة لما يكون الشيء مستقراً عليه وربما عنوا بالمكان الشيء الحاوي للشيء، كالدن للشراب والبيت للناس وبالجملة ما يكون فيه الشيء وإن لم يستقر عليه وهذا هو الأغلب عندهم وإن لم يشعروا به إذ الجمهور منهم يجعلون السهم ينفذ في مكان وأنَّ السماء والأرض عند من فهم صورة العالم منهم مستقرة في مكان وإن لم يعتمد على شيء لكن الحكماء وجدوا للشيء الذي يقع عليه اسم المكان بالمعنى الثاني أوصافاً مثل أن يكون الشيء فيه ويفارقه بالحركة ولا يسعه معه غيره إلخ انتهى، ومنه أنَّ المكان بالمعنى المصطلح ليس أمراً وراء ما يعرف العامة. قوله : ( مشار إليه) إِنَّ أراد به مشار إليه بالذات فممنوع، وإن أراد أنه مشار إليه ولو بتبعية الجسم المتمكن فمسلم ، لكنه لا يقتضي ذلك وجوده، بل وجود ما ينتزع منه ويشار إليه بتبعيته كما هو مذهب الأشاعرة . قوله: (ضرورة أنه مشار إليه إشارة حسية فيه بحث، أما أولاً : فلما قيل من أنَّ الحكماء جوزوا الإشارة الحسية إلى النقطة في وسط الخط، وإلى الخط في وسط السطح مع أنهما موهومان لأن الخط عندهم ليس مركباً من النقط ولا السطح من الخطوط بل هما متصلان لا مفصل فيهما فلا يلزم عندهم كون المشار إليه بالإشارة الحسية موجوداً في الخارج. بل يلزم أحد الأمرين إما وجوده فيه أو وجود المحل الذي يتوهم المشار إليه فيه وأما ثانياً : فلان المشار إليه إشارة حسية بهنا وهناك هو ما يقال له المكان في العرف العام فلا يثبت به وجود المكان الحقيقي، ويمكن أن يدفع الثاني بأن جمهور العقلاء يشيرون إلى الطير الواقف في الهواء بأنه هناك، مع أنه لامكان بالمعنى العامي كما سيذكره .
The Second Observatory - The Ninth Objective: Place, and it is of continuous quantity. (He brought it after time due to its suitability to it in their relation to motion, and because it goes back to divisions of continuous quantity according to some opinions. And he first showed its existence, then pointed to its reality, and said: It exists necessarily because it is pointed to by sensory indication with 'here' and 'there', and necessarily. His saying: 'Expression concerning conjunction' i.e. concerning the renewing from the aspect of conjunction and companionship. His saying: 'Concerning place in al-Shifa': the word 'place' is used by the common people for that on which a thing rests, and sometimes they mean by place the thing that contains a thing, like the vessel for drink and the house for people. In general, what a thing is in, even if it does not rest upon it. And this is the most common among them, even if they are not aware of it. Indeed, the common people consider an arrow to penetrate into a place, and that the heavens and earth, according to those who understand the form of the world among them, are stable in a place even if they do not depend on anything. But the philosophers found for the thing that receives the name 'place' in the second sense attributes such as that the thing is in it and separates from it by motion, and nothing else can contain it with it, etc. End. And from this, place in the technical sense is nothing beyond what the common people know. His saying: 'Pointed to' – if he means pointed to in itself, it is denied; if he means it is pointed to even by way of dependence on the body occupying it, it is accepted, but that does not entail its existence, rather the existence of something abstracted from it and pointed to by following it, as is the Ash'ari school. His saying: 'Necessarily because it is pointed to by sensory indication' – there is a discussion in it. Firstly, because it is said: The philosophers allow sensory indication to a point in the middle of a line, and to a line in the middle of a surface, even though both are imaginary; because the line, according to them, is not composed of points, nor the surface of lines; rather they are continuous without a joint. So in their view, it is not necessary that what is indicated by sensory indication be existent in the external world. Rather, one of two things is necessary: either its existence in it, or the existence of the locus in which the indicated thing is imagined. Secondly, because the thing pointed to by sensory indication with 'here' and 'there' is what is called place in common usage, so it does not prove the existence of real place. The second [point] can be refuted by [saying] that the majority of rational people point to a bird standing in the air as 'there', even though there is no place in the common sense, as he will mention.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage discusses the existence and definition of place (makan). It begins by stating that place is a type of continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttasil) and that it is discussed after time due to their shared relation to motion. The author first argues for its existence: place exists because it is pointed to by sensory indication (ishara hissiyya) with 'here' and 'there'. This is a common proof. The commentator then explains that the term 'place' in common usage refers to what contains a thing, but the philosophers (hukama') define it more precisely, e.g., as a surface that contains a body away from it and is equal to it. The commentator adds that there is a discussion about whether sensory pointing proves real existence. The point about the point in a line shows that one can point to something imaginary. Thus, pointing to 'here' and 'there' may only indicate something mental, not a real entity. The commentator concludes that place in the technical sense may be nothing beyond what the common people understand, and that the Ash'ari school holds that place is not a real entity but an abstracted concept. Finally, the commentator refutes a possible objection by noting that even ordinary people point to a bird in the air as 'there' even though it has no container, showing that sensory pointing does not require a real place.

Key Terms
مكان
كم متصل
إشارة حسية
متمكن
عرف عام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensory pointing to a location alone proves the existence of a real, distinct entity called 'place'.
Correction: The passage shows that the term 'place' in common usage may be simply a mental concept derived from bodies, and that the technical sense does not go beyond what is commonly understood. The proof is not decisive without further analysis.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason given for the existence of place?
A) It is mentioned in scripture
B) It is pointed to by sensory indication with 'here' and 'there'
C) It is necessary for motion
D) It is defined by philosophers

Key Takeaway
Place's existence is argued from sensory pointing, but its exact nature as a real entity or a mental abstraction remains open.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you point to 'here' or 'there', do you ever pause to consider that this simple act is evidence for something real? If not, what does that say about your neglect of the signs around you? Make a commitment to be more mindful of such sensory proofs today.
?
The passage concludes that the technical meaning of place is nothing beyond what common people know. Does this humble you to realize that profound truths often lie in the ordinary? What ordinary experiences have you dismissed that might contain deep spiritual lessons? Will you look for them starting now?
?
If place exists because it is sensorily perceived, then your own location and movement are not trivial—they are part of a divine order. How often do you thank Allah for the simple fact that you are somewhere? Will you make a daily practice of reflecting on your surroundings as signs of His creation?

Critique of the Sensory Proof for Place
Commentator critiques the sensory proof for place and defends the Ash'ari view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections to the sensory proof for place and the Ash'ari response.

Core Idea
Sensory pointing alone does not prove a real place; it may point to imaginary or derivative things.

This lesson examines the critical discussion that follows the proof of place. The commentator raises two objections: first, that sensory indication can point to imaginary things like a point on a line; second, that the pointing with 'here' and 'there' only indicates place in common usage, not a real entity. He then offers a refutation by noting that even common people point to a bird in the air without implying a container.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the objections and refutation. Then read the translation carefully.
Notice the commentator's dialectical style.
2
Identify the Ash'ari position
The commentator first clarifies that 'pointed to' can mean pointed to by dependence on the body, which does not entail a real place. This is the Ash'ari view.
This sets the stage for the objections.
3
Understand the two objections
First objection: philosophers point to imaginary things like a point on a line, so pointing does not guarantee external existence. Second objection: pointing with 'here' and 'there' refers to common usage, not a real place.
4
Follow the refutation
The commentator refutes the second objection using the example of a bird in the air. People say 'there' even though there is no common sense place, showing that pointing does not require a real container.
This supports the Ash'ari position that place is an abstraction.
5
Reflect on overall argument
Ask yourself: Does the sensory proof for place survive these criticisms? What does the refutation imply about the nature of place?
This connects to the lesson's learning objective.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل ] (في المكان أورده عقيب الزمان لمناسبته إياه في تعلقهما بالحركة ولكونه راجعاً إلى أقسام الكم المتصل على بعض الأقوال وبين أولاً وجوده ثم أشار إلى حقيقته فقال : ( وهو موجود ( ضرورة أنه مشار إليه إشارة حسية ) بهنا وهناك و ضرورة قوله : ( عبارة عن الاقتران أي عن المتجدد من حيث الاقتران والمعية . قوله : ( في المكان في الشفاء لفظ المكان قد يستعمله العامة لما يكون الشيء مستقراً عليه وربما عنوا بالمكان الشيء الحاوي للشيء، كالدن للشراب والبيت للناس وبالجملة ما يكون فيه الشيء وإن لم يستقر عليه وهذا هو الأغلب عندهم وإن لم يشعروا به إذ الجمهور منهم يجعلون السهم ينفذ في مكان وأنَّ السماء والأرض عند من فهم صورة العالم منهم مستقرة في مكان وإن لم يعتمد على شيء لكن الحكماء وجدوا للشيء الذي يقع عليه اسم المكان بالمعنى الثاني أوصافاً مثل أن يكون الشيء فيه ويفارقه بالحركة ولا يسعه معه غيره إلخ انتهى، ومنه أنَّ المكان بالمعنى المصطلح ليس أمراً وراء ما يعرف العامة. قوله : ( مشار إليه) إِنَّ أراد به مشار إليه بالذات فممنوع، وإن أراد أنه مشار إليه ولو بتبعية الجسم المتمكن فمسلم ، لكنه لا يقتضي ذلك وجوده، بل وجود ما ينتزع منه ويشار إليه بتبعيته كما هو مذهب الأشاعرة . قوله: (ضرورة أنه مشار إليه إشارة حسية فيه بحث، أما أولاً : فلما قيل من أنَّ الحكماء جوزوا الإشارة الحسية إلى النقطة في وسط الخط، وإلى الخط في وسط السطح مع أنهما موهومان لأن الخط عندهم ليس مركباً من النقط ولا السطح من الخطوط بل هما متصلان لا مفصل فيهما فلا يلزم عندهم كون المشار إليه بالإشارة الحسية موجوداً في الخارج. بل يلزم أحد الأمرين إما وجوده فيه أو وجود المحل الذي يتوهم المشار إليه فيه وأما ثانياً : فلان المشار إليه إشارة حسية بهنا وهناك هو ما يقال له المكان في العرف العام فلا يثبت به وجود المكان الحقيقي، ويمكن أن يدفع الثاني بأن جمهور العقلاء يشيرون إلى الطير الواقف في الهواء بأنه هناك، مع أنه لامكان بالمعنى العامي كما سيذكره .
The Second Observatory - The Ninth Objective: Place, and it is of continuous quantity. (He brought it after time due to its suitability to it in their relation to motion, and because it goes back to divisions of continuous quantity according to some opinions. And he first showed its existence, then pointed to its reality, and said: It exists necessarily because it is pointed to by sensory indication with 'here' and 'there', and necessarily. His saying: 'Expression concerning conjunction' i.e. concerning the renewing from the aspect of conjunction and companionship. His saying: 'Concerning place in al-Shifa': the word 'place' is used by the common people for that on which a thing rests, and sometimes they mean by place the thing that contains a thing, like the vessel for drink and the house for people. In general, what a thing is in, even if it does not rest upon it. And this is the most common among them, even if they are not aware of it. Indeed, the common people consider an arrow to penetrate into a place, and that the heavens and earth, according to those who understand the form of the world among them, are stable in a place even if they do not depend on anything. But the philosophers found for the thing that receives the name 'place' in the second sense attributes such as that the thing is in it and separates from it by motion, and nothing else can contain it with it, etc. End. And from this, place in the technical sense is nothing beyond what the common people know. His saying: 'Pointed to' – if he means pointed to in itself, it is denied; if he means it is pointed to even by way of dependence on the body occupying it, it is accepted, but that does not entail its existence, rather the existence of something abstracted from it and pointed to by following it, as is the Ash'ari school. His saying: 'Necessarily because it is pointed to by sensory indication' – there is a discussion in it. Firstly, because it is said: The philosophers allow sensory indication to a point in the middle of a line, and to a line in the middle of a surface, even though both are imaginary; because the line, according to them, is not composed of points, nor the surface of lines; rather they are continuous without a joint. So in their view, it is not necessary that what is indicated by sensory indication be existent in the external world. Rather, one of two things is necessary: either its existence in it, or the existence of the locus in which the indicated thing is imagined. Secondly, because the thing pointed to by sensory indication with 'here' and 'there' is what is called place in common usage, so it does not prove the existence of real place. The second [point] can be refuted by [saying] that the majority of rational people point to a bird standing in the air as 'there', even though there is no place in the common sense, as he will mention.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage discusses the existence and definition of place (makan). It begins by stating that place is a type of continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttasil) and that it is discussed after time due to their shared relation to motion. The author first argues for its existence: place exists because it is pointed to by sensory indication (ishara hissiyya) with 'here' and 'there'. This is a common proof. The commentator then explains that the term 'place' in common usage refers to what contains a thing, but the philosophers (hukama') define it more precisely, e.g., as a surface that contains a body away from it and is equal to it. The commentator adds that there is a discussion about whether sensory pointing proves real existence. The point about the point in a line shows that one can point to something imaginary. Thus, pointing to 'here' and 'there' may only indicate something mental, not a real entity. The commentator concludes that place in the technical sense may be nothing beyond what the common people understand, and that the Ash'ari school holds that place is not a real entity but an abstracted concept. Finally, the commentator refutes a possible objection by noting that even ordinary people point to a bird in the air as 'there' even though it has no container, showing that sensory pointing does not require a real place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we can point to something sensorily, it must be a real external object.
Correction: The passage shows that one can point to imaginary things (like a point on a line) and to things in common usage (like 'here' and 'there') that do not correspond to a real entity. Pointing does not guarantee external existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one reason why sensory indication does not prove the existence of real place?
A) Because pointing is subjective
B) Because one can point to imaginary things like a point on a line

Key Takeaway
The sensory proof for place is undermined by the possibility of pointing to imaginary or common constructs; place may be an abstraction rather than a real entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at a bird in the sky, you think you know where it is. But this passage tells you that the 'there' you point to is a mental construct, not a real space. Has it ever occurred to you that your most basic perceptions of location might be illusions? How does this realization call you to rely less on your senses and more on the Quran and Sunnah for understanding reality? Commit to reflecting on one sensory assumption you hold that might be false.
?
The Ash'ari argument reduces place to an abstraction. This challenges the very fabric of how you experience the world. If the 'place' you inhabit is not a real entity, then what does that say about your attachment to worldly spaces—your home, your city? Are you holding onto a mirage? This lesson should make you examine whether you have placed too much significance on physical location instead of your relationship with Allah.
?
The scholars' debate on place may seem like a dry theological point, but it strips away a layer of presumed reality. How much of your faith is built on assumptions that have not been critically examined? After reading this passage, you must ask yourself: Are you willing to let go of a comfortable understanding of the world if the evidence points otherwise? Let this lesson push you to seek truth even when it unsettles your base beliefs.

The True Nature of Time in Kalam
Sanusi defines time as the measure of simultaneity and discusses its relational nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of time as the measure of simultaneity and its distinction from events and conventional markers.

Core Idea
Time is not events but the container of their togetherness.

Before engaging in deeper theological discussions about time and creation, we must first clarify what time really means in Islamic kalam. This passage from Sanusi presents a precise definition of time as the relational measure of simultaneity, differentiating it from the events that occur in time and from the conventional markers people often mistake for time.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic passage once slowly, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to how the author defines time and how he relates it to events.
Notice the repetitive structure: 'كل معينين فهما في أمر ما معاً...'
2
Identify the Core Definition
Find where the author gives the definition of time. He says: 'فذلك الشيء الذي فيه المعية هو الوقت' — that thing which contains the togetherness is time.
The definition hinges on the concept of 'المعية' (simultaneity or togetherness).
3
Distinguish Time from Events
The author emphasizes that togetherness (المعية) is not the thing in which events occur; rather it is an accident (عارضة) measured relative to the container. This distinguishes time as a relation, not a substance.
4
Understand the Critique of Markers
The author criticizes those who make the markers of time (أعلام الأوقات) into time itself, which leads to reversals in timing. The correct view is that time is a real measure, not conventional signs.
This connects to the larger debate about the nature of time in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل معينين فهما في أمر ما معاً فذلك الشيء الذي فيه المعية هو الوقت الذي يجمعهما ويمكن أن يجعل كل منهما دالاً عليه بل يمكن أن يدل عليه بغيرهما من الأمور الواقعة فيه فليست المعية نفس ما يقع فيه الحوادث بل هي عارضة لها مقيسة إلى ماتقع فيه وكذلك القبلية والبعدية وذلك مما لا يشتبه على متأمل فأصحاب هذا المذهب جعلوا أعلام الأوقات أوقاتاً ولذلك يتعاكس التوقيت عندهم وإذا اعتبر ما هو وقت في الحقيقة امتنع التعكيس في التوقيت .
Every two specified things are together in some matter, so that thing in which the togetherness is, is the time that brings them together. It is possible to make each of them indicative of it, rather it is possible to indicate it by other than them from the occurrences happening in it. So the togetherness is not the very thing in which events occur, rather it is an accident of them measured relative to that in which they occur. Likewise for beforeness and afterness. This is not ambiguous for a ponderer. So the adherents of this school made the markers of times into times, therefore timings are reversed according to them. And if what is truly time is considered, then reversal in timing becomes impossible.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of time (الوقت). It begins by defining time as the thing that contains the togetherness of two specified things. Time is not the events themselves but a relation (عارضة) measured relative to the container. The passage then criticizes those who make the markers of time (أعلام الأوقات) into time itself, leading to reversals in timing. The correct view is that time is a real measure, not the conventional markers.

Key Terms
الوقت
المعية
القبلية
البعدية
التوقيت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think time is the events themselves or the conventional markers (like the movement of the sun).
Correction: The author states that time is not the events nor the markers; it is the thing that contains the togetherness (المعية) of events, an accident measured relative to the container.

Checkpoint
What does the author state time is in relation to two simultaneous events?
A) The events themselves
B) The thing containing the togetherness of the two events
C) The motion of the spheres
D) The a priori form of intuition

Key Takeaway
Time is the relational measure of simultaneity (المعية) and beforeness/afterness (القبلية والبعدية), not the events themselves or their conventional markers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often measure your life by achievements, age, and calendar dates. But according to this lesson, those are only markers. What is the true 'container' that holds your deeds together? Are you more concerned with the container (your relationship with Allah) or the markers?
?
The author says confusion leads to reversal. In your daily schedule, do you ever reverse priorities—placing worldly markers (work deadlines, social events) ahead of the togetherness with Allah? How can you realign your timing today to reflect what is truly time?

The First Proof: Existence of Place from Bodily Transition
First wajh: transition from and to place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first necessary indication for the existence of place: that bodies move from one location to another, implying a real substrate.

Core Idea
Bodies move from place to place; therefore place must exist.

This lesson introduces the first of four necessary proofs for the existence of place. Al-Sanusi begins by defining place as a continuous quantity and then presents the observation that bodies are seen to be present, then disappear, and other bodies appear in their place. This phenomenon requires a substrate – place – that receives these changes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, including the definition and the first proof. Let the author's own words come through before interpreting.
Notice how the definition connects place to continuous quantity.
2
Understand the observation
The proof begins with an observable fact: bodies are present, then vanish, and other bodies appear. Ask yourself what this implies.
3
Grasp the logical implication
Transition from one location to another requires a real substrate—place—that the body leaves and enters. The commentary explains that transition is exchange of proximity and distance.
Place is an imaginary limit between bodies, but its existence is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل
(أنه ينتقل منه الجسم و ينتقل إليه). فإنا نشاهد الجسم يكون حاضراً ثم يغيب ويحضر جسم آخر من حيث هو (و) ضرورة ) أنه مقدار له نصف وثلث )، فإن مكان النصف نصف مكان الكل وكذا الحال في الثلث والربع ( و ) ضرورة ( أنه متفاوت فيه زيادة ونقصان ، فإنَّ مكان الكبير يزيد على مكان الصغير ( ولا يتصور شيء منها )، أي من الأمور المذكورة ( للعدم المحض) . فإن المعدوم في الخارج لا يقبل الإشارة الحسية ولا يتصور انتقال الجسم منه ،وإليه ولا يقبل التقدير بالتنصيف والتثليث ولا يتصف بالزيادة والنقصان وهذه وجوه أربعة نبه بها على وجود المكان
.
مع كونه قوله : ( وضرورة أنه ينتقل منه الجسم وإليه فيه أنَّ الانتقال ليس إلا استبدال القرب والبعد نص عليه في الشفاء. فاللازم منه وجود ما يقرب الجسم فيه وما يبعد عنه، وإنما نسب الانتقال إلى المكان لكونه محدوداً وهمياً باعتبار وقوعه بين الأجسام التي حصل القرب والبعد عنها . قوله : ( فإنَّ مكان النصف إلخ ) فيه أنَّ هذا تقدير وتنصيف بتبع الجسم لا بالذات فاللازم وجوده وكذا الكلام في أنه متفاوت .
عنه
قوله : ( فإن المعدوم إلخ) أي المعدوم في الخارج لا تتعلق به الإشارة الحسية بل لا بد من وجوده حين تعلق الإشارة، سواء كانت قبل التعلق موجوداً أولا كالنقطة في الخط والخط في السطح فإنها حين الإشارة موجودة، وإن لم تكن قبلها موجودة ولا يلزم أن يكون كل نقطة أو خط نهاية .
قوله : ( وأنه ينتقل منه الجسم وإليه المنتقل إليه بالحصول فيه يجب أن يكون موجوداً وقت الانتقال . وأما المنتقل إليه بتحصيله فيمتنع وجوده حال الانتقال، كالكيفية التي تتوجه إلى الجسم حال حركته في الكيف هذا هو المشهور وفيه اعتراض مشهور وهو أنه لا يستقيم على تقدير كون المكان هو السطح كيف والطير الذي يطير من موضع إلى موضع في الهواء ينتقل إلى ما انتهى إليه حركته مع كونه معدوماً قبل وصوله إليه لكون الهواء متصلاً عندهم في نفسه لا سطح موجوداً في جوفه، فإذا خرقه المتحرك بحجمه حصل هناك سطح محيط به. ويمكن أن يجاب عنه هاهنا بأنَّ المدعى وجوب وجود المنتقل إليه ولو حال انقطاع الحركة لا حال الحركة. غاية ما في الباب اشتراك جميع الحركات في هذا الأمر. قوله : ( ولا يتصف بالزيادة والنقصان فإن قلت : الواقف على طرف العالم إن لم يمكنه مد اليد إلى الخارج فهناك جسم مانع وإن أمكنه ذلك فالذي يتسع من خارج العالم طرف أصبعه غير متسع لكل يده فخارج العالم قابل للزيادة والنقصان مع قلت : أنه لا شيء محض عندهم، تعذر مد اليد لا لوجود مانع بل لعدم الشرط، وهو عدم الحيز والمكان ومنه علم أن وقوف ذي اليد على طرف العالم مما لا يمكن له ولو أمكن لم يحتج إلى قصة مد اليد . بل يقال : ما يتسع كل الواقف أزيد مما يتسع بعضه. اللهم إلا أن يفرض الوقوف بحيث لا يتجاوز سطح الواقف سطح العالم تأمل.
The Second Observatory - The Ninth Aim: Place, and it is of continuous quantity. (That the body moves from it and moves to it). For we observe the body being present, then it disappears, and another body appears in its place. (And) necessarily (it is a quantity having a half and a third), for the place of the half is half the place of the whole, and likewise for the third and quarter. (And) necessarily (it is variable in increase and decrease), for the place of the large increases over the place of the small. (And none of these things are conceivable) i.e., of the mentioned matters (for pure non-being). For non-being in external reality does not admit of sensory indication, nor is transition of body from it and to it conceivable, nor does it admit of estimation by halving and thirding, nor is it characterized by increase and decrease. These are four aspects by which he alerts to the existence of place. With his statement: (And necessarily that the body moves from it and to it) contains that transition is nothing but the exchange of proximity and distance, as stated in al-Shifa'. So what follows from it is the existence of that in which the body approaches and from which it distances. And the attribution of transition to place is only because it is an imaginary limit in consideration of its occurrence between the bodies that have proximity and distance. His statement: (For the place of the half, etc.) contains that this is an estimation and division following the body, not essentially, so its existence is necessary. Likewise the statement about its being variable. His statement: (For non-being, etc.) i.e., non-being in external reality has no sensory indication attached to it; rather its existence is necessary at the time of the attachment of the indication, whether it existed before the attachment or not, like the point in the line and the line in the surface; for they exist at the time of indication, even if they did not exist before it, and it is not necessary that every point or line be an endpoint. His statement: (And that the body moves from it and to it) the place moved to by obtaining in it must exist at the time of transition. As for the place moved to by its actualization, its existence at the moment of transition is impossible, like the quality that is directed towards the body during its motion in quality. This is the well-known view, and there is a well-known objection: it is not sound on the assumption that place is the surface, for the bird that flies from a place to a place in the air moves to what its motion ends at, despite its being non-existent before its arrival, because the air is continuous in itself, with no surface existing in its interior, so when the moving body pierces it with its bulk, a surface surrounding it occurs there. And it can be answered here that the claim is the necessity of the existence of the moved-to, even if at the moment of cessation of motion, not at the moment of motion. The extent of the matter is the sharing of all motions in this. His statement: (And it is not characterized by increase and decrease) for if you say: The one standing at the edge of the world, if he cannot extend his hand outward, then there is a prevenient body; if he can, then what widens from outside the world by the tip of his finger is not widening for his whole hand, so the outside of the world is subject to increase and decrease. Then I say: Non-being is pure nothing in their view; the inability to extend the hand is not due to the existence of an obstacle but to the absence of the condition, which is the absence of space and place. From this it is known that the standing of one with a hand at the edge of the world is impossible for him, and if it were possible, he would not need the story of extending the hand. Rather, it is said: what expands for the whole of the standing person is greater than what expands for part of him. Unless it is assumed that the standing is such that the surface of the standing does not exceed the surface of the world. Reflect.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage defines place and provides four necessary proofs for its existence. It begins by identifying place as a type of continuous quantity. The first proof: we observe bodies moving from one location to another, implying a substrate that receives them. The second proof: place has magnitude (half, third), shown by the half of a body occupying half the place of the whole—this requires a real quantity. The third proof: place varies in size (larger bodies have larger places), so it admits increase and decrease. The fourth proof: pure non-being (al-‘adam al-mahd) cannot possess any of these properties—sensory indication, transition, divisibility, variability—therefore place cannot be non-being, so it must exist. Each proof is explained in the commentary: transition is exchange of proximity, place is an imaginary limit; the magnitude and variability are secondary to the body but still imply a real substrate; non-being lacks all relevant attributes. The commentary also discusses nuances such as the difference between moving to a place by obtaining in it versus by actualization, and addresses a famous objection about a bird flying through continuous air—the answer is that the claim concerns the end of motion, not the moment of motion.

Key Terms
مکان
کم متصل
انتقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the proofs for place are empirical rather than apriori necessary.
Correction: The proof is based on a rational necessity (darura), not just empirical observation. The transition phenomenon triggers the rational judgment that place must exist.

Checkpoint
What is the key observable fact used in the first proof for the existence of place?
A) Bodies have weight and occupy space.
B) Bodies are present, then disappear, and other bodies appear in their place.
C) Places have definite shapes.
D) Bodies move only in straight lines.

Key Takeaway
Bodily transition necessitates the existence of place as a real substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every time you move from one room to another, you rely on the necessity that place exists. But do you ever pause to reflect that this simple act is a proof of a reality beyond your senses? How does that reshape your awareness of the unseen realities that your daily life depends on? Will you commit to remembering this proof when you walk today?
?
The proof for place begins with the observation of bodies moving. But what about the movement of your own soul—your shifting thoughts and intentions? Does your inner life also demand a substrate? If place is necessary for physical change, what does that imply about the permanence of your own self? Are you content to be a mere changing body, or is there a deeper abiding reality within you?
?
Imagine if place did not exist—every transition would be impossible. Yet you experience transition constantly. This lesson reveals that reason itself forces you to affirm an unseen reality. Are you living as if the unseen is as real as the seen? What would change in your life if you truly internalized that the necessary truths of reason point to a greater, invisible order?

The Second and Third Proofs: Place as a Quantity with Magnitude and Variability
Second and third wajh: magnitude and variability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proofs from magnitude (half/third) and variability (increase/decrease) that place must exist as a real quantity.

Core Idea
Place has magnitude and varies; these require a real substrate.

In this lesson, we examine two further necessary indications: place is divisible into halves and thirds, and it admits increase and decrease. These properties are only conceivable for a real continuum, not for non-being. Al-Sanusi argues that the place of a half is half the place of the whole, and the place of a larger body is larger, which implies that place is a real quantity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on magnitude
Read the part of the matn that says place has a half and a third, and the commentary that explains this is an estimation following the body.
The example: the place of the half is half the place of the whole.
2
Understand the argument from magnitude
If a body is divided into halves, each half occupies half the place of the whole. Therefore place must have real magnitude (it is a quantity).
3
Consider the variability proof
The place of a larger body is larger than that of a smaller body. This shows place admits increase and decrease, another property of real quantities.
This is the third wajh, but presented together in the commentary.
4
Link to necessity
Both magnitude and variability are properties that cannot belong to non-being, so place must exist as a real entity.
These proofs reinforce the conclusion from the first wajh.

The Second Observatory - The Ninth Aim: Place, and it is of continuous quantity. (That the body moves from it and moves to it). For we observe the body being present, then it disappears, and another body appears in its place. (And) necessarily (it is a quantity having a half and a third), for the place of the half is half the place of the whole, and likewise for the third and quarter. (And) necessarily (it is variable in increase and decrease), for the place of the large increases over the place of the small. (And none of these things are conceivable) i.e., of the mentioned matters (for pure non-being). For non-being in external reality does not admit of sensory indication, nor is transition of body from it and to it conceivable, nor does it admit of estimation by halving and thirding, nor is it characterized by increase and decrease. These are four aspects by which he alerts to the existence of place. With his statement: (And necessarily that the body moves from it and to it) contains that transition is nothing but the exchange of proximity and distance, as stated in al-Shifa'. So what follows from it is the existence of that in which the body approaches and from which it distances. And the attribution of transition to place is only because it is an imaginary limit in consideration of its occurrence between the bodies that have proximity and distance. His statement: (For the place of the half, etc.) contains that this is an estimation and division following the body, not essentially, so its existence is necessary. Likewise the statement about its being variable. His statement: (For non-being, etc.) i.e., non-being in external reality has no sensory indication attached to it; rather its existence is necessary at the time of the attachment of the indication, whether it existed before the attachment or not, like the point in the line and the line in the surface; for they exist at the time of indication, even if they did not exist before it, and it is not necessary that every point or line be an endpoint. His statement: (And that the body moves from it and to it) the place moved to by obtaining in it must exist at the time of transition. As for the place moved to by its actualization, its existence at the moment of transition is impossible, like the quality that is directed towards the body during its motion in quality. This is the well-known view, and there is a well-known objection: it is not sound on the assumption that place is the surface, for the bird that flies from a place to a place in the air moves to what its motion ends at, despite its being non-existent before its arrival, because the air is continuous in itself, with no surface existing in its interior, so when the moving body pierces it with its bulk, a surface surrounding it occurs there. And it can be answered here that the claim is the necessity of the existence of the moved-to, even if at the moment of cessation of motion, not at the moment of motion. The extent of the matter is the sharing of all motions in this. His statement: (And it is not characterized by increase and decrease) for if you say: The one standing at the edge of the world, if he cannot extend his hand outward, then there is a prevenient body; if he can, then what widens from outside the world by the tip of his finger is not widening for his whole hand, so the outside of the world is subject to increase and decrease. Then I say: Non-being is pure nothing in their view; the inability to extend the hand is not due to the existence of an obstacle but to the absence of the condition, which is the absence of space and place. From this it is known that the standing of one with a hand at the edge of the world is impossible for him, and if it were possible, he would not need the story of extending the hand. Rather, it is said: what expands for the whole of the standing person is greater than what expands for part of him. Unless it is assumed that the standing is such that the surface of the standing does not exceed the surface of the world. Reflect.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage defines place and provides four necessary proofs for its existence. It begins by identifying place as a type of continuous quantity. The first proof: we observe bodies moving from one location to another, implying a substrate that receives them. The second proof: place has magnitude (half, third), shown by the half of a body occupying half the place of the whole—this requires a real quantity. The third proof: place varies in size (larger bodies have larger places), so it admits increase and decrease. The fourth proof: pure non-being (al-‘adam al-mahd) cannot possess any of these properties—sensory indication, transition, divisibility, variability—therefore place cannot be non-being, so it must exist. Each proof is explained in the commentary: transition is exchange of proximity, place is an imaginary limit; the magnitude and variability are secondary to the body but still imply a real substrate; non-being lacks all relevant attributes. The commentary also discusses nuances such as the difference between moving to a place by obtaining in it versus by actualization, and addresses a famous objection about a bird flying through continuous air—the answer is that the claim concerns the end of motion, not the moment of motion.

Key Terms
کم متصل
تنصيف
تفاوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That place's magnitude is intrinsic and independent of bodies.
Correction: The commentary explains that place's division and variability are 'following the body' (bi-taba' al-jism), not essential properties of place alone.

Checkpoint
According to the second wajh, what does the fact that the place of a half is half the place of the whole indicate?
A) Place is a spiritual entity.
B) Place has real magnitude.
C) Place is eternal.
D) Place is imaginary.

Key Takeaway
The divisibility and variability of place show it is a real continuous quantity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you walk from a narrow hallway into a wide open space, do you recognize that the very variability of place is a sign of Allah's continuous creation? How can you train your heart to see every place you occupy as a created gift?
?
Consider the fact that place has real magnitude and can be divided. Does this make you more aware of the order and precision in Allah's creation? What would it mean for your worship if you truly believed every inch of space holds a divine purpose?
?
If place is a real continuous quantity, then your location matters to Allah. Do you live with the mindfulness that where you are right now is known to Him and carries accountability? What change will you make today in how you treat the places you inhabit?

The Fourth Proof: Non-Being Cannot Possess the Properties of Place
Fourth wajh: impossibility of properties for pure non-being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth necessary indication: that pure non-being lacks sensory indication, transition, divisibility, and variability, therefore place must exist.

Core Idea
Pure non-being lacks all place-like attributes, so place is real.

This lesson presents the fourth proof, which is a negative argument. If place did not exist, then the properties we observe – transition, magnitude, variability – would have to apply to pure non-being, which is impossible. Therefore, place exists. The commentary also addresses objections about motion through air and the extension of a hand beyond the world, showing that these do not undermine the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on non-being
Read the main text stating that none of the previous properties can be conceived for pure non-being, and the commentary elaborating.
Pure non-being (al-‘adam al-mahd) is absolute nothingness.
2
Understand the main argument
Since non-being cannot receive sensory indication, cannot be moved from/to, cannot be divided, and does not vary, but we observe these properties in place, place cannot be non-being. Therefore it exists.
3
Consider the bird objection
The objection: a bird flying through air moves to a surface that does not exist before arrival. The answer: the claim is about the existence of the place at the end of motion, not during.
4
Consider the hand outside the world
The hand example shows that if place were non-being, increase/decrease would be impossible. The inability to extend the hand is due to lack of condition, not a prevenient body.
This reinforces that non-being cannot have spatial properties.

(أنه ينتقل منه الجسم و ينتقل إليه). فإنا نشاهد الجسم يكون حاضراً ثم يغيب ويحضر جسم آخر من حيث هو (و) ضرورة ) أنه مقدار له نصف وثلث )، فإن مكان النصف نصف مكان الكل وكذا الحال في الثلث والربع ( و ) ضرورة ( أنه متفاوت فيه زيادة ونقصان ، فإنَّ مكان الكبير يزيد على مكان الصغير ( ولا يتصور شيء منها )، أي من الأمور المذكورة ( للعدم المحض) . فإن المعدوم في الخارج لا يقبل الإشارة الحسية ولا يتصور انتقال الجسم منه ،وإليه ولا يقبل التقدير بالتنصيف والتثليث ولا يتصف بالزيادة والنقصان وهذه وجوه أربعة نبه بها على وجود المكان

How does this distinction between non-being and existence change the way you prepare for the meeting with your Lord?

Objection and Answer: Does Place Lead to Infinite Regress or Circularity?
Objection against place and its two-part refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against the existence of place based on infinite regress and reversal of in-ness is refuted by distinguishing the properties of place and the multiple meanings of 'in'.

Core Idea
Place's self-application is impossible; the word 'in' resolves the reversal objection.

This passage presents a powerful objection against the existence of place, namely that it either leads to infinite regress or contradicts itself. The commentator then provides a twofold answer, clarifying that place cannot be self-predicated and that the term 'in' is ambiguous.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection that place must be either a body or an accident, and see how each leads to absurdity.
2
Understand the commentator's glosses
The commentator clarifies the meaning of key phrases in the objection.
These glosses are important to understand the precise reasoning.
3
Analyze the answer to infinite regress
The answer shows that place cannot be self-referential like light or existence.
4
Analyze the answer to the reversal
The answer uses the polysemy of 'in' to resolve the apparent contradiction.
5
Synthesize the overall refutation
See how the objections are defeated without dismissing the existence of place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشكك عليه أي على وجود المكان بأنه لو وجد المكان فإما متحيز فله مكان إذ لا معنى للمتحيز إلا ذلك وحينئذ لتسلسل الأمكنة إلى غير النهاية إذ لكل مكان مكان آخر على ذلك التقدير أو حال في المتحيز فإما لجسم أي فذلك المتحيز الذي حل فيه المكان إما الجسم الذي متمكن فيه فيكون المكان حينئذ في الجسم لا الجسم في المكان وهذا باطل
هو
قوله : ( إلا ذلك ) أي ما يكون في مكان .
قوله : ( إذ لكل مكان مكان آخر لامتناع كون المكان نفس المتمكن أو جزءه وإلا لانتقل بانتقاله .
قوله : (نبه بها على وجود المكان فالمنوع الواردة على الوجوه الأربعة لا تضر وفي قوله كما أشار إليه بلفظ الضرورة بحث ظاهر وهو أنَّ الضرورة هاهنا دخلت على مقدمة من مقدمات الدليل، وضروريتها لا تستلزم ضرورية الدعوى، اللهم إلا أن يثبت مانقل عن الشارح من أنَّ العلم بكونه مشاراً إليه إشارة حسية يتضمن العلم بأنه موجود كما أن العلم بالإنسانية يتضمن العلم بالحيوانية، فضرورية الأول تستلزم ضرورية الثاني، بقي الكلام في أنه كيف يسمع دعوى الضرورة في حكم أطبق المتكلمون وبعض من قدماء الفلاسفة على خلافه؟ مع أن القول بأن دعوى الضرورة غير مسموعة في محل النزاع شائع بينهم. وقد نبهناك في المرصد الثالث في أقسام العلم على وجه الرد والقبول فليتذكر.
قوله: (وحينئذ تتسلسل الأمكنة فإن قلت : المتحيز إذا كان غير مكان فله مكان زائد وإذا كان مكاناً فله مكان هو نفسه على قياس ماقيل في الضوء والوجود، والتقدم الزماني لأجزاء الزمان فلا يلزم التسلسل . قلت : للمكان خواص متساوية لا تتصور في الشيء بالنسبة إلى نفسه، ولا كذلك الحال في الأمثلة السابقة. قوله : ( فيكون المكان في الجسم لا الجسم في المكان) رد عليه بأن لكلمة في معان فيجوز أن يكون الجسم في مكان بأحدها والعكس بمعناها الآخر مثلاً يكون الجسم في بمعنى كونه مالئاً له، والمكان فيه بمعنى قيامه به ولا منافاة بينهما فإن قلت : معنى قوله : لا
It was objected to him (i.e., to the existence of place): If place exists, then either it is a body (mutahayyiz), so it has a place—for there is no meaning of body except that—and then the places would infinitely regress, since every place would have another place on that assumption; or it is an accident in a body, so that body is either the body in which it inheres, so then the place would be in the body, not the body in the place—and this is false. This is the objection. His statement: (Except that) i.e., what is in a place. His statement: (Since every place has another place) because it is impossible for place to be the same as the thing occupying it or a part of it, otherwise it would move with its motion. His statement: (He alerted to the existence of place by them) so the preventives that come upon the four aspects do not harm. And in his statement 'as he indicated by the word necessity' there is an apparent investigation, namely that necessity here entered upon a premise of the proof, and its necessity does not entail the necessity of the claim—unless what was transmitted from the commentator is established: that knowledge that it is sensorily pointed to entails knowledge that it exists, just as knowledge of humanity entails knowledge of animality, so the necessity of the first entails the necessity of the second. There remains discussion: how can the claim of necessity be heard in a ruling on which the theologians and some of the ancient philosophers have agreed to the contrary? While the statement that the claim of necessity is not heard in a disputed matter is common among them. We have alerted you in the Third Observatory on the divisions of knowledge to the way of rejection and acceptance, so let it be remembered. His statement: (And then the places would regress infinitely) for if you say: The body, if it is not a place, then it has an extra place; and if it is a place, then it has a place which is itself, by analogy with what was said about light and existence, and the temporal priority of parts of time, so infinite regress does not follow. I say: Place has equal properties that are not conceivable in a thing with respect to itself, unlike the previous examples. His statement: (So the place would be in the body, not the body in the place) is answered by the fact that the word 'in' has multiple meanings. So it is permissible that the body be in place in one sense, and the opposite in its other sense, e.g., 'body in' meaning it fills it, and 'place in' meaning it subsists in it, and there is no conflict. Then if you say: the meaning of his statement: no...
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the existence of place and its refutation. The objection: If place exists, it is either a body (mutahayyiz) or an accident. If it is a body, then it itself has a place, leading to an infinite regress. If it is an accident, it inheres in a body, so the place would be inside the body, not the body inside the place—which contradicts the notion of place. The commentator then supplies the answer. First, regarding infinite regress: the objector might argue that if a body is not a place, it needs an extra place; if it is a place, its place is itself, like light and existence are not distinct from their subjects, and temporal priority does not require infinite regress. But the answer is that place has uniform properties that cannot apply to a thing with respect to itself, so the analogy fails. Second, regarding the reversal of in-ness: the word 'in' (fī) is ambiguous. The body is 'in' its place as a filler, while the place is 'in' the body as a substrate (qiyām). There is no contradiction. The passage also discusses whether the argument can be considered necessary, noting that some object to the claim of necessity in disputed matters, and refers to an earlier section for resolution.

Key Terms
متحیز
تسلسل
في
جسم
محل
المكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection conclusively disproves the existence of place.
Correction: The refutation shows that the objection relies on false analogies and ambiguous language.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator answer the objection that place would lead to infinite regress?
A) By denying that place exists altogether.
B) By showing that place has properties that cannot apply to itself, unlike light and existence.
C) By claiming that infinite regress is acceptable.
D) By stating that place is not a body.

Key Takeaway
The existence of place is defended by showing that infinite regress is avoided because place cannot be self-predicated, and the reversal is resolved by the ambiguity of the word 'in'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth about Allah or His creation because of a seemingly straightforward logical objection that you never examined deeply? What would it take for you to revisit that with the patience and precision shown in this lesson?
?
The objector uses an 'either/or' trap: if place exists, it must be this or that. Where in your own faith are you using such binary thinking to avoid accepting a difficult truth? Are you willing to see the subtle distinctions that might resolve the tension?
?
The ambiguity of the word 'in' saved the argument for place. When have you misread a verse or hadith because you assumed a word had only one meaning? What did you learn about the importance of precise language in matters of eternal consequence?

The Objection of Infinite Regress Against Place and Its Resolution
Objection and answer regarding infinite regress of place

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if place exists it leads to infinite regress, and why the attempted analogy with light and existence fails.

Core Idea
Place's properties are symmetrical, preventing it from being its own place.

Theologians argued about the nature of place. One powerful objection claims that if place exists as a body, it would need another place, leading to infinite regress. This lesson examines that objection and the response that saves the concept of place.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening lines: if place exists, it is a body, so it has a place, leading to infinite regress. Notice the logical flow.
2
Consider the counter-argument
A potential reply says: the body could be its own place, like light or existence, stopping the regress. Ask yourself if that analogy holds.
The examples given involve asymmetry.
3
Understand the refutation
Sanusi explains that place has equal properties (خواص متساوية) that cannot apply to itself, unlike the examples. Grasp why this distinction is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشكك عليه أي على وجود المكان بأنه لو وجد المكان فإما متحيز فله مكان إذ لا معنى للمتحيز إلا ذلك وحينئذ لتسلسل الأمكنة إلى غير النهاية إذ لكل مكان مكان آخر على ذلك التقدير. قوله: وحينئذ تتسلسل الأمكنة فإن قلت : المتحيز إذا كان غير مكان فله مكان زائد وإذا كان مكاناً فله مكان هو نفسه على قياس ما قيل في الضوء والوجود، والتقدم الزماني لأجزاء الزمان فلا يلزم التسلسل . قلت : للمكان خواص متساوية لا تتصور في الشيء بالنسبة إلى نفسه، ولا كذلك الحال في الأمثلة السابقة.
And it was objected against the existence of place: if place exists, then it is either a body (mutahayyiz) and thus has a place, for the meaning of body is nothing other than that, and in that case there would be an infinite regress of places, since every place has another place on this assumption. His saying: 'And then places would regress infinitely.' For you might say: the body, if it is not a place, has an additional place, and if it is a place, then its place is itself, by analogy with what is said about light and existence, and the temporal priority of parts of time, so no regress follows. I say: place has equal properties that cannot be conceived with respect to itself, unlike the cases in the examples.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the existence of place based on infinite regress, followed by a refutation of a potential counter-argument. The objector argues: if place exists, it must be a body (since it can be pointed to), and every body has a place, so the place of that body would require another place, leading to an infinite chain. The commentator then anticipates a reply: one might say that if the body itself is its own place (as in light and existence, or temporal parts), no regress occurs. The answer is that place has symmetrical properties (خواص متساوية) – meaning that the relationship of containment applies equally between place and body – which cannot hold for a thing relative to itself, unlike the examples given. Thus the regress argument stands, as the examples are not analogous.

Key Terms
مكان (makan)
متحيز (mutahayyiz)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
خواص متساوية (equal properties)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that place can be its own container, analogously to light and existence.
Correction: Place has equal properties (خواص متساوية) that cannot hold for a thing relative to itself, so the analogy fails.

Checkpoint
Why does the objection of infinite regress not apply to place in the same way as the examples of light and existence?
A) Because place is a body and light is not.
B) Because place has equal properties that cannot be conceived for the thing itself.
C) Because light has no place.
D) Because the examples are not analogical.

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress objection against place is refuted by showing that place's relational properties prevent it from being its own place, unlike light and time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Like the infinite regress of places, do you ever find yourself caught in a never-ending chain of justifications for your actions? What would it take to stop that chain and ground yourself in something beyond yourself?
?
Place cannot contain itself because of its relational properties. In what areas of your life are you trying to be self-sufficient or be your own foundation? How might that be leading to a logical or spiritual dead end?
?
The objection assumes place is a body. What assumptions do you hold about yourself that might lead to intellectual or moral blockages? Are you willing to examine those assumptions in light of this lesson?
