The Objection That Place Would Be in the Body
Objection that if place is an accident, it would be in the body, with refutation via ambiguity of 'in'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an objection against place being an accident is refuted by recognizing the multiple meanings of the preposition 'in'.

Core Idea
Ambiguity in the word 'in' dissolves the contradiction between accident and location.

We have been considering arguments that place is an accident. One critic objects: if place is an accident, it must inhere in a body, but that would put the place in the body, not the body in the place. How can we respond? The key is to realize that the word 'in' has multiple senses. Let's see how the scholars resolve this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Notice the two parts: first the objection, then the reply.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'in'
The refutation hinges on distinguishing two meanings of the word 'in' (fī). Find them in the commentary: one is 'filling' (māli'), the other is 'subsisting' (qiyām).
3
Reflect on why the objection fails
Understand how using these two distinct meanings allows both statements to be true simultaneously without contradiction. This shows the objection is based on an ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو حال في المتحيز فإما الجسم الذي متمكن فيه فيكون المكان حينئذ في الجسم لا الجسم في المكان وهذا باطل. قوله: فيكون المكان في الجسم لا الجسم في المكان رد عليه بأن لكلمة في معان فيجوز أن يكون الجسم في مكان بأحدها والعكس بمعناها الآخر مثلاً يكون الجسم في بمعنى كونه مالئاً له، والمكان فيه بمعنى قيامه به ولا منافاة بينهما.
Or it is an accident (ḥāl) in a body, then it is either the body in which it inheres, so on that assumption the place is in the body, not the body in the place, and this is false. His saying: 'Thus the place is in the body, not the body in the place' is refuted by saying that the word ['in' (fī)] has meanings; it is permissible that the body be in the place in one of them, and the opposite in another of its meanings; for example, the body is in the sense of filling it, and the place is in the sense of subsisting in it, and there is no contradiction between them.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents another objection against the existence of place: if place is an accident (ḥāl), it must inhere in a body, but that body is the one located in the place, so the place would be in the body (as an accident) rather than the body being in the place – which is absurd. The refutation is that the preposition 'in' (في) is ambiguous: it can mean 'filling' (مالئ) when the body is in the place, and 'subsisting' (قيام) when the place is in the body as an accident. Both meanings can hold simultaneously without contradiction. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
حال (ḥāl)
في (fī)
مالئ (māli')
قيام (qiyām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'in' has only one meaning, leading to the false conclusion that place cannot be an accident.
Correction: The word 'in' has multiple meanings, such as 'filling' and 'subsisting', so the objection is invalid.

Checkpoint
How is the objection that place would be in the body refuted?
A) By showing that place is not an accident.
B) By pointing out that the word 'in' has multiple meanings.
C) By arguing that the body is not in the place.
D) By denying that place exists as an accident.

Key Takeaway
The objection that place as accident leads to contradiction fails because the preposition 'in' has different meanings when applied to the body and to the place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a teaching that seems contradictory, do you immediately reject it, or do you pause to consider if your understanding of the key terms might be limited? How might pride in your own definitions close you off from a deeper truth?
?
In your daily interactions, how often do you assume that words like 'in' or 'with' have only one meaning, leading to misunderstandings? Will you commit to seeking clarity before concluding that another person's statement is false?
?
This lesson shows that a superficial objection can dissolve when the ambiguity of language is resolved. What does that tell you about your own approach to theological questions? Are you willing to study the precise meanings of terms before forming judgments, even if it humbles you?

Objection to Place as an Accident Attached to Another Body or as a Spiritual Substance
Objection to place as accident attaching by interpenetration or contact, and rejection of place as spiritual substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why place cannot be an accident that attaches to another body by interpenetration or contact, and why it cannot be a spiritual substance.

Core Idea
Place cannot be an accident or spiritual substance without contradiction or infinite regress.

In this passage, the author further demolishes the view that place is an accident inhering in a body. He presents two ways such an attachment could occur—interpenetration or contact—and shows that both lead to absurd consequences. Then he considers the possibility that place is a non-physical substance and rejects it for lacking the necessary property of being pointable. This completes the argument that place is not an accident or spiritual substance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the interpenetration objection
Read the part about mudākhala and see why it leads to two bodies overlapping (tadākhul), which is impossible.
Tadākhul is a key term meaning interpenetration.
2
Understand the contact objection
Read the part about mumāssa and see how it leads to an infinite regress of bodies, which is also impossible.
This is a classic regress argument (tasalsul).
3
Understand the rejection of spiritual substance
Read the last part about jawhar mujarrad and why it fails because it lacks ishāra (spatial indication).
Ishāra is crucial for place; a spiritual substance cannot be pointed to.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( إما بالمداخلة ) وذلك بأن يكون حلول المكان في محله سريانياً فيلزم تداخل الجسمين بالضرورة. قوله : ( وإما بالمماسة ) للجسم الذي حل فيه مكانه، وذلك بأن يكون حلوله فيه غير سرياني فيكون المكان حينئذ عرضاً قائماً بأطراف الجسم الآخر، ولكل جسم مكان بالضرورة فيكون للجسم الآخر مكان حال في جسم ثالث يماسه الجسم الآخر، وهكذا فيلزم التسلسل وعدم تناهي الأجسام. وأما المتحيز ولا حال فيه بل يكون جوهراً معقولاً مجرداً فلا إشارة إليه وأنه باطل بالضرورة.
His saying: 'Either by interpenetration (mudākhala)' – that is, that the place's indwelling in its locus is penetrative, so the interpenetration of two bodies necessarily follows. His saying: 'Or by contact (mumāssa)' – for the body in which its place indwells, and that by its indwelling being non-penetrative, so then the place is an accident subsisting on the extremities of the other body; and every body necessarily has a place, so the other body has a place that is an accident in a third body which the other body contacts, and so on, leading to infinite regress and non-finite bodies. And as for a body without an accident, but rather it is a spiritual substance (jawhar ma‘qūl mujarrad), then there is no indication (ishāra) to it, and that is necessarily false because place, as mentioned, is indicated by 'here' and 'there'.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents two further problems for the view that place is an accident inhering in a body. First, if place indwells its locus (the body it is in) by interpenetration (mudākhala), then two bodies (the place and the body) would interpenetrate, which is impossible. Second, if it indwells by mere contact (mumāssa) without interpenetration, then place becomes an accident on the surface of the other body; but that other body itself must have a place, leading to another body, and so on ad infinitum, requiring an infinite number of bodies – also impossible. Finally, the option that place is a non-bodily substance (jawhar mujarrad) is rejected because place must be pointed to (ishāra) as a physical location, whereas a spiritual substance cannot be indicated by 'here' or 'there'. Thus all alternatives for the existence of place as an accident or substance are impossible.

Key Terms
مداخلة (mudākhala)
مماسة (mumāssa)
تداخل (tadākhul)
جوهر معقول مجرد (jawhar ma‘qūl mujarrad)
إشارة (ishāra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that place could be a spiritual substance that is not physical.
Correction: The passage shows that place must be perceivable by sense and indicated spatially, so it cannot be a spiritual substance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of place being an accident attached to another body by interpenetration?
A) Infinite regress of places.
B) Interpenetration of two bodies.
C) Place becomes a spiritual substance.
D) No consequence.

Key Takeaway
Place cannot be an accident attaching to another body, nor a spiritual substance, because each leads to an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there beliefs you hold about the world that, if examined logically, lead to an infinite regress like the one in this passage? Would you abandon them if you saw the contradiction?
?
The text insists place must be indicated physically (ishara). How does this affect the way you think about Allah's relation to place? Have you ever inadvertently attributed a physical location to Him without realizing the impossibility?
?
When you consider the impossibility of interpenetration, does that challenge any assumptions you have about how the spiritual and physical realms interact in your own life—for example, in acts of worship or the indwelling of faith?

Definition of Place and Its Separation from the Body
Al-Jurjani defines place and argues it is not part of the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial definition of place as continuous quantity and the argument that place cannot be a part of the body because it would then transfer with the body.

Core Idea
Place is not part of the body; otherwise, movement would be impossible.

Al-Jurjani begins by defining place as a continuous quantity and presents a simple but powerful argument: if place were part of the body, it would move with the body, but we clearly see bodies move from one place to another. This sets the stage for a deeper investigation into what place actually is.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the short Arabic statement carefully. Notice how Al-Jurjani defines place and immediately draws a necessary consequence.
The term 'continuous quantity' (al-kamm al-muttasil) is a standard category in kalam physics.
2
Identify the argument
The argument is: if place were a part of the body, then when the body moves, its place would move with it—but we observe bodies moving from place to place. Therefore place is not a part of the body.
3
Reflect on the implication
This argument establishes that place must be distinct from the body. It is the foundation for the subsequent classification of possibilities.
The author says 'its corruption is apparent' meaning the idea that place is part of the body is obviously false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل قطعاً ) وأيضاً ينتقل ( المكان ( بانتقاله ) أي بانتقال الجسم لوجوب انتقال الحال بانتقال محله فلا يتصور انتقال الجسم من المكان، وإليه وفساده ظاهر . ( وإما جسم غيره ) أي غير الجسم المتمكن في ذلك المكان . وهو أيضاً باطل لأن حصول الجسم المتمكن في مكانه ( إما بالمداخلة في الجسم الذي هو محل مكانه وذلك بأن يكون حلوله في محله سريانياً ( فيلزم تدخل الجسمين) الباطل بالضرورة ( وإما بالمماسة ) للجسم الذي حل فيه مكانه، وذلك بأن يكون حلوله فيه غير سرياني فيكون المكان حينئذ عرضاً قائماً بأطراف الجسم الآخر، ولكل جسم مكان بالضرورة) فيكون للجسم الآخر مكان حال في جسم ثالث يماسه الجسم الآخر، وهكذا (فيلزم التسلسل وعدم تناهي الأجسام وسنبطله فيما بعد ( وأما المتحيز ولا حال فيه ) بل يكون جوهراً معقولاً مجرداً ( فلا إشارة) حينئذ ( إِليه ) أي إلى المكان لأن الجواهر المعقولة لا تقبل الإشارة ) وأنه باطل بالضرورة ) لأن المكان كما مر مشار إليه بهنا وهناك . ( وأيضاً فلا يمكن حصول الجسم فيه أي في المكان على ذلك التقدير، قوله : ( وهذا باطل قطعاً إذ لا ينسب المكان أنَّ الجسم فلا إلى ما يقال : الدن في الشراب ولا البيت في زيد . قوله : ( إما بالمداخلة) التسلسل اللازم على تقدير المماسة لازم على هذا التقدير أيضاً إلا أن هذا اللازم أشد استحالة فلذا تعرض له . قوله : ( فيلزم التسلسل إذ لا يجوز كون كل منهما مكاناً آخر إذ لا ينسب المكان إلى المتمكن بفي . الجسم في المكان، لا هو فيه فقط وهو باطل قطعاً. لأنا نعلم بديهة أنَّ مكان الشيء خارج منفصل عنه . قلت : معلومية انفصال مكان الشيء ء عنه بناء على أنه لو لم يكن كذلك لم يتصور أن ينقل الجسم منه وإليه فلا يكون قوله : وأيضاً فينتقل له وجهاً مستقلاً تأمل. قوله : ( وذلك بأن يكون حلوله أي بأن يكون حلول المكان في محله سريانياً، وإنما يلزم تداخل الجسمين حينئذ ، لأنَّ المتمكن في مكان مالئ ،له والمكان مملوء منه فيلزم على تقدير أن يكون حلول المكان في محله سريانياً تداخل الجسم المتمكن من الجسم الآخر بالضرورة. قوله : ( ولكل جسم مكان بالضرورة فإن قلت: كان يكفي حينئذ أن يقال : وأما جسم مكان بالضرورة فالترديد مستدرك . قلت : إنما فصل إظهاراً لفساد فاحش في غيره، ولكل جسم آخر الشقين. قوله : ( فيلزم التسلسل وعدم تناهي الأجسام فإن قلت: لم لا يجوز أن يكون طرف ذلك مكاناً لهذا، وطرف هذا مكاناً لذلك . قلت : يجب أن يكون المتمكن منطبقاً على مكانه الحقيقي كما سيأتي ولا يتحقق هذا فيما ذكر. المعنى المشهور أعني نفي العينية أو الجزئية إذ لا يسبق الوهم إلى العينية. قوله : (وليس المكان إلخ) أي الدليل المذكور كما دل على نفي الجزئية دل على نفي الحالية أيضاً، وهو المطلوب في هذا المقام، ليترتب عليه قوله : ثم الجسم ينطبق عليه إلا أنه لم يذكره لعدم القول به .
The Second Observatory - The Ninth Objective: Place (al-makan), and it is of continuous quantity. Absolutely, and also place transfers with its transfer, i.e., with the transfer of the body, because it is necessary for the state to transfer with the transfer of its locus, so it is unimaginable for the body to transfer from place, and to it, and its corruption is apparent. (Or it is another body), i.e., other than the body located in that place. And it is also false, because the obtaining of the located body in its place (is either by interpenetration (al-madakhala) into the body that is the locus of its place, and that by its indwelling in its locus being permeating, so the interpenetration of the two bodies follows, which is false by necessity; (or by contact (al-mumassa)) with the body in which its place indwells, and that by its indwelling in it not being permeating, so place then becomes an accident subsisting in the extremities of the other body, and every body necessarily has a place, so the other body has a place indwelling in a third body which the other body contacts, and so on, so infinite regress and non-finiteness of bodies follow, and we will refute it later. (And as for a thing extended (mutahayyiz) but having nothing indwelling in it), but rather it is an intellectually grasped substance abstract, so there is then no pointing to it, i.e., to the place, because intellectually grasped substances do not admit of pointing; and it is false by necessity, because place, as mentioned, is pointed to here and there. (And also, it is not possible for the body to obtain in it, i.e., in the place, on that estimation. His statement: (And this is absolutely false, because place is not attributed to the body—not as one says: the jar in the wine, nor the house in Zayd). His statement: (Either by interpenetration): the necessary infinite regress on the estimation of contact is also necessary on this estimation, except that this necessary consequence is more impossible, so he addressed it. His statement: (So infinite regress follows) because it is not permissible for each of them to be another place, since place is not attributed to the located by 'fi' (in). The body is in the place, not that it is only in it, and it is absolutely false. For we know intuitively that the place of a thing is external and separate from it. I said: The knownness of the separation of a thing's place from it is based on the fact that if it were not so, it would not be conceivable to transfer the body from it and to it, so his statement: 'And also it transfers' does not have an independent aspect; ponder. His statement: (And that by its indwelling), i.e., that the indwelling of place in its locus be permeating. The interpenetration of the two bodies follows then because the located in a place fills it, and the place is filled from it, so on the estimation that the indwelling of place in its locus is permeating, the interpenetration of the located body with the other body necessarily follows. His statement: (And every body necessarily has a place). If you say: It would have sufficed then to say: 'And as for a body, it necessarily has a place', so the division is redundant. I say: He only detailed it to show the manifest corruption of the other [alternative], and [the statement] 'and every body' [indicates] the other of the two divisions. His statement: (So infinite regress and non-finiteness of bodies follow). If you say: Why is it not permissible for the extremity of that to be a place for this, and the extremity of this a place for that? I say: The located must coincide with its true place, as will come, and this is not realized in what was mentioned. (And if these three divisions, which exhaust the rational possibilities, are invalid, then the existence of place is absolutely invalid.) And the answer: That its existence is necessary (daruri), known to every rational person, and what you mentioned of the doubt impugning its existence is a skeptical questioning of the self-evident (al-badihi) which no one doubts, and it is manifest sophistry (safsata) and clear fallacy, not deserving an answer, because its invalidity is known with certainty, even if the precise aspect of the state of affairs is not determined, as in general refutations. (And its solution will be known in the answer to the doubts raised against the doctrines) concerning the reality of place, i.e., the solution of what you mentioned, so the aspect of its corruption is determined, as one might say, for example: We choose that it is an accident indwelling in another body, attached to its extremities without its depths, and it is the surface, and there does not follow infinite regress of bodies nor their non-finiteness, because it is permissible for them to end at a body that has no place but rather has position, as will come. (Then, it, i.e., place, is external to the located), i.e., it is not a part of it, (otherwise place would transfer with its transfer) due to the impossibility of the separation of the whole, which is the located, from the part, which is the place, so it is unimaginable for the body to transfer from its place. And place is not a thing indwelling in the located, otherwise it would also transfer with its transfer. And he did not mention it because no one said it, unlike the part, for (some ancient philosophers said: It, i.e., place, is matter (al-hayula), for it, i.e., means, accepts the succession of bodies located in it. And it is not hidden from you that its outcome is that place accepts the succession of bodies and matter also accepts the succession of bodies, i.e., bodily forms, so it is the same: the first receiver, which is place, is identical to the second receiver, namely matter. And you have already known its invalidity, i.e., the invalidity of place being matter, from what has passed of place that it is said: that his statement: (Its existence is necessary) [contains] that the opponent does not concede its existence, let alone its necessity, and mere claim is not heard in the locus of dispute. His statement: (As one might say, etc.) And it was said: The necessary consequence of its not being extended (mutahayyiz) in the sense of being located in a place is that it would have no place, unless it has extension in itself, so it is permissible for it to be a dimension subsisting by itself, and it would have no place, and the body would be located in it by interpenetration, and there is no impossibility in the interpenetration of the material dimension in the abstract dimension, as will come. His statement: (Then it, etc.) is conjunct to his statement: 'and it exists'. His statement: (i.e., it is not a part of him) means that the intended meaning of affirming its externality is the negation of parthood, not the well-known meaning, i.e., the negation of identity or parthood, since the imagination does not precede to identity. His statement: (And it is not place, etc.) i.e., the mentioned proof, just as it indicates the negation of parthood, it also indicates the negation of inherence, and that is what is required in this context, so that his statement: 'then the body coincides with it' may follow from it, except that he did not mention it because no one holds it.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about the nature of place (al-makan) in the context of kalam. It begins by defining place as a continuous quantity and then presents three possible accounts of what place could be, each of which is refuted, leading to the opponent's conclusion that place does not exist. The author then responds by affirming that the existence of place is necessary (daruri) and self-evident, and that the objections are mere sophistry. Finally, he clarifies that place is external to the body and refutes the view that place is matter (hayula).

The argument proceeds as follows: First, the author notes that if place were a part of the body, it would transfer with the body's transfer—which is impossible because the body moves from place to place. This shows that place cannot be part of the body. Then he considers three exhaustive divisions: (1) Place could be the body itself; this is false because place is distinct from the body. (2) Place could be another body; this leads to either interpenetration (impossible) or infinite regress (also impossible). (3) Place could be an abstract substance without location; but then it cannot be pointed to, contradicting the nature of place. Since all three are false, the opponent argues that place does not exist.

The author answers that place's existence is necessary and known to every rational person. The objections are skeptical doubts against self-evident truths and are sophistry that does not deserve a detailed answer. However, a solution will be provided later: place is actually an accident (a surface) inhering in another body, and it does not entail infinite regress because bodies can end at a body that has position but no place.

Next, the author affirms that place is external to the located body, meaning it is not a part of it; otherwise, it would transfer with it. It is also not an accident inhering in the body, for the same reason. Then he refutes an ancient view that place is matter (hayula), because matter also accepts the succession of bodies, so the two would be identical, and the identity is false.

The commentary (indicated by 'qala') further explains each point, clarifying the possibility of interpenetration, the necessity of coincidence, and the meaning of externality as negation of parthood rather than identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument strips 'place' from being part of your identity. What 'place' – social status, career, or physical location – have you mistaken for part of who you are, and what would it cost you to move from it?
?
If place is not part of the body, then your true self is not defined by where you are. Where is your heart attached that you need to detach from, so that you can truly move toward Allah without dragging a false sense of identity?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
8 min
Three Alternatives for Place and Their Refutation
Al-Jurjani enumerates three alternatives for place, refutes each, and draws the opponent's conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three exhaustive possibilities for what place could be (another body, an abstract substance, or the body itself) and why each is refuted.

Core Idea
All three rational possibilities for place are impossible, so place seems nonexistent—but this is a false conclusion.

After showing place is not part of the body, Al-Jurjani presents three mutually exclusive possibilities: place could be the body itself, another body, or an abstract substance. He systematically refutes each one, leading to the objection that place might not exist at all. But this sets up the main response in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three alternatives
In the Arabic, look for the three phrases: 'إما هو نفسه' (it is the body itself), 'وإما جسم غيره' (another body), and 'وأما متحيز ولا حال فيه' (an extended thing with nothing in it, i.e., abstract substance).
The first alternative (itself) is only mentioned in passing; the focus is on the other two.
2
Understand the refutation of place as another body
This alternative leads to two sub-possibilities: interpenetration (madakhala) or contact (mumassa). Both lead to absurd consequences: interpenetration means two bodies in one place, and contact leads to infinite regress of bodies.
The infinite regress is a key argument in kalam against the physical theories of the philosophers.
3
Understand the refutation of place as an abstract substance
The third alternative says place is a non-extended substance that is not located anywhere. Al-Jurjani argues that such a thing cannot be pointed to, but place is pointed to (here and there). Also, a body cannot be located in something that has no spatial properties.
This refutation relies on the intuitive notion that place must be spatially referenced.
4
See the conclusion
Since all three possibilities are false, the opponent concludes that place does not exist at all. This sets up the need for a positive response.

The Second Observatory - The Ninth Objective: Place (al-makan), and it is of continuous quantity. Absolutely, and also place transfers with its transfer, i.e., with the transfer of the body, because it is necessary for the state to transfer with the transfer of its locus, so it is unimaginable for the body to transfer from place, and to it, and its corruption is apparent. (Or it is another body), i.e., other than the body located in that place. And it is also false, because the obtaining of the located body in its place (is either by interpenetration (al-madakhala) into the body that is the locus of its place, and that by its indwelling in its locus being permeating, so the interpenetration of the two bodies follows, which is false by necessity; (or by contact (al-mumassa)) with the body in which its place indwells, and that by its indwelling in it not being permeating, so place then becomes an accident subsisting in the extremities of the other body, and every body necessarily has a place, so the other body has a place indwelling in a third body which the other body contacts, and so on, so infinite regress and non-finiteness of bodies follow, and we will refute it later. Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about the nature of place (al-makan) in the context of kalam. It begins by defining place as a continuous quantity and then presents three possible accounts of what place could be, each of which is refuted, leading to the opponent's conclusion that place does not exist. The author then responds by affirming that the existence of place is necessary (daruri) and self-evident, and that the objections are mere sophistry. Finally, he clarifies that place is external to the body and refutes the view that place is matter (hayula).

The argument proceeds as follows: First, the author notes that if place were a part of the body, it would transfer with the body's transfer—which is impossible because the body moves from place to place. This shows that place cannot be part of the body. Then he considers three exhaustive divisions: (1) Place could be the body itself; this is false because place is distinct from the body. (2) Place could be another body; this leads to either interpenetration (impossible) or infinite regress (also impossible). (3) Place could be an abstract substance without location; but then it cannot be pointed to, contradicting the nature of place. Since all three are false, the opponent argues that place does not exist.

The author answers that place's existence is necessary and known to every rational person. The objections are skeptical doubts against self-evident truths and are sophistry that does not deserve a detailed answer. However, a solution will be provided later: place is actually an accident (a surface) inhering in another body, and it does not entail infinite regress because bodies can end at a body that has position but no place.

Next, the author affirms that place is external to the located body, meaning it is not a part of it; otherwise, it would transfer with it. It is also not an accident inhering in the body, for the same reason. Then he refutes an ancient view that place is matter (hayula), because matter also accepts the succession of bodies, so the two would be identical, and the identity is false.

The commentary (indicated by 'qala') further explains each point, clarifying the possibility of interpenetration, the necessity of coincidence, and the meaning of externality as negation of parthood rather than identity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that place could be another body, either by interpenetration or contact, but both lead to contradictions.
Correction: Interpenetration is impossible as two bodies cannot occupy the same space, and contact leads to an infinite regress of bodies each requiring a place.

Checkpoint
What is the second sub-possibility for the 'place is another body' alternative?
A) Interpenetration
B) Contact
C) Abstract substance
D) Continuous quantity

Key Takeaway
The three exhaustive alternatives for place are all impossible, leading to a challenge to the existence of place.

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
6 min
The Existence of Place is Self-Evident and Objections are Sophistry
Al-Jurjani rejects the skeptical denial of place and asserts its self-evident existence, promising a resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jurjani's response that the existence of place is necessary and self-evident, and that the dialectical objections are mere sophistry.

Core Idea
Place exists by direct intuition, even if we cannot yet fully explain its nature.

Having shown that the three alternatives lead to a dead end, Al-Jurjani now responds to the implied objection that place does not exist. He argues that the existence of place is known to every rational person as self-evident, and the alleged contradictions are sophistical doubts that do not undermine the clear intuition. He then hints at the correct solution: place is a surface attached to another body.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer
Notice that Al-Jurjani does not accept the opponent's conclusion. Instead, he affirms the existence of place as 'necessary' (daruri) and 'self-evident' (badihi).
The term 'daruri' means known without need for proof—a foundational concept in kalam.
2
Understand the accusation
He calls the objections 'skepticism of the self-evident' and 'sophistry'. This is a strong rhetorical move that dismisses the arguments as mere fallacy.
Sophistry (safsata) is a serious charge in Islamic philosophy, implying deliberate deception.
3
Preview the solution
He says the solution will be known later: place is an accident (a surface) attached to the extremities of another body, and infinite regress is avoided because bodies can end at a body that has position but no place.
This solution will be detailed in the next lesson.

Source Text

المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل قطعاً ) وأيضاً ينتقل ( المكان ( بانتقاله ) أي بانتقال الجسم لوجوب انتقال الحال بانتقال محله فلا يتصور انتقال الجسم من المكان، وإليه وفساده ظاهر . ( وإما جسم غيره ) أي غير الجسم المتمكن في ذلك المكان . وهو أيضاً باطل لأن حصول الجسم المتمكن في مكانه ( إما بالمداخلة في الجسم الذي هو محل مكانه وذلك بأن يكون حلوله في محله سريانياً ( فيلزم تدخل الجسمين) الباطل بالضرورة ( وإما بالمماسة ) للجسم الذي حل فيه مكانه، وذلك بأن يكون حلوله فيه غير سرياني فيكون المكان حينئذ عرضاً قائماً بأطراف الجسم الآخر، ولكل جسم مكان بالضرورة) فيكون للجسم الآخر مكان حال في جسم ثالث يماسه الجسم الآخر، وهكذا (فيلزم التسلسل وعدم تناهي الأجسام وسنبطله فيما بعد ( وأما المتحيز ولا حال فيه ) بل يكون جوهراً معقولاً مجرداً ( فلا إشارة) حينئذ ( إِليه ) أي إلى المكان لأن الجواهر المعقولة لا تقبل الإشارة ) وأنه باطل بالضرورة ) لأن المكان كما مر مشار إليه بهنا وهناك . ( وأيضاً فلا يمكن حصول الجسم فيه أي في المكان على ذلك التقدير، قوله : ( وهذا باطل قطعاً إذ لا ينسب المكان أنَّ الجسم فلا إلى ما يقال : الدن في الشراب ولا البيت في زيد . قوله : ( إما بالمداخلة) التسلسل اللازم على تقدير المماسة لازم على هذا التقدير أيضاً إلا أن هذا اللازم أشد استحالة فلذا تعرض له . قوله : ( فيلزم التسلسل إذ لا يجوز كون كل منهما مكاناً آخر إذ لا ينسب المكان إلى المتمكن بفي . الجسم في المكان، لا هو فيه فقط وهو باطل قطعاً. لأنا نعلم بديهة أنَّ مكان الشيء خارج منفصل عنه . قلت : معلومية انفصال مكان الشيء ء عنه بناء على أنه لو لم يكن كذلك لم يتصور أن ينقل الجسم منه وإليه فلا يكون قوله : وأيضاً فينتقل له وجهاً مستقلاً تأمل. قوله : ( وذلك بأن يكون حلوله أي بأن يكون حلول المكان في محله سريانياً، وإنما يلزم تداخل الجسمين حينئذ ، لأنَّ المتمكن في مكان مالئ ،له والمكان مملوء منه فيلزم على تقدير أن يكون حلول المكان في محله سريانياً تداخل الجسم المتمكن من الجسم الآخر بالضرورة. قوله : ( ولكل جسم مكان بالضرورة فإن قلت: كان يكفي حينئذ أن يقال : وأما جسم مكان بالضرورة فالترديد مستدرك . قلت : إنما فصل إظهاراً لفساد فاحش في غيره، ولكل جسم آخر الشقين. قوله : ( فيلزم التسلسل وعدم تناهي الأجسام فإن قلت: لم لا يجوز أن يكون طرف ذلك مكاناً لهذا، وطرف هذا مكاناً لذلك . قلت : يجب أن يكون المتمكن منطبقاً على مكانه الحقيقي كما سيأتي ولا يتحقق هذا فيما ذكر. ( و إذا بطل هذه الأقسام الثلاثة الحاصرة للاحتمالات العقلية بطل وجود المكان مطلقاً ) والجواب : أن وجوده (ضروري) معلوم لكل عاقل ( وما ذكرتم ) من الشبهة القادحة في وجوده ) تشكيك في البديهي) الذي لا يشك فيه (وأنه سفسطة) ظاهرة ومغالطة بينة ( لا تستحق الجواب ) لأن بطلانه معلوم يقيناً . وإن لم يكن وجه الحال فيه معيناً كما في النقوض الإجمالية ( وسيعلم في جواب الشكوك الواردة على المذاهب ) في حقيقة المكان ( حله ) أي حل ما ذكرتموه، فيتعين وجه فساده كأن يقال مثلا نختار أنه عرض حال في جسم آخر متعلق بأطرافه دون أعماقه وهو السطح ولا يلزم تسلسل الأجسام ولا تناهيها لجواز انتهائها إلى جسم لا مكان له بل له وضع كما سيأتي (ثم إنه ) أي المكان ( خارج عن المتمكن) أي ليس جزءاً له ( وإلا انتقل ( المكان ) بانتقاله ضرورة امتناع انفكاك (الكل) الذي هو المتمكن ( عن الجزء) الذي هو المكان، فلا يتصور انتقال الجسم عن مكانه وليس المكان أمراً حالاً في المتمكن وإلا انتقل بانتقاله أيضاً . ولم يذكره لأنه لم يقل به أحد بخلاف الجزء فإنه ( قال بعض قدماء الحكماء : إنه ) أي المكان ) هو الهيولى فإنه ) يعني ( يقبل تعاقب الأجسام) المتمكنة فيه ( ولا يخفى) عليك ( أنَّ حاصله ) هو المكان يقبل تعاقب الأجسام والهيولى ( أيضاً ، ( تقبل تعاقب الأجسام) أي الصور الجسمية ( فهو هو ( أي القابل الأول الذي هو المكان هو بعينه القابل الثاني أعني الهيولى ( وقد عرفت بطلانه ) يعني بطلان كون المكان هو الهيولى بما مر من المكان أن يقال : أن قوله : (إِنَّ وجوده (ضروري فيه أنَّ الخصم لا يسلم وجوده فضلاً عن الضرورة، ومجرد الدعوى لا يسمع في محل النزاع . قوله : ( كأن يقال إلخ) وكان يقال : اللازم من عدم كونه متحيزاً بمعنى حاصلاً في مكان أن لا يكون له مكان، إلا أن يكون له امتداد في نفسه، فيجوز أن يكون بعداً قائماً بنفسه، ولا يكون له مكان ويتمكن الجسم فيه بالمداخلة ولا امتناع في مداخلة البعد المادي في البعد المجرد كما . سيجيء. قوله : ( ثم إنه إلخ ) عطف على قوله وهو موجود قوله : (أي ليس جزءاً له) يعني أنَّ المراد من إثبات خروجه نفي الجزئية لا المعنى المشهور أعني نفي العينية أو الجزئية إذ لا يسبق الوهم إلى العينية. قوله : (وليس المكان إلخ) أي الدليل المذكور كما دل على نفي الجزئية دل على نفي الحالية أيضاً، وهو المطلوب في هذا المقام، ليترتب عليه قوله : ثم الجسم ينطبق عليه إلا أنه لم يذكره لعدم القول به .
The Second Observatory - The Ninth Objective: Place (al-makan), and it is of continuous quantity. Absolutely, and also place transfers with its transfer, i.e., with the transfer of the body, because it is necessary for the state to transfer with the transfer of its locus, so it is unimaginable for the body to transfer from place, and to it, and its corruption is apparent. (Or it is another body), i.e., other than the body located in that place. And it is also false, because the obtaining of the located body in its place (is either by interpenetration (al-madakhala) into the body that is the locus of its place, and that by its indwelling in its locus being permeating, so the interpenetration of the two bodies follows, which is false by necessity; (or by contact (al-mumassa)) with the body in which its place indwells, and that by its indwelling in it not being permeating, so place then becomes an accident subsisting in the extremities of the other body, and every body necessarily has a place, so the other body has a place indwelling in a third body which the other body contacts, and so on, so infinite regress and non-finiteness of bodies follow, and we will refute it later. (And as for a thing extended (mutahayyiz) but having nothing indwelling in it), but rather it is an intellectually grasped substance abstract, so there is then no pointing to it, i.e., to the place, because intellectually grasped substances do not admit of pointing; and it is false by necessity, because place, as mentioned, is pointed to here and there. (And also, it is not possible for the body to obtain in it, i.e., in the place, on that estimation. His statement: (And this is absolutely false, because place is not attributed to the body—not as one says: the jar in the wine, nor the house in Zayd). His statement: (Either by interpenetration): the necessary infinite regress on the estimation of contact is also necessary on this estimation, except that this necessary consequence is more impossible, so he addressed it. His statement: (So infinite regress follows) because it is not permissible for each of them to be another place, since place is not attributed to the located by 'fi' (in). The body is in the place, not that it is only in it, and it is absolutely false. For we know intuitively that the place of a thing is external and separate from it. I said: The knownness of the separation of a thing's place from it is based on the fact that if it were not so, it would not be conceivable to transfer the body from it and to it, so his statement: 'And also it transfers' does not have an independent aspect; ponder. His statement: (And that by its indwelling), i.e., that the indwelling of place in its locus be permeating. The interpenetration of the two bodies follows then because the located in a place fills it, and the place is filled from it, so on the estimation that the indwelling of place in its locus is permeating, the interpenetration of the located body with the other body necessarily follows. His statement: (And every body necessarily has a place). If you say: It would have sufficed then to say: 'And as for a body, it necessarily has a place', so the division is redundant. I say: He only detailed it to show the manifest corruption of the other [alternative], and [the statement] 'and every body' [indicates] the other of the two divisions. His statement: (So infinite regress and non-finiteness of bodies follow). If you say: Why is it not permissible for the extremity of that to be a place for this, and the extremity of this a place for that? I say: The located must coincide with its true place, as will come, and this is not realized in what was mentioned. (And if these three divisions, which exhaust the rational possibilities, are invalid, then the existence of place is absolutely invalid.) And the answer: That its existence is necessary (daruri), known to every rational person, and what you mentioned of the doubt impugning its existence is a skeptical questioning of the self-evident (al-badihi) which no one doubts, and it is manifest sophistry (safsata) and clear fallacy, not deserving an answer, because its invalidity is known with certainty, even if the precise aspect of the state of affairs is not determined, as in general refutations. (And its solution will be known in the answer to the doubts raised against the doctrines) concerning the reality of place, i.e., the solution of what you mentioned, so the aspect of its corruption is determined, as one might say, for example: We choose that it is an accident indwelling in another body, attached to its extremities without its depths, and it is the surface, and there does not follow infinite regress of bodies nor their non-finiteness, because it is permissible for them to end at a body that has no place but rather has position, as will come. (Then, it, i.e., place, is external to the located), i.e., it is not a part of it, (otherwise place would transfer with its transfer) due to the impossibility of the separation of the whole, which is the located, from the part, which is the place, so it is unimaginable for the body to transfer from its place. And place is not a thing indwelling in the located, otherwise it would also transfer with its transfer. And he did not mention it because no one said it, unlike the part, for (some ancient philosophers said: It, i.e., place, is matter (al-hayula), for it, i.e., means, accepts the succession of bodies located in it. And it is not hidden from you that its outcome is that place accepts the succession of bodies and matter also accepts the succession of bodies, i.e., bodily forms, so it is the same: the first receiver, which is place, is identical to the second receiver, namely matter. And you have already known its invalidity, i.e., the invalidity of place being matter, from what has passed of place that it is said: that his statement: (Its existence is necessary) [contains] that the opponent does not concede its existence, let alone its necessity, and mere claim is not heard in the locus of dispute. His statement: (As one might say, etc.) And it was said: The necessary consequence of its not being extended (mutahayyiz) in the sense of being located in a place is that it would have no place, unless it has extension in itself, so it is permissible for it to be a dimension subsisting by itself, and it would have no place, and the body would be located in it by interpenetration, and there is no impossibility in the interpenetration of the material dimension in the abstract dimension, as will come. His statement: (Then it, etc.) is conjunct to his statement: 'and it exists'. His statement: (i.e., it is not a part of him) means that the intended meaning of affirming its externality is the negation of parthood, not the well-known meaning, i.e., the negation of identity or parthood, since the imagination does not precede to identity. His statement: (And it is not place, etc.) i.e., the mentioned proof, just as it indicates the negation of parthood, it also indicates the negation of inherence, and that is what is required in this context, so that his statement: 'then the body coincides with it' may follow from it, except that he did not mention it because no one holds it.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about the nature of place (al-makan) in the context of kalam. It begins by defining place as a continuous quantity and then presents three possible accounts of what place could be, each of which is refuted, leading to the opponent's conclusion that place does not exist. The author then responds by affirming that the existence of place is necessary (daruri) and self-evident, and that the objections are mere sophistry. Finally, he clarifies that place is external to the body and refutes the view that place is matter (hayula).

The argument proceeds as follows: First, the author notes that if place were a part of the body, it would transfer with the body's transfer—which is impossible because the body moves from place to place. This shows that place cannot be part of the body. Then he considers three exhaustive divisions: (1) Place could be the body itself; this is false because place is distinct from the body. (2) Place could be another body; this leads to either interpenetration (impossible) or infinite regress (also impossible). (3) Place could be an abstract substance without location; but then it cannot be pointed to, contradicting the nature of place. Since all three are false, the opponent argues that place does not exist.

The author answers that place's existence is necessary and known to every rational person. The objections are skeptical doubts against self-evident truths and are sophistry that does not deserve a detailed answer. However, a solution will be provided later: place is actually an accident (a surface) inhering in another body, and it does not entail infinite regress because bodies can end at a body that has position but no place.

Next, the author affirms that place is external to the located body, meaning it is not a part of it; otherwise, it would transfer with it. It is also not an accident inhering in the body, for the same reason. Then he refutes an ancient view that place is matter (hayula), because matter also accepts the succession of bodies, so the two would be identical, and the identity is false.

The commentary (indicated by 'qala') further explains each point, clarifying the possibility of interpenetration, the necessity of coincidence, and the meaning of externality as negation of parthood rather than identity.

Key Terms
الضروري
البديهي
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because the three alternatives are false, place does not exist.
Correction: Al-Jurjani argues that existence of place is self-evident; the failure of those alternatives only shows they are not the correct account, not that place is unreal.

Checkpoint
How does Al-Jurjani characterize the objection that place does not exist?
A) As a valid philosophical doubt
B) As a sophisticated argument
C) As sophistry and fallacy
D) As a necessary truth

Key Takeaway
Place's existence is known intuitively; objections are sophistical and will be resolved by identifying place as a surface attached to another body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What self-evident truth about your faith or existence have you allowed intellectual doubts to weaken? Will you trust your fitrah or let sophistry shake your certainty?
?
Think of a time when you were certain of something from your heart but let clever arguments cause you to waver. How can you use al-Jurjani's approach—affirming the self-evident despite incomplete explanation—to stand firm?
?
Al-Jurjani says the invalidity of the objection is known with certainty even if the specific flaw isn't clear. Where in your life do you need to act on certainty despite not having all the answers? What is one step you can take today to live by that certainty?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
6 min
Place is External to the Body and Refutation of Place as Matter
Al-Jurjani asserts place is external to the body and refutes the identification of place with matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jurjani's final position: place is external to the body (not a part, not an accident), and the view that place is matter (hayula) is false.

Core Idea
Place is an external surface, not matter; otherwise, bodies couldn't move.

Al-Jurjani now concludes the discussion by clarifying the relationship between body and place: place is external to the body, neither a part nor an accident inhering in it. He then refutes an ancient view that place is prime matter (hayula), showing that it would lead to absurd consequences identical to rejecting place's true nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the affirmation that place is external
Al-Jurjani states that place is 'outside the located' (kharīj ‘an al-mutamakkin). He then gives an argument: if it were a part, it would transfer with the body; if it were an accident in the body, it would also transfer. Neither happens, so place is external.
The argument is a double reduction: if place were part, movement impossible; if place were an accident, same problem.
2
Understand the refutation of place as matter
Some ancient philosophers identified place with matter (hayula) because both receive changes. Al-Jurjani argues that if place were matter, then matter would also be place—but matter is something else, and this view has already been shown false.
The refutation is brief because he says 'you have already known its falsity' from earlier discussions.
3
Observe the conclusion
The lesson ends with the affirmation that place is an external surface (the correct view), and the false view of matter is rejected.

( وأيضاً فلا يمكن حصول الجسم فيه أي في المكان على ذلك التقدير، قوله : ( وهذا باطل قطعاً إذ لا ينسب المكان أنَّ الجسم فلا إلى ما يقال : الدن في الشراب ولا البيت في زيد . قوله : ( إما بالمداخلة) التسلسل اللازم على تقدير المماسة لازم على هذا التقدير أيضاً إلا أن هذا اللازم أشد استحالة فلذا تعرض له . قوله : ( فيلزم التسلسل إذ لا يجوز كون كل منهما مكاناً آخر إذ لا ينسب المكان إلى المتمكن بفي . الجسم في المكان، لا هو فيه فقط وهو باطل قطعاً. لأنا نعلم بديهة أنَّ مكان الشيء خارج منفصل عنه . قلت : معلومية انفصال مكان الشيء ء عنه بناء على أنه لو لم يكن كذلك لم يتصور أن ينقل الجسم منه وإليه فلا يكون قوله : وأيضاً فينتقل له وجهاً مستقلاً تأمل. قوله : ( وذلك بأن يكون حلوله أي بأن يكون حلول المكان في محله سريانياً، وإنما يلزم تداخل الجسمين حينئذ ، لأنَّ المتمكن في مكان مالئ ،له والمكان مملوء منه فيلزم على تقدير أن يكون حلول المكان في محله سريانياً تداخل الجسم المتمكن من الجسم الآخر بالضرورة. قوله : ( ولكل جسم مكان بالضرورة فإن قلت: كان يكفي حينئذ أن يقال : وأما جسم مكان بالضرورة فالترديد مستدرك . قلت : إنما فصل إظهاراً لفساد فاحش في غيره، ولكل جسم آخر الشقين. قوله : ( فيلزم التسلسل وعدم تناهي الأجسام فإن قلت: لم لا يجوز أن يكون طرف ذلك مكاناً لهذا، وطرف هذا مكاناً لذلك . قلت : يجب أن يكون المتمكن منطبقاً على مكانه الحقيقي كما سيأتي ولا يتحقق هذا فيما ذكر. فيه (وأنه سفسطة) ظاهرة ومغالطة بينة ( لا تستحق الجواب ) لأن بطلانه معلوم يقيناً . وإن لم يكن وجه الحال فيه معيناً كما في النقوض الإجمالية ( وسيعلم في جواب الشكوك الواردة على المذاهب ) في حقيقة المكان The Second Observatory - The Ninth Objective: Place (al-makan), and it is of continuous quantity. Absolutely, and also place transfers with its transfer, i.e., with the transfer of the body, because it is necessary for the state to transfer with the transfer of its locus, so it is unimaginable for the body to transfer from place, and to it, and its corruption is apparent. (Or it is another body), i.e., other than the body located in that place. And it is also false, because the obtaining of the located body in its place (is either by interpenetration (al-madakhala) into the body that is the locus of its place, and that by its indwelling in its locus being permeating, so the interpenetration of the two bodies follows, which is false by necessity; (or by contact (al-mumassa)) with the body in which its place indwells, and that by its indwelling in it not being permeating, so place then becomes an accident subsisting in the extremities of the other body, and every body necessarily has a place, so the other body has a place indwelling in a third body which the other body contacts, and so on, so infinite regress and non-finiteness of bodies follow, and we will refute it later. (And as for a thing extended (mutahayyiz) but having nothing indwelling in it), but rather it is an intellectually grasped substance abstract, so there is then no pointing to it, i.e., to the place, because intellectually grasped substances do not admit of pointing; and it is false by necessity, because place, as mentioned, is pointed to here and there. (And also, it is not possible for the body to obtain in it, i.e., in the place, on that estimation. His statement: (And this is absolutely false, because place is not attributed to the body—not as one says: the jar in the wine, nor the house in Zayd). His statement: (Either by interpenetration): the necessary infinite regress on the estimation of contact is also necessary on this estimation, except that this necessary consequence is more impossible, so he addressed it. His statement: (So infinite regress follows) because it is not permissible for each of them to be another place, since place is not attributed to the located by 'fi' (in). The body is in the place, not that it is only in it, and it is absolutely false. For we know intuitively that the place of a thing is external and separate from it. I said: The knownness of the separation of a thing's place from it is based on the fact that if it were not so, it would not be conceivable to transfer the body from it and to it, so his statement: 'And also it transfers' does not have an independent aspect; ponder. His statement: (And that by its indwelling), i.e., that the indwelling of place in its locus be permeating. The interpenetration of the two bodies follows then because the located in a place fills it, and the place is filled from it, so on the estimation that the indwelling of place in its locus is permeating, the interpenetration of the located body with the other body necessarily follows. His statement: (And every body necessarily has a place). If you say: It would have sufficed then to say: 'And as for a body, it necessarily has a place', so the division is redundant. I say: He only detailed it to show the manifest corruption of the other [alternative], and [the statement] 'and every body' [indicates] the other of the two divisions. His statement: (So infinite regress and non-finiteness of bodies follow). If you say: Why is it not permissible for the extremity of that to be a place for this, and the extremity of this a place for that? I say: The located must coincide with its true place, as will come, and this is not realized in what was mentioned. (And if these three divisions, which exhaust the rational possibilities, are invalid, then the existence of place is absolutely invalid.) And the answer: That its existence is necessary (daruri), known to every rational person, and what you mentioned of the doubt impugning its existence is a skeptical questioning of the self-evident (al-badihi) which no one doubts, and it is manifest sophistry (safsata) and clear fallacy, not deserving an answer, because its invalidity is known with certainty, even if the precise aspect of the state of affairs is not determined, as in general refutations. (And its solution will be known in the answer to the doubts raised against the doctrines) concerning the reality of place, i.e., the solution of what you mentioned, so the aspect of its corruption is determined, as one might say, for example: We choose that it is an accident indwelling in another body, attached to its extremities without its depths, and it is the surface, and there does not follow infinite regress of bodies nor their non-finiteness, because it is permissible for them to end at a body that has no place but rather has position, as will come. (Then, it, i.e., place, is external to the located), i.e., it is not a part of it, (otherwise place would transfer with its transfer) due to the impossibility of the separation of the whole, which is the located, from the part, which is the place, so it is unimaginable for the body to transfer from its place. And place is not a thing indwelling in the located, otherwise it would also transfer with its transfer. And he did not mention it because no one said it, unlike the part, for (some ancient philosophers said: It, i.e., place, is matter (al-hayula), for it, i.e., means, accepts the succession of bodies located in it. And it is not hidden from you that its outcome is that place accepts the succession of bodies and matter also accepts the succession of bodies, i.e., bodily forms, so it is the same: the first receiver, which is place, is identical to the second receiver, namely matter. And you have already known its invalidity, i.e., the invalidity of place being matter, from what has passed of place that it is said: that his statement: (Its existence is necessary) [contains] that the opponent does not concede its existence, let alone its necessity, and mere claim is not heard in the locus of dispute. His statement: (As one might say, etc.) And it was said: The necessary consequence of its not being extended (mutahayyiz) in the sense of being located in a place is that it would have no place, unless it has extension in itself, so it is permissible for it to be a dimension subsisting by itself, and it would have no place, and the body would be located in it by interpenetration, and there is no impossibility in the interpenetration of the material dimension in the abstract dimension, as will come. His statement: (Then it, etc.) is conjunct to his statement: 'and it exists'. His statement: (i.e., it is not a part of him) means that the intended meaning of affirming its externality is the negation of parthood, not the well-known meaning, i.e., the negation of identity or parthood, since the imagination does not precede to identity. His statement: (And it is not place, etc.) i.e., the mentioned proof, just as it indicates the negation of parthood, it also indicates the negation of inherence, and that is what is required in this context, so that his statement: 'then the body coincides with it' may follow from it, except that he did not mention it because no one holds it.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about the nature of place (al-makan) in the context of kalam. It begins by defining place as a continuous quantity and then presents three possible accounts of what place could be, each of which is refuted, leading to the opponent's conclusion that place does not exist. The author then responds by affirming that the existence of place is necessary (daruri) and self-evident, and that the objections are mere sophistry. Finally, he clarifies that place is external to the body and refutes the view that place is matter (hayula).

The argument proceeds as follows: First, the author notes that if place were a part of the body, it would transfer with the body's transfer—which is impossible because the body moves from place to place. This shows that place cannot be part of the body. Then he considers three exhaustive divisions: (1) Place could be the body itself; this is false because place is distinct from the body. (2) Place could be another body; this leads to either interpenetration (impossible) or infinite regress (also impossible). (3) Place could be an abstract substance without location; but then it cannot be pointed to, contradicting the nature of place. Since all three are false, the opponent argues that place does not exist.

The author answers that place's existence is necessary and known to every rational person. The objections are skeptical doubts against self-evident truths and are sophistry that does not deserve a detailed answer. However, a solution will be provided later: place is actually an accident (a surface) inhering in another body, and it does not entail infinite regress because bodies can end at a body that has position but no place.

Next, the author affirms that place is external to the located body, meaning it is not a part of it; otherwise, it would transfer with it. It is also not an accident inhering in the body, for the same reason. Then he refutes an ancient view that place is matter (hayula), because matter also accepts the succession of bodies, so the two would be identical, and the identity is false.

The commentary (indicated by 'qala') further explains each point, clarifying the possibility of interpenetration, the necessity of coincidence, and the meaning of externality as negation of parthood rather than identity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think place is matter (hayula) because both act as a receiver.
Correction: Al-Jurjani rejects this identity as already proven false; place is a surface, not the underlying matter.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument that place is not a part of the body?
A) If it were part, it would transfer with the body, but bodies move from place to place.
B) Place is a continuous quantity, so it cannot be a part.
C) The body would then have two places.
D) Places are abstract substances.

Key Takeaway
Place is external to the body (not part, not accident) and is not matter; it is a surface that allows movement.

Place as Dimension or Surface: Two Types of Contact
Defines place via two types of contact between body and place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of contact between a body and its place, and how each leads to a distinct definition of 'place' (al-makān) in Kalām.

Core Idea
Place is not one thing—it is either dimension or surface, depending on how the body contacts it.

In this passage, the author investigates what 'place' means by examining the relationship between a body and its place. He finds that the body fills its place entirely without excess or deficiency, and the place completely encompasses it. This forces a necessary contact, which can occur in two distinct ways. Each way yields a different definition of place. Let's read the passage and unpack these two modes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and the translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly. Then read the English translation to understand the overall argument. Notice the three initial statements about body and place, and the conclusion that contact is necessary.
2
Identify the two types of contact
The passage distinguishes between 'contact by totality' (interpenetration) and 'contact by sides' (contiguity). Find where these are introduced and their names in Arabic.
Interpenetration = mudākhala; Contiguity = mumāssa.
3
Connect each contact type to a definition of place
Interpenetration leads to place being a dimension (bu'd); contiguity leads to place being a surface (sath). Understand why each follows logically.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters in Kalām. It shows that 'place' is not a single concept but can refer to either a dimensional void or a containing surface, depending on the scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فشرع يتكلم عليه فقال : ( ثم الجسم منطبق على مكانه ) الحقيقي ليس زائداً عليه ( مالئ له ) ليس ناقصاً عنه بحيث لا يخلو شيء من مكانه عنه ( والمكان محيط به ) أي هوي بتمامه في المكان ليس شيء منه خارجاً عنه، ولهذا ينسب إليه بكلمة في مملوء منه كما ذكرناه وقد عرفت أنه يجوز انتقاله عنه ( ولا يتصور ذلك ) المذكور من حال الجسم ومكانه بالقياس إلى صاحبه ) إلا بالملاقاة ) بينهما وتلك الملاقاة ( إما بالتمام ) بحيث إذا فرض جزء من المتمكن يفرض بإزائه جزء من المكان وبالعكس فيتطابقان بالكلية . ( وتسمى ( الملاقاة على هذا الوجه ( المداخلة فيكون ) المكان على هذا التقدير ) هو البعد الذي ينفذ فيه الجسم، وينطبق البعد الحال فيه على ذلك البعد في أعماقه وأقطاره ) وإما لا بالتمام بل بالأطراف ) أي تكون أطراف الجسم ملاقية لمكانه دون أعماقه ) تسمى ( الملاقاة على هذا الوجه ( المماسة فيكون المكان حينئذ ( هو السطح الباطن للحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من المحوى، فإذن المكان إما البعد وإما سطح الحاوي لا ثالث لهما
Then he began to speak about it and said: 'Then the body is coincident with its place' – meaning it is not in excess of it; 'filling it' – meaning it is not deficient from it, such that nothing of its place is empty of it; 'and the place encompasses it' – meaning its entirety is in the place, nothing of it is outside it, and hence it is attributed to it by the word 'in' filling it as we mentioned, and you have known that it is possible for it to transfer from it; 'and that is not conceivable' – the mentioned state of the body and its place in relation to its possessor – 'except by contact' between them. And that contact is either 'by totality' such that if a part of the located thing is supposed, a part of the place is supposed corresponding to it and vice versa, so they correspond entirely. And this type of contact is called 'interpenetration' (al-mudākhala). Thus, on this estimation, place is the dimension (al-bu'd) into which the body penetrates, and the dimension subsisting in it coincides with that dimension in its depths and dimensions. Or it is not by totality but by the sides, i.e., the sides of the body contact its place without its depths. This type of contact is called 'contiguity' (al-mumāssa). Thus, on this estimation, place is the inner surface of the container (al-ḥāwī) which is contiguous to the outer surface of the contained. Therefore, place is either dimension or surface of the container, no third.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines 'place' (al-makān) by examining the relationship between a body and its place. It begins by stating three characteristics: the body coincides with its place (not exceeding), fills it (not deficient), and the place encompasses it entirely. This relationship necessitates contact between body and place. Two types of contact are distinguished. First, contact by totality (bi-at-tamām): if any part of the body is taken, a corresponding part of the place is taken, so they coincide completely. This is called 'interpenetration' (al-mudākhala). In this case, place is a dimension (al-bu'd) that the body penetrates, and the dimension in the body coincides with the dimension of the place in all depths and directions. Second, contact not by totality but by the sides (al-aṭrāf): only the surfaces of the body touch the place. This is called 'contiguity' (al-mumāssa). In this case, place is the inner surface (as-sath al-bāṭin) of the containing body that is contiguous to the outer surface of the contained body. The conclusion is that place is either a dimension or a surface, with no third possibility. The passage follows the author's own logical order: from the general relationship between body and place to the two modes of contact, and then to the two corresponding definitions of place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Place is always a surface that a body rests on or is contained by.
Correction: In Kalām, place can also be a dimension (void) that a body penetrates entirely, such as a body submerged in water.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two types of contact between body and place?
A) Mudakhala and Mura'a
B) Mudakhala and Mumassa
C) Mumassa and Muhadath
D) Inhidad and Imtidād

Key Takeaway
Place in Kalām is not a fixed concept; it is defined as either a dimension (when body interpenetrates) or a surface (when body is contiguous to its container).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

But have you ever considered that your physical existence is not merely 'in' a box of space, but that space itself coincides with you at every part? How should this awareness change your feeling of being 'contained' or 'enclosed' in this world?
?
The passage forces you to admit that there is a precise contact between your body and its place. If every moment you are in such exact contact with space, how does that affect your sense of being present? Do you truly dwell in the moment, or are you absent even as your body fills its place?
?
The distinction between interpenetration and contiguity reveals that your relationship to place is not one-dimensional. Is your soul's relationship to your body like interpenetration (fully present and filling it) or like contiguity (merely touching the surface)? Which one describes your spiritual state right now? What one action can you take today to move toward interpenetration?

Three Possibilities for Dimension and Critique of Common Definition
Analyses dimension into three modes and refutes the popular definition of place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational possibilities for the nature of place (dimension) and why the common definition of place as 'that which prevents falling' is inadequate.

Core Idea
Place must be either a surface, an imaginary volume, or a non-material volume—never a point or line.

In this passage, the author presents a rational analysis to determine the nature of place. By examining how a body fills its location, he deduces three and only three possibilities for what place could be. He then critiques the everyday notion of place as 'that which prevents a thing from falling,' showing its flaws. This lesson will guide you through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get the overall argument.
2
Understand the rational deduction
Notice how the author eliminates indivisible and one-dimensional place candidates, concluding that place must be either two-dimensional (surface) or three-dimensional (volume).
The volume possibility is further subdivided into imaginary or non-material.
3
Identify the three possibilities
List the three possibilities: (1) inner surface of the containing body, (2) imaginary dimension, (3) non-material existing dimension. These correspond to the terms سطح, موهوم, and موجود (non-material).
The author states there is no fourth possibility.
4
Examine the critique of common definition
The author shows that defining place as 'what prevents a thing from falling' fails for objects in motion or suspended (e.g., arrow in flight, stone on a rope).
The commentator clarifies the distinction between nuzūl (descent) and suqūṭ (falling).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبعد إما موجود أو مفروض موهوم أي مع قطع النظر عن دلائل الوجود. رابع لها وتوضيح ذلك بما لا مزيد عليه أن يقال : لما كان الجسم بكليته في مكانه مالئا له، لم يجز أن يكون المكان أمراً غير منقسم لاستحالة أن يكون المنقسم في جميع جهاته حاصلاً بتمامه لا ينقسم، ولا أن يكون أمراً منقسماً في جهة واحدة فقط كالخط مثلاً لاستحالة كونه محيطاً بالجسم بكليته، فهو إما منقسم في جهتين أو في الجهات كلها. وعلى الأول يكون المكان سطحاً عرضياً لامتناع الجزء وما في حكمه، ولا يجوز أن يكون حالاً في المتمكن لما مر، بل فيما يحويه، ويجب أن يكون مماساً للسطح الظاهر من المتمكن في جميع جهاته، وإلا لم يكن مالئا له، فهو السطح الباطن من الجسم الحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من المحوي. وعلى الثاني يكون المكان بعداً منقسماً في جميع الجهات مساوياً للبعد الذي في الجسم بحيث ينطبق أحدهما على الآخر سارياً فيه بكليته، فذلك البعد الذي هو المكان. أما أن يكون أمراً موهوماً يشغله الجسم ويملؤه على سبيل التوهم كما هو مذهب المتكلمين، وإما أن يكون أمراً موجوداً، ولا يجوز أن يكون بعداً مادياً قائماً بالجسم، إذ يلزم من حصول الجسم فيه تداخل الأجسام، فهو بعد مجرد. فلا مزيد للاحتمالات على الثلاثة. هذا ما عليه أهل العلم والتحقيق. وأما العامة فإنهم يطلقون لفظ المكان على ما يمنع الشيء من النزول، فيجعلون الأرض مكاناً للحيوان دون الهواء. قوله : ( فإنهم يطلقون ) قد نقلنا فيما سبق من الشفاء أن الأغلب عندهم إطلاق المكان على ما يكون فيه الشيء وإن لم يستقر فيه. قوله : ( على ما يمنع الشيء من النزول ) أي ما يستقر عليه الشيء، ويقع عليه اعتماده. والنزول غير السقوط فلا يرد أنه يلزم أن يكون الحبل الذي علق به الحجر من رأسه مكاناً له، وكذا القوة القسرية المصعدة للحجر وليس كذلك، على أن المقصود بيان الإطلاق لا التعريف الجامع المانع. والدرقة محركة ترس من الجلد ليس فيه خشب ولا عصب. قوله : على ما يمنع الشيء من النزول الأظهر أن يقول ما يعتمد عليه الشيء ويمنعه من النزول، إذ الاقتصار على الثاني يوهم أن يكون الحبل الذي علق به الحجر من رأسه مكاناً له عند العامة، وكذا القوة القسرية المصعدة للحجر، وليس كذلك. واعلم أن جعل المكان عبارة عما ذكر خطأ عامي، لأنه يوجب أن لا يكون السهم النافذ في الهواء والطائر فيما بين السماء والأرض، وكذا الحجر المتحرك بالقسر إلى جهة فوق في مكان إذ ليس لها في تلك الحالة موضع يمنعها من
And the dimension is either existing or hypothetical/imaginary, meaning disregarding proofs of existence. A fourth [possibility] for it and its clarification in a manner beyond which there is no more is to say: Since the body in its entirety is in its place filling it, it is not permissible for place to be something indivisible, because it is impossible for the divided in all its dimensions to be wholly present in it [the indivisible]; nor for it to be something divided in only one direction, like a line, because it is impossible for it to encompass the body entirely. So it is either divided in two directions or in all directions. On the first, place would be a surface (sath) dimensionally, because a part and its equivalent are impossible; and it is not permissible for it to be inherent in the located thing due to what has passed, but rather in that which contains it; and it must be contiguous to the outer surface of the located thing in all its directions, otherwise it would not be filling it. Thus it is the inner surface of the containing body contiguous to the outer surface of the contained. On the second, place is a dimension (bu'd) divided in all directions, equal to the dimension in the body such that one coincides with the other permeating it entirely. That dimension is place. It is either an imaginary thing which the body occupies and fills by way of imagination, as is the doctrine of the mutakallimūn, or it is an existing thing; and it is not permissible for it to be a material dimension subsisting in the body, because that would entail the interpenetration of bodies. Thus it is a non-material dimension. So there is no surplus beyond these three possibilities. This is the position of the people of knowledge and investigation. As for the common people, they apply the word 'place' to that which prevents a thing from falling, making the earth a place for animals but not the air. His statement: 'For they apply' – we have transmitted earlier from the Shifā' that the prevalent among them is to apply the word 'place' to that in which a thing is even if it does not settle. His statement: 'to that which prevents a thing from falling' – i.e., that upon which a thing settles and on which its support falls. And descent (nuzūl) is not falling (suqūṭ), so it does not follow that the rope by which a stone is suspended from its top is a place for it, nor likewise the coercive force that lifts a stone – it is not so, given that the purpose is to explain usage, not a comprehensive exclusive definition. 'And al-daraqa (with vowel movement) is a shield of leather without wood or sinew.' His statement: 'to that which prevents a thing from falling' – the more apparent is that he should say 'that upon which a thing relies and which prevents it from falling' because restricting to the second gives the impression that the rope by which a stone is suspended from its top would be a place for it in common usage, and likewise the coercive force that lifts a stone – it is not so. Know that making 'place' an expression for what has been mentioned is a common error, because it would entail that an arrow piercing through air and a bird between heaven and earth, and likewise a stone moved forcibly upwards, would not have a place, since in that state there is no location that prevents it from
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument to determine the nature of place. It begins by noting that the dimension (bu'd) which is one candidate for place can be either existing (mawjūd), hypothetical (mafrūḍ), or imaginary (mawhūm). Then it provides a detailed demonstration: a body fills its place entirely, so place cannot be indivisible (like a point) because a body with multiple dimensions cannot be wholly present in something indivisible; nor can it be one-dimensional (like a line) because it cannot encompass the body. Therefore, place must be either two-dimensional (a surface) or three-dimensional (a volume). If it is a surface, it is the inner surface of the containing body that is contiguous to the outer surface of the contained. If it is a volume, it is a dimension equal to the body's dimension, with which it coincides. This dimension can only be either imaginary (as the mutakallimūn hold) or existing but non-material, because a material dimension would require interpenetration of bodies. Hence only three possibilities exist. The passage then critiques the common (عامة) definition of place as 'that which prevents a thing from falling' (mā yamna‘u ash-shay’a min an-nuzūl). The commentator notes that this is merely a linguistic usage, not a precise definition. He illustrates its inadequacy: a stone suspended by a rope would have the rope as its 'place' under this definition (since the rope prevents it from falling), which is absurd. Similarly, a bird in flight or an arrow in motion would have no place. The correct definition is philosophical: place is either the inner surface of the container or the dimension occupied by the body. The passage concludes by emphasizing that the common usage is a popular error.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Place is whatever prevents a thing from falling.
Correction: This fails for moving objects and suspended objects; place is defined by containment and contiguity, not by preventing descent.

Checkpoint
According to the rational argument, why can place not be an indivisible point (non-divisible)?
A) Because a point cannot contain a body's dimensions
B) Because the body is divided in all directions and cannot be wholly present in something indivisible
C) Because the place would not be contiguous
D) Because the common people define place differently

Key Takeaway
Place is either the inner surface of the container or a non-material dimension that the body occupies, while the everyday notion of 'preventing falling' is an inadequate definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You heard that the common definition of place as 'that which prevents falling' is inadequate. In your own life, where do you settle for comfortable, surface-level definitions of important things—like faith, God, or the afterlife—without pushing deeper? What would it take for you to seek the 'inner surface' of truth instead of just the 'preventing falling' version?
?
The passage says that a point or a line cannot be a place because they cannot contain a body fully. Think about a relationship or a belief you hold: is it merely a point—a single, isolated claim—or does it truly encompass your whole being? If you discovered your core belief is like a point, would you be willing to expand it into a dimension that fills your entire life?
?
The scholar critiques the common error without condemning the people—he explains why they are mistaken. When you see others holding a flawed understanding, do you rush to correct them with frustration, or do you patiently dissect the error as this commentator does? Examine your heart: is your goal to be right or to guide others to the truth?
